Khiyar al-Ru'yah Becomes Void Upon Death of the Buyer
Ruling that khiyar al-ru'yah does not pass to heirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah after the buyer's death and explain why it is not inheritable.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'yah is a personal right that dies with the buyer—it does not pass to heirs.

This lesson explains a key principle in Hanafi sales law: the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) is a personal right tied to the buyer's knowledge. When the buyer dies, the option expires because it is not classified as inheritable property. Understanding this helps distinguish between different types of options in Islamic commercial law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) held by a buyer. The ruling: it becomes void (batil) upon the buyer's death.
The act here is the option itself, not the sale.
2
Locate the illa (reason)
Why does it become void? The text says 'because inheritance does not apply to it according to us.' The illa is that it is a personal right not subject to inheritance.
In Hanafi usul, khiyar al-ru'yah is considered a right that cannot be transferred to heirs.
3
Contrast with khiyar al-shart
The author references a discussion on khiyar al-shart (option of condition). In some cases, khiyar al-shart may be inheritable because it involves a contractual term that can pass to heirs.
This comparison highlights the unique nature of khiyar al-ru'yah.
4
Note cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow inheritance of khiyar al-ru'yah in certain circumstances, viewing it as a financial right.
The Hanafi position is based on the personal, subjective nature of the option.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a buyer dies after purchasing an item sight-unseen with khiyar al-ru'yah, the heirs cannot cancel the sale; the sale becomes final.
The option ends, and the buyer is considered to have accepted the purchase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مات وله خيار الرؤية بطل خياره لانه لا يجرى فيه الارث عندنا وقد ذكرنا في خيار الشرط
And whoever dies while having the option of sight, his option becomes void because inheritance does not apply to it according to us, and we have mentioned this in the option of condition.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This is a brief ruling that khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) terminates upon the death of the person holding it. The Hanafi school holds that this option is not inheritable, meaning it does not pass to the heirs of the deceased buyer. The reason is that the option is based on personal knowledge and satisfaction, which are extinguished by death. The author references the earlier discussion on khiyar al-shart for further detail. This contrasts with other types of options (like khiyar al-shart in some cases) that may be inheritable.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The buyer's option to cancel a purchase upon seeing the item, based on lack of prior knowledge.
إرث (irth): Inheritance; transfer of rights and property from deceased to heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to khiyar al-ru'yah when the buyer dies?
A) It passes to his heirs by inheritance.
B) It remains valid until exercised by his executor.
C) It becomes void automatically.
D) It lapses only if the seller also dies.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, khiyar al-ru'yah is a personal right that terminates upon the death of the buyer; it is not an inheritable property right.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah After a Delay: Change and Dispute
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah with time delay and change/dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for khiyar al-ru'yah after a delay and apply the Hanafi rules for resolving disputes about change.

Core Idea
Prior sight eliminates the option only if the item is unchanged; change or failure to recognize revives it.

When a buyer sees an item and purchases it later, the option of sight hinges on whether the item remains the same. The Hanafi school distinguishes between unchanged goods, which give no option, and changed goods, which preserve the buyer's right to cancel. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the dispute resolution rules, including the exception for long delays introduced by later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios after delay
The passage presents two main cases: the item remains unchanged, or it has changed. Determine which scenario applies to your case.
2
Apply the hukm for unchanged item
If the item is exactly as seen, the buyer has no option because knowledge of attributes was obtained from the prior sight.
Exception: if the buyer does not recognize the item as the one seen, the option exists.
3
Apply the hukm for changed item
If the item has changed, the buyer retains the option because the prior sight did not inform him of the current attributes.
4
Resolve dispute about change
Default rule: the seller's statement is taken because change is a new occurrence (istishab al-'adam). Exception: if a long time has passed, the buyer's statement is accepted because time likely caused change.
The exception is the view of later scholars (mutakhkhirin).
5
Compare with dispute about sight itself
If they dispute whether the buyer saw the item, the buyer's statement is taken because sight is a new event that he denies (istishab 'adam al-ru'yah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من رای شيئا لم اشتراه بعد مدة فان كان على الصفة التى راه فلاخيار له لان العلم باوصا فه حاصل له بالروية السابقة وبفواته يثبت الخيار الااذا كان لا يعلمه مرئية لعدم الرضاء به وان وجده متغير افله الخيار لان تلك الرؤية لم تقع معلمة باوصافه فكانه لم يره وان اختلفافى التغير فالقول قول البائع لان التغير حادث وسبب اللزوم ظاهر الا اذا بعدت المدة على ماقالو الان الظاهر شاهد للمشترى بخلاف ما اذا اختلفافى الرؤية لا نها امر حادث والمشترى ينكره فيكون القول قوله
And whoever sees something then buys it after a period: if it is in the condition he saw, then he has no option because knowledge of its attributes is obtained by the prior sight. If it is lost, the option is established, except if he does not know it is the seen item due to dissatisfaction with it. And if he finds it changed, he has the option because that prior sight did not inform him of its attributes, so it is as if he did not see it. If they differ about the change, the statement of the seller is taken because change is a new occurrence and the cause of obligation is apparent, except if the period is long, as they (later scholars) said, then the apparent state supports the buyer. This is unlike if they differ about the sight itself, because seeing is a new occurrence and the buyer denies it, so his statement is taken.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah when the buyer sees the merchandise and then purchases it after some delay. The Hanafi position is that if the item remains unchanged, the buyer has no option because he already knows its attributes from the prior sight. The option only arises if the buyer is unaware that this is the same item (i.e., he does not identify it) or if the item has changed since he saw it. In the latter case, the prior sight did not give him knowledge of the current attributes, so he retains the option. If the seller and buyer dispute whether the item changed, the default rule is that the seller's statement is accepted because the original contract is presumed valid (istishab al-'adam for change). However, the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) add an exception: if a long time has passed, the buyer's claim of change is accepted because the passage of time likely caused change. Regarding disputes about whether the buyer actually saw the item, the buyer's statement is accepted because seeing is a new event that he denies, and the default is that he did not see it (istishab 'adam al-ru'yah).

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): Buyer's option to cancel upon seeing the item if it does not match his expectation.
تغير (taghayyur): Change in the item's condition from the time of sighting to purchase.
القول قول البائع (al-qawl qawl al-ba'i'): The seller's statement is given weight in cases of dispute about change, due to presumptive continuity of the original condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer sees a cloth and buys it six months later, claiming it has changed. The seller denies any change. According to the later Hanafi scholars, whose statement is accepted?
A) The buyer's, because a long period has passed.
B) The seller's, because change is a new occurrence.
C) The buyer's, because he has the option anyway.
D) The seller's, because the contract is presumed valid.

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-ru'yah after a delay, the buyer retains the option if the item has changed or if he does not recognize it; for disputes about change, the seller's word prevails unless the delay is long.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah: Effect of Prior Sight of the Purchased Item
Explains khiyar al-ru'yah when buyer had prior sight of the item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which khiyar al-ru'yah is negated or established when the buyer had prior sight of the item, and apply the Hanafi rule regarding disputes about change and sight.

Core Idea
Prior sight that provides knowledge of attributes negates khiyar al-ru'yah; if the item changed, the option revives.

Khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) is a buyer's right to rescind a contract upon first seeing the purchased item. But what if the buyer had already seen the item before the contract? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the prior sight provided actual knowledge of the item's attributes. This lesson unpacks the scenarios of unchanged and changed items, and how disputes about change or prior sight are resolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central legal question
The passage addresses the effect of a prior sighting of the item on the buyer's khiyar al-ru'yah. The key variable is whether the item is found unchanged, changed, or whether there is a dispute about change or sight.
2
Analyze the rule for unchanged item
If the buyer previously saw the item and it remains unchanged, the prior sight gave him knowledge of its attributes. Therefore, he has no khiyar at the time of purchase, because he already knew what he was buying.
Exception: if the buyer does not recognize it as the same item, he retains khiyar due to lack of satisfaction.
3
Analyze the rule for changed item
If the item has changed since the prior sight, the buyer has khiyar because the prior sight did not inform him of the current attributes. It is as if he never saw it.
4
Resolve disputes about change vs. prior sight
The passage sets rules for two disputes: (1) If they differ on whether the item changed, the seller's statement is taken (because change is a new event, default is binding). (2) If they differ on whether prior sight occurred, the buyer's statement is taken (because sight is a new event and buyer denies it).
Exception: for a long period, the apparent evidence supports the buyer in the change dispute.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi difference
Early Hanafi authorities held the seller's word in change disputes regardless of time. Later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirin) said if a long period has passed, the buyer's word is accepted because external evidence favors the buyer.
The mufta-bihi (operative opinion) is the later view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من رای شیئا لم یشتره بعد مدة فان کان علی الصفة التی راه فلا خیار له لان العلم باوصافه حاصل له بالرویة السابقة وبفواته یثبت الخیار الا اذا کان لا یعلمه مرئیة لعدم الرضاء به وان وجده متغیرا فله الخیار لان تلک الرویة لم تقع معلمة باوصافه فکان لم یره وان اختلفا فی التغیر فالقول قول البائع لان التغیر حادث وسبب اللزوم ظاهر الا اذا بعدت المدة علی ماقالوا فان الظاهر شاهد للمشتری بخلاف ما اذا اختلفا فی الرؤیة لانها امر حادث والمشتری ینکره فیکون القول قوله.
And whoever saw something and did not buy it until after a period, if it was upon the description that he saw it, then there is no option for him, because knowledge of its attributes is obtained by him through the previous sight, and by its being lost the option is established, except when he does not know it as the seen item due to lack of satisfaction with it. And if he finds it changed, then he has the option, because that sight did not inform him of its attributes, so it is as if he did not see it. And if they differ regarding the change, the statement is the statement of the seller, because change is a new occurrence and the cause of binding is apparent, except if the period has lengthened according to what they said, for the apparent evidence is a witness for the buyer, contrary to when they differ regarding the sight, because it is a new occurrence and the buyer denies it, so his statement is accepted.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) when the buyer had previously seen the item. The author first states the general rule: if the item remains unchanged from the prior sighting, the buyer has no khiyar, because he already knows its attributes from that sight. However, if the buyer does not recognize it as the same item he saw (e.g., he forgot), then he retains the khiyar because his consent was not established. Next, if the item has changed, the buyer has khiyar because the prior sight did not provide knowledge of the current attributes; it is as if he never saw it. The passage then addresses a dispute between buyer and seller about whether the item changed: the seller's word is taken (with oath) because change is a new event and the default is that the sale is binding. However, if a long time has passed (as stated by later Hanafi scholars), then the apparent circumstance supports the buyer, so his word is accepted. Finally, if they dispute about whether the buyer had actually seen the item prior, the buyer's word is taken because sighting is a new event and the buyer denies it. This ruling is based on the rational principle that khiyar al-ru'yah is meant to provide knowledge; if knowledge exists, no khiyar.

Key Terms
خیار الرویة (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option to rescind a sale after seeing the purchased item for the first time, if the item does not match the description or expectations.
رویة سابقة (ru'yah sabiqah): A prior sighting of the item before the purchase contract.
تغیر (taghayyur): Change in the item's condition or attributes between prior sight and contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer sees a cloth, then buys it after one week, and the cloth is exactly the same, does he have khiyar al-ru'yah?
A) Yes, because he saw it before buying.
B) No, because he already knows its attributes from the prior sight.
C) Yes, if he forgot that he saw it.
D) No, because one week is too long.

Key Takeaway
Prior sight that gives full knowledge of the item's attributes negates khiyar al-ru'yah; if the item changes, the option returns, and disputes about change are resolved based on the length of the period.

Selling Part of Unseen Goods and Its Effect on Khiyar al-Ru'yah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar al-ru'yah after partial disposal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on khiyar al-ru'yah when part of the goods is sold and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Sarakhsi regarding its return after faskh.

Core Idea
Selling part of an unseen purchase forfeits the entire khiyar al-ru'yah, and once lost, it does not return — even if the part comes back.

This passage examines a practical scenario: a buyer purchases a bundle of cloth without seeing it, then sells a piece. Does he retain the right to return the rest for non-inspection? The answer hinges on the nature of khiyar al-ru'yah and a key analogy with khiyar al-shart. We will unravel the reasoning and the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether khiyar can be regained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
A buyer buys a bundle of cloth unseen, then sells or gifts a piece and delivers it. The default ruling is that he loses the right to return any of the bundle except for a defect.
This applies equally to khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-shart.
2
Understand why selling part forfeits khiyar
The sold part left his ownership, so returning the rest would split the transaction before it is fully binding (tafriq al-safqah). Khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-shart prevent completion, so partial disposal destroys the option.
Contrast with khiyar al-'ayb, where the sale is complete after possession.
3
Note the exception when the part returns via faskh
Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi holds that if the sold part returns through a rescission (faskh), the original buyer's khiyar al-ru'yah revives.
Faskh includes cancellation due to defect or mutual agreement.
4
Recognize Abu Yusuf's counter-analogy
Abu Yusuf compares khiyar al-ru'yah to khiyar al-shart: once the option is lost by partial disposal, it does not return even if the part comes back.
He argues that the cause of loss (tafriq) is complete and cannot be undone.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
Al-Quduri relied on Abu Yusuf's view. Thus, the operative ruling is that khiyar al-ru'yah does not return after being lost, even if the disposed part returns via faskh.
This is the preferred view in the school for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن اشترى عدل زطى ولم يره فباع منه ثوبا او وهبه وسلمه لم يرد شيئا منها الا من عيب وكذلك خيار الشرط لانه تعذر الرد فيما خرج عن ملكه وفي ردها بقى تفريق الصفقه قبل التمام لان خيار الرؤيه والشرط يمنعان تمامها بخلاف خيار العيب لان الصفقة تتم مع خيار العيب بعد القبض وان كانت لا تتم قبله وفيه وضع المسألة فلو عاد اليه بسبب هو فسخ فهو على خيار الرؤية كذا ذكره شمس الائمة السرخسي وعن ابى يوسف انه لا يعود بعد سقوطه كخيار الشرط وعليه اعتمد القدوري.
He said: And whoever buys a bundle of Zatti cloths and did not see it, then sells from it a cloth or gifts it and delivers it, he cannot return anything from it except due to a defect, and likewise for khiyar al-shart. Because returning what left his ownership is impossible, and in returning the rest there is a splitting of the transaction before completion. Because khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-shart prevent its completion, unlike khiyar al-'ayb, because the transaction is completed with khiyar al-'ayb after possession, even if it is not completed before it. And in it is the placement of the issue: if it returns to him due to a cause that is annulment, then he retains khiyar al-ru'yah. Such is mentioned by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. And from Abu Yusuf that it does not return after it has fallen, like khiyar al-shart, and upon this al-Quduri relied.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of disposing of part of an unseen purchase on khiyar al-ru'yah. The ruling: if someone buys a bundle of cloth without seeing it, and then sells or gifts one cloth from the bundle with delivery, he forfeits his right to return any of it except for a defect. This is analogous to khiyar al-shart (option of condition). The reason: returning the sold or gifted part is impossible since it left his ownership, and returning the rest would constitute a splitting of the transaction (tafriq al-safqah) before completion, which is impermissible because khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-shart prevent the sale from being fully binding. The passage contrasts this with khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect), where the sale is considered complete after possession, so selling part does not prevent returning the rest. The author then notes a nuance: if the sold or gifted cloth returns to the buyer through a cause that is a rescission (faskh), such as the buyer rescinding the sale of that cloth due to a defect, then the original buyer's khiyar al-ru'yah returns. This is the view of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. However, Abu Yusuf holds that once khiyar al-ru'yah is lost (by selling part), it does not return even if the part comes back, just as khiyar al-shart does not return. Al-Quduri relied on Abu Yusuf's view. The mufta bihi position is the latter.

Key Terms
عدل زطى (adl Zatti): A bundle of cloths from Zatt (a place in India known for textiles).
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting the transaction by returning part of the goods while keeping the rest, which is impermissible when khiyar exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, if a buyer sells part of an unseen bundle and then the sold part returns via a rescission (faskh), what happens to his khiyar al-ru'yah?
A) It returns because the cause of loss is removed.
B) It does not return, similar to khiyar al-shart.
C) He retains it for the unsold part only.
D) It is replaced by khiyar al-'ayb.

Key Takeaway
Selling any part of an unseen purchase eliminates the entire khiyar al-ru'yah, and according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, this right does not revive even if the sold part returns.

Evidentiary Rule in Disputes Over Change in Sold Item
Ruling on acceptance of statements in khiyar al-ru'yah disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which party's statement is accepted with oath in a dispute over change in a sold item, applying the Hanafi principles of mudda'i and munkir based on the apparent state (zahir hal).

Core Idea
The one whose claim aligns with the apparent state is the defendant; his statement with oath prevails.

In sales with inspection rights (khiyar al-ru'yah), disputes may arise when the buyer claims the item has changed after inspection. The Hanafi school provides clear rules based on the procedural distinction between claimant (mudda'i) and defendant (munkir). This lesson unpacks three scenarios from the Hidayah: short inspection period, long period, and dispute over whether inspection occurred at all.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default apparent state (zahir hal)
Determine what is considered the normal, default state of the item at the time of the dispute. In a recent sale, the default is no change; change is a deviation. After a long period, change becomes the default. In a dispute about seeing, the default is that the buyer did not see the item (since seeing is an act that needs proof).
The default state is key to classifying claimant and defendant.
2
Determine which party's claim aligns with the default
Compare each party's assertion to the default. The party whose claim matches the default is the munkir (defendant); the party whose claim contradicts is the mudda'i (claimant).
Only one party can align; the other is the claimant.
3
Classify parties as mudda'i and munkir
Label the buyer and seller accordingly: if the buyer claims change (against default in short period), he is mudda'i; if he denies change (after long period), he is munkir. Similarly for the seller.
The labels are not fixed; they shift with the scenario.
4
Apply the procedural rule of evidence
The general rule: if the claimant (mudda'i) has no evidence or witnesses, the defendant's (munkir's) statement with an oath is accepted. Apply this to the classified parties.
This rule is from the broader Hanafi evidence framework.
5
Consider temporal and factual variations
Note how the default state changes when time passes (long period) or when the nature of the dispute differs (e.g., whether the buyer saw the item versus whether the item changed). Reclassify and apply the rule accordingly.
This step ensures correct application across all sub-cases.

اگر بائع اور مشتری نے اختلاف کیا یعنی مشتری نے کہا مبیع متغیر ہو گئی۔ اور بائع نے کہا کہ متغیر نہیں ہوئی۔ تو قسم کے ساتھ بائع کا قول معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ مبیع کے اندر تغیر کا پیدا ہونا امر جدید عارض ہے اور خلاف ظاہر ہے اور بیع لازم ہونے کا سبب امرظاہر ہے پس مشتری کا قول ظاہر حال کے خلاف ہوا اور بائع کا قول ظاہر کے موافق ہوا اور جس کا قول ظاہر کے خلاف ہوتا ہے وہ مدعی کہلاتا ہے۔ اور جس کا قول ظاہر کے موافق ہو وہ مدعی علیہ اور منکر کہلاتا ہے پس مشتری مدعی اور بائع مدعی علیہ ہوا۔ اور شریعت کا ضابطہ ہے کہ مدعی کے پاس اگر بینہ اور گواہ نہ ہوں تو مدعی علیہ کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس مسئلہ میں مدعی علیہ یعنی بائع کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہو گا۔ ہاں اگر مدت رویت طویل ہوگئی تو مذکورہ اختلاف کی صورت میں مشتری کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ کیونکہ اب ظاہر حال مشتری کی موافقت کرتا ہے۔ یعنی ایک مدت دراز کے بعد مبیع کا متغیر ہو جانا ظاہر حال کے موافق ہے۔ اور جس کا قول ظاہر حال کے موافق ہو وہ مدعی علیہ ہوتا ہے اور گواہ نہ ہونے کی صورت میں مدعی علیہ کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں مشتری کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ اور اگر نفس رویت ہی میں اختلاف ہو گیا۔ بایں طور کہ مشتری کہتا ہے کہ میں نے مبیع نہیں دیکھی تھی اور بائع کہتا ہے کہ تو دیکھ چکا ہے تو اس صورت میں بائع کا قول قبول نہ ہوگا بلکہ مشتری کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مبیع کو دیکھنا ایک امر جدید عارض ہے اور بائع اس کا دعوی کرتا ہے اور مشتری اس کا منکر ہے تو بائع یعنی مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے منکر یعنی مشتری کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہو گا۔
If the seller and buyer dispute, i.e., the buyer says the sold item has changed, and the seller says it has not changed, then the seller's statement with an oath is accepted because the occurrence of change in the sold item is a new accidental matter contrary to the apparent [original state], and the cause for the sale becoming binding is an apparent matter. So the buyer's statement goes against the apparent state, and the seller's statement agrees with the apparent. The one whose statement goes against the apparent is called a claimant (mudda'i), and the one whose statement agrees with the apparent is called the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) and the denier (munkir). So the buyer is the claimant and the seller is the defendant. The rule of Shari'ah is that if the claimant has no evidence or witnesses, then the defendant's statement with an oath is accepted. Therefore in this case, the defendant's, i.e., the seller's, statement with an oath is accepted. However, if the period of inspection (ru'yat) has become long, then in the mentioned dispute the buyer's statement will be accepted because now the apparent state agrees with the buyer, i.e., after a long period, the change of the sold item is in accordance with the apparent state. And if there is a dispute about the actual seeing, i.e., the buyer says 'I did not see the sold item' and the seller says 'you have seen it,' then in this case the seller's statement is not accepted; rather, the buyer's statement is accepted because seeing the sold item is a new accidental matter, and the seller is claiming it while the buyer denies it. So the seller is the claimant without evidence, so the denier's (i.e., buyer's) statement with an oath is accepted.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage deals with evidentiary rules in khiyar al-ru'yah disputes. The author explains the general Hanafi principle: the party whose assertion aligns with the apparent (zahir) state is the defendant (munki), and their statement is taken with oath. In the first case, the default is that the sale is binding; change is an accidental event. So the seller's claim of no change aligns with the default, making him defendant; the buyer's claim of change is a claim against the default, making him claimant. Since the claimant lacks evidence, the defendant's statement prevails. If a long time has passed, the default shifts: items naturally change over time, so the buyer's claim of change now aligns with the apparent, making him defendant. In the sub-case where they dispute whether the buyer saw the item, seeing is a new event; the seller's claim of seeing is a new assertion, so he becomes claimant. The buyer's denial aligns with the apparent (no seeing), so he is defendant and his statement accepted.

Key Terms
مدعی (mudda'i): The party whose assertion contradicts the apparent state (zahir hal); must provide evidence or their claim is rejected with oath of opponent.
مدعی علیہ (mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant whose assertion aligns with the apparent state; if claimant lacks evidence, their statement with oath is accepted.
ظاہر حال (zahir hal): The apparent, default state of affairs considered legally normal unless evidence shows otherwise. Used to determine claimant and defendant.
منکر (munkir): One who denies a claim; synonymous with defendant when their statement aligns with the apparent state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer claims the sold item changed two years after the sale, and the seller denies, whose statement with oath is accepted?
A) The seller's, because he is the original owner.
B) The buyer's, because after a long period change is expected.
C) Both statements are equal; a judge decides.
D) The buyer's, because he has the right of inspection.

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of a party's statement in a dispute over change in a sold item depends on whether their claim aligns with the apparent state, which shifts with time and the nature of the claim.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Purchase of Unseen Bundle: Effect of Partial Sale on Option of Inspection and Its Return
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah with partial disposal and intra-Hanafi dispute on its return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah when a buyer disposes of part of the mabi', and evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether the option returns after a rescissory cause.

Core Idea
Selling part of an unseen bundle cancels the option of inspection; whether it returns divides the Hanafi school.

You have purchased a bundle of cloth without seeing it. Later, you sell one piece. Does your right to return the bundle disappear? The Hanafi school says yes, due to the prohibition of splitting an incomplete transaction (tafriq al-safqah qabl al-tamam). But what if that piece later comes back to you through a rescissory cause (like return due to defect)? This lesson explores the core rule and the scholarly disagreement within the Hanafi school, focusing on the principle 'al-saqit la ya'ud' (the fallen does not return).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
Read the source passage carefully. The buyer buys a bundle without seeing it, takes possession, then sells or gifts one cloth. The initial ruling: the buyer loses the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) for the entire bundle. Note that the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) remains.
The sale or gift with possession must be complete.
2
Understand the reason: tafriq al-safqah qabl al-tamam
The buyer cannot return the remaining cloths because returning only part would split the transaction before it is complete. Khiyar al-ru'yah prevents the transaction from being complete, so any return would be a split before completion, which is impermissible. Distinguish this from khiyar al-'ayb, which does not prevent completion after possession.
After possession, khiyar al-'ayb does not block completion, so returning the rest is splitting after completion, which is permissible.
3
Analyze the sub-issue: return of the disposed item
Suppose the sold or gifted cloth returns to the buyer via a rescissory cause (e.g., the second buyer returns it due to a defect, or the gift is revoked). Does the option of inspection revive? The source presents two views: the majority (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, al-Sarakhsi) say yes, because the obstacle (disposal) is removed. Abu Yusuf and al-Quduri say no, based on 'al-saqit la ya'ud'.
The disputing opinion is attributed to Abu Yusuf, and al-Quduri relied on it.
4
Evaluate the mufta bihi position
According to the text, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Yusuf: once the option falls, it does not return, even if the item comes back. The practical consequence: if the sold cloth returns, the buyer cannot use khiyar al-ru'yah to return the rest.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference; other schools may differ on khiyar al-ru'yah itself.

A bundle of cloth bought without seeing, from which he sold one cloth or gifted it and handed it over, then he will not return [the entire purchase] due to the option of inspection, except due to a defect. And similarly, the option of condition. Because returning what has left his ownership is impossible, and returning the rest involves splitting the transaction before completion, because the option of inspection and the option of condition prevent its completion, unlike the option of defect, because the transaction is completed with the option of defect after possession, although it is not completed before possession. And in this [matter] lies the scenario: if [the sold item] returns to him due to a cause that is a rescission, then he retains the option of inspection. Thus mentioned Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. And from Abu Yusuf: it does not return after falling, like the option of condition, and al-Quduri relied on this.
Translation: In al-Jami' al-Saghir it is said: Whoever buys a bundle of Zutti cloths without seeing, and sells one cloth from it or gifts it and hands it over, he cannot return anything from it except due to a defect. And similarly for the option of condition, because the cloth that left his ownership is impossible to return, and returning the rest entails splitting the transaction before completion, because the option of inspection and the option of condition prevent its completion, unlike the option of defect, because the transaction is completed with the option of defect after possession, although it is not completed before possession. And the scenario is [considered] in this [context]. So if the cloth that was sold or gifted returns to him due to a cause that is a rescission, then the buyer retains the option of inspection. Thus mentioned Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that the option of inspection does not return after falling, like the option of condition does not return, and Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri relied on this.
Explanation: The scenario is that if someone bought a bundle of Zutti cloths without seeing them, and took possession, then sold one cloth from the bundle or gifted it and handed it over to the donee, then the buyer cannot return anything from it due to the option of inspection, meaning his option of inspection is lost. However, if a defect appears in the remaining cloths, he has the option to return due to the defect. The same ruling applies to the option of condition. The reason is that the cloth that left the buyer's ownership due to sale or gift with possession has become related to the right of another (the second buyer or donee), so returning it has become impossible. And returning the rest entails splitting the transaction before completion, which is impermissible. Splitting before completion occurs because the option of inspection and option of condition prevent the transaction from being completed; as long as either exists, the transaction is not complete. As for the option of defect, it does not prevent completion after possession, although it does prevent completion before possession. In this case, the buyer had taken possession, so the transaction is complete despite the option of defect, and returning the rest would be splitting after completion, which is permissible.
The author of al-Hidayah says: If the cloth that the buyer sold or gifted returns to him via a cause that is a rescission – e.g., the second buyer returned it due to a defect after a judge's ruling, or the donee revoked the gift – then the buyer (or donor) retains his option of inspection; he can return all the cloths if he wishes, because the obstacle (his prior disposal) has been removed. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentions this. From Imam Abu Yusuf it is narrated that once the option of inspection is lost, it does not return, because the rule is 'the fallen does not return' (al-saqit la ya'ud), just as the option of condition does not return once lost. Imam al-Quduri's relied-upon position is this.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage addresses the khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) when a buyer purchases a bundle of cloth without seeing it, and then disposes of part of it by sale or gift. The ruling: such disposal cancels the option for the entire purchase, because returning the whole would require splitting the transaction before completion (tafriq al-safqah qabl al-tamam), which is impermissible. The option of inspection and option of condition prevent the transaction from being complete; while the option of defect does not prevent completion after possession. Therefore, after disposal, the option of inspection is lost, but the option of defect remains for the remaining items. A sub-issue: if the disposed item returns to the buyer via a rescissory cause (like return due to defect or revocation of gift), the option of inspection is restored according to the majority (including al-Sarakhsi), because the obstacle (the disposal) is removed. However, according to Abu Yusuf and al-Quduri, once the option falls, it does not return, even if the item comes back, based on the principle 'al-saqit la ya'ud' (the fallen does not return). This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and the mufta bihi position, according to the text, is that of Abu Yusuf (relied upon by al-Quduri).

Key Terms
خیار الرؤیة (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection: the buyer's right to return the item if he had not seen it at the time of contract and is dissatisfied upon seeing it.
خیار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): Option of condition: a right to rescind within a specified period, agreed upon by parties in the contract.
خیار العیب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option of defect: the buyer's right to return the item due to a latent defect existing at the time of sale.
تفریق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting the transaction: returning part of a multi-item sale while keeping the rest. It is impermissible before the transaction is complete (qabl al-tamam) but permissible after completion.
الساقط لا يعود (al-saqit la ya'ud): A legal maxim meaning 'the fallen does not return'. Once a right is extinguished, it cannot be revived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the text (relied upon by al-Quduri), what happens to khiyar al-ru'yah if the buyer sells part of the mabi' and that part later returns via a rescissory cause?
A) The option returns because the obstacle is removed.
B) The option does not return because it was permanently lost.
C) The option returns only if the rescission was due to a defect.
D) The option remains lost for the sold part but revives for the rest.

Key Takeaway
Partial disposal of the mabi' cancels the option of inspection for the entire contract, and whether it returns is a matter of intra-Hanafi disagreement; the relied-on view is that it does not return.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Effect of Selling Part of Bundle on Khiyar al-Ru'ya and Khiyar al-Shart
Explaining the ruling when a buyer sells part of goods purchased with khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer with khiyar al-ru'ya or khiyar al-shart disposes of part of the goods, distinguish khiyar al-'ayb from these options in this context, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding revival of khiyar.

Core Idea
Disposing part of khiyar goods cancels that option; khiyar al-'ayb remains; revival of khiyar after return is disputed.

When a buyer purchases a bundle of cloth with the right to return it after seeing it (khiyar al-ru'ya) or within a specified period (khiyar al-shart), what happens if he sells or gifts one piece? This lesson examines the legal consequences, focusing on the cancellation of khiyar for the disposed piece, the survival of khiyar al-'ayb, and an intra-Hanafi debate about whether the khiyar can revive if the piece returns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the buyer's disposition
The buyer purchases a bundle with khiyar al-ru'ya or khiyar al-shart, takes possession, then sells or gifts one piece and delivers it. Determine the effect on his khiyar options.
The disposition is with possession given to the second party.
2
Apply the rule of tafriq al-safqah
Understand that returning the entire bundle after selling part would split the transaction before completion (tafriq qabl al-tamam), which is invalid. Hence khiyar al-ru'ya/shart is cancelled for the sold piece.
After possession, the transaction is considered complete for khiyar al-'ayb, so splitting after completion is permissible.
3
Distinguish between khiyar types
Note that khiyar al-'ayb (defect option) survives the disposition because the transaction is complete after possession, allowing return of remaining pieces if a defect appears.
Khiyar al-'ayb does not prevent completion after possession, unlike khiyar al-ru'ya/shart.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on revival
When the sold piece returns to the buyer by a rescissory cause (e.g., defect rescission or gift revocation), does the original khiyar revive? Abu Hanifa (via Sarakhsi) says yes; Abu Yusuf and al-Quduri say no, citing the maxim 'al-saqit la ya'ud'.
The mufta bihi position follows Abu Yusuf and al-Quduri that khiyar does not revive.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the piece returns, according to the reviving view, the buyer can still return the entire bundle due to khiyar. According to the non-reviving view, he cannot.
This affects the buyer's ability to rescind the entire contract.

Source Text

كتاب البيوع
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد هشتم
كر لیا ۔ پھر اس گٹھری میں سے ایک تھان کسی کو فروخت کر دیا یا ہبہ کیا اور موہوب لہ کو قبضہ دے دیا تو مشتری خیار رویت کی وجہ سے اس میں سے کچھ واپس نہیں کر سکتا یعنی اس کا خیار رویت ساقط ہو گیا ہے۔ ہاں اگر باقی تھانوں میں سے کوئی عیب ظاہر ہوا تو خیار عیب کی وجہ واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہے۔ یہی حکم خیار شرط کا ہے یعنی اگر گٹھری خیار شرط کے ساتھ خرید کر اس پر قبضہ کر لیا پھر اسمیں سے ایک تھان فروخت کیا یا ہبہ کر کے موہوب لا کو سپرد کر دیا تو اب خیار شرط کی وجہ سے باقی تھان واپس کرنے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا ۔ ہاں خیار عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کر سکتا ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ کپڑے کا جو تھان مشتری کی ملک سے فروخت کرنے یا ہبہ مع القبضہ کرنے کی وجہ سے نکل گیا ہے۔ چونکہ اس کے ساتھ غیر یعنی مشتری ثانی یا موہوب لہ کا حق متعلق ہو گیا ہے اسلئے اس کا واپس کرنا تو معذور ہو گیا۔ یعنی ایک تھان میں خیار رویت ساقط ہو گیا ہے۔ اور باقی تھانوں کو واپس کرنے میں تفریق صفقہ قبل التمام لازم آتا ہے۔ حالانکہ تفریق صفقہ قبل التمام شرعا نا جائز ہے۔ اور تفریق صفقہ قبل اتمام اس لئے لازم آتا ہے کہ خیار رویت اور خیار شرط صفقہ پورا ہونے سے مانع ہیں۔ یعنی ان دونوں میں سے کسی ایک کے ہوتے ہوئے صفقہ پورا نہیں ہوتا اور رہا خیار عیب تو وہ قبضہ کے بعد صفقہ پورا ہونے سے مانع نہیں ہوتا۔ اگر چہ قبضہ سے پہلے صفقہ پورا نہیں ہوتا۔ یعنی خیار عیب کی صورت میں اگر مشتری نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا ہے تو صفقہ پورا ہو جاتا ہے اور اگر قبضہ نہیں کیا تو صفقہ پورا نہیں ہوتا۔ یہاں مسئلہ اسی صورت میں فرض کیا گیا ہے جبکہ مشتری نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا ہو۔ پس جب مشتری نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا تو خیار عیب کے باوجود صفقہ پورا ہو گیا اور جب صفقہ پورا ہو گیا تو فروخت کئے ہوئے تھان یا ہبہ مع القب کیے ہوئے تھان کے علاوہ باقی تھانوں کو واپس کرنے سے تفریق صفقہ تو لازم آئے گا۔ مگر تفریق صفقہ بعد التمام لازم آئے گا نہ کہ قبل التمام اور تفریق صفقہ بعد التمام جائز ہے۔ اسلئے خیار عیب کی وجہ سے باقی تھان واپس کرنے کی اجازت دی گئی ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ وہ تھان جس کو مشتری نے فروخت کیا تھا یا ہبہ کر کے موہوب لا کے سپرد کیا تھا اگر وہ تھان مشتری کے پاس ایسے سبب سے لوٹ آیا جو مسخ ہے۔ مثلا مشتری ثانی نے عیب کی وجہ قضاء قاضی کے ذریعہ اس تھان کو مشتری اول کو واپس کر دیا اور جبہ کی صورت میں اس نے ہبہ واپس لے لیا تو یہ مشتری اول یا واہب اپنے خیار رویت پر رہے گا۔ یعنی تمام تھانوں کو اگر خیار رؤیت کے تحت واپس کرنا چاہے تو واپس کر سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ واپس کرنے سے جو چیز مانع تھی یعنی مشتری کا تصرف وہ زائل ہو گیا۔ شمس الائمہ سری نے یہی ذکر کیا ہے۔ حضرت امام ابو یوسف سے روایت ہے کہ ایک بار خیار رویت ساقط ہونے کے بعد دوبارہ عود نہیں کرے گا۔ کیونکہ قاعدہ ہے الساقط لا يعود جیسے خیار شرط ساقط ہونے کے بعد عود نہیں کرتا۔ امام قدوری کا معتمد علیہ مذہب بھی یہی ہے۔ جمیل احمد عفی عنہ
He took possession. Then he sold one piece from this bundle or gifted it and gave possession to the donee, then the buyer cannot return anything from it due to khiyar al-ru'ya, meaning his khiyar al-ru'ya is cancelled. Yes, if a defect appears in the remaining pieces, he has the option to return due to khiyar al-'ayb. The same ruling applies to khiyar al-shart: if he bought the bundle with khiyar al-shart and took possession, then sold one piece or gifted it and handed it over to the donee, then due to khiyar al-shart he will not be permitted to return the remaining pieces. Yes, due to khiyar al-'ayb he can return. The proof is that the piece of cloth that left the buyer's ownership due to sale or gift with possession, since the right of another (the second buyer or donee) is attached to it, returning it is prevented. That is, the khiyar al-ru'ya is cancelled in that one piece. In returning the remaining pieces, tafriq al-safqah qabl al-tamam (splitting the transaction before completion) becomes necessary, whereas tafriq al-safqah qabl al-tamam is not permissible in Shari'ah. Tafriq al-safqah before completion is necessary because khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart prevent completion of the transaction. That is, with either of them present, the transaction is not complete. As for khiyar al-'ayb, it does not prevent completion after possession, although before possession it does prevent completion. That is, in case of khiyar al-'ayb, if the buyer has taken possession, the transaction is complete; if he has not taken possession, it is not complete. Here the issue is assumed in the case where the buyer has taken possession. So when the buyer took possession, despite the khiyar al-'ayb, the transaction became complete. And when the transaction became complete, returning the remaining pieces other than the sold or gifted piece would necessitate tafriq al-safqah, but it would be tafriq al-safqah after completion, not before completion, and tafriq al-safqah after completion is permissible. Therefore, permission was given to return the remaining pieces due to khiyar al-'ayb.
The author of al-Hidayah says: That piece which the buyer had sold or gifted and handed over to the donee, if that piece returns to the buyer by a cause that annuls the transaction, e.g., the second buyer returned it to the first buyer through a judge's ruling due to a defect, or in the case of gift he revoked the gift, then this first buyer (or donor) will retain his khiyar al-ru'ya. That is, if he wants to return all the pieces under khiyar al-ru'ya, he can return, because the thing that was preventing return (i.e., the buyer's disposition) has been removed. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned this. It is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf that once khiyar al-ru'ya is cancelled, it does not return again, because the principle is 'al-saqit la ya'ud' (that which has fallen does not return), just as khiyar al-shart does not return after being cancelled. The relied-upon madhhab of Imam al-Quduri is also this. Jamil Ahmad, may Allah forgive him.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the effect of a buyer's disposition on his khiyar options when he purchases a bundle of cloth with khiyar al-ru'ya or khiyar al-shart. The scenario: a buyer takes possession of a bundle, then sells or gifts one piece. The ruling is that his khiyar al-ru'ya (or khiyar al-shart) is cancelled for that piece because the piece has left his ownership and another party's right is attached. He cannot return the entire bundle due to khiyar al-ru'ya because it would require splitting the transaction (tafriq al-safqah) before it is complete, which is invalid. However, if a defect appears in the remaining pieces, he can return them due to khiyar al-'ayb, because once possession is taken, the transaction is complete, and splitting after completion is permissible. The passage then discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the sold or gifted piece returns to the buyer by a cause such as judicial rescission due to defect or revocation of gift, does the original khiyar al-ru'ya revive? The author of al-Hidayah (presumably following Abu Hanifa) states that it does revive, citing Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds that once khiyar al-ru'ya is cancelled, it does not return, relying on the principle 'al-saqit la ya'ud'. Imam al-Quduri's relied-upon position is also that it does not return. This illustrates the application of the legal maxim in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
خیار رؤیت (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option to rescind a contract after seeing the goods if they were unseen at the time of contract.
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): The option to rescind the contract within an agreed period stipulated in the contract.
تفریق صفقہ (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting a single sale transaction into separate parts.
الساقط لا يعود (al-saqit la ya'ud): Legal maxim: that which has fallen (i.e., a right that has been dropped) does not return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the bundle case, when a buyer with khiyar al-shart sells one piece and delivers it, what happens to his khiyar for the remaining pieces?
A) He can still return the remaining pieces due to khiyar al-shart.
B) He can return the remaining pieces due to khiyar al-'ayb if a defect appears.
C) He loses all options for the remaining pieces.
D) He can deduct the price of the sold piece from the return.

Key Takeaway
Disposing of part of khiyar goods cancels that option for the disposed part and prevents return of the rest via that option, but khiyar al-'ayb survives after possession; the revival of khiyar upon return of the piece is disputed, with the mufta bihi holding it does not revive.

Khiyar al-'Ayb: Option Due to Defect
Beginning of the chapter on Option of Defect from Al-Hidayah with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-'ayb, identify the condition for its application, and explain the rationale based on the hadith proof.

Core Idea
When a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect, he may return the item or keep it for full price — no partial refund allowed.

This lesson introduces Khiyar al-'Ayb, the buyer's option due to a defect. We will examine the ruling, its proof, and the reasoning behind the Hanafi position that the buyer cannot keep the item and claim a reduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is khiyar al-'ayb? It is the buyer's right to return a defective item. The ruling: the buyer may either keep it for the full price or return it.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith where the Prophet (s) bought a slave and wrote a guarantee that it was free from defects. This shows the contract implies soundness.
The hadith is cited in the text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith establishes that the buyer expects a sound item. When soundness is missing, the buyer has a choice to protect himself from harm.
4
Note the conditions
The defect must have existed with the seller at the time of sale, and the buyer must not have seen it at contract or delivery.
If the buyer saw the defect, he is deemed to have consented.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a defect, the buyer can return the item for full refund. He cannot keep it and demand a partial price reduction.
This is because attributes have no separate price in the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار العيب
تشریح
خیار رویت اور خیار شرط مانع تمام صفقہ ہونے کی وجہ سے چونکہ اقوی ہیں اور خیار عیب تمام صفقہ کے بعد مانع لز دوم ہونے کی وجہ سے اضعف ہے۔ اور اقومی غیر قومی سے مقدم ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے خیار شرط اور خیار رویت کا ذکر پہلے کیا گیا اور خیار عیب کا ذکر بعد میں کیا گیا۔ خیار عیب میں خیار کی اضافت ، عیب کی طرف اضافت شی الی السبب کے قبیلہ سے ہے۔ کیونکہ خیار عیب میں عیب ہی خیار حاصل ہونے کا سبب ہے۔ عیب وہ کہلائے گا جس سے شی کی اصل فطرت سلیمہ خالی ہو اور اسکی وجہ سے وہ شی ، ناقص شمار کی جانے لگے۔ مشتری بیچ پر مطلع ہوا تو پوری مبیع لے لے یا پوری کو رد کر دے
و اذا اطلع المشترى على عيب فى المبيع فهو بالخيار ان شاء اخذه بجميع الثمن وان شاء رده لان مطلق العقد يقضى وصف السلامة فعند فواته يتخير كيلا يتضرر بلزوم مالا يرضی به وليس له ان يمسكه وياخذ النقصان لان الاوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن في مجرد العقد ولانه لم يرض بزواله عن ملكه باقل من المسمى فيتضرر به ودفع الضرر عن المشترى ممكن بالرد بدون تضرره والمرادبه عيب كان عند البائع ولم يره المشترى عند البيع ولا عند القبض لان ذالک رضاء به
ترجمہ
اگر مشتری مبیع میں کسی عیب پر مطلع ہوا تو اس کو اختیار ہے چاہے مبیع کو پورے شمن میں لے لے اور چاہے اس کو واپس کر دے۔ اسلئے کہ مطلق عقد بیع کے صحیح سالم ہونے کا تقاضہ کرتا ہے۔ پس وصف سلامت کے فوت ہونے کے وقت مشتری کو خیار حاصل ہوگا تا کہ مشتری اس چیز کے لازم آنے سے ضرر نہ اٹھائے جس پر وہ راضی نہیںہے اور مشتری کے لیے یہ جائز نہیں ہے کہ مبیع کو رو کرے۔ اور ( بائع سے ) بقدر نقصان لے لے کیونکہ مجر عقد میں اوصاف کے مقابل ثمن میں سے کچھ نہیں ہوتا ہے اور اس لئے کہ بائع مبیع کو مقررہ شمن سے کم کے عوض اپنی ملک سے نکالنے پر راضی نہیں ہوا ( پس ایسا کرنے میں ) اس کو ضرر پہنچے گا اور بغیر بائع کے ضرر اٹھائے مشتری کا ضرر دور کرنا مبیع واپس کر کے ممکن ہے۔ اور عیب سے مراد وہ عیب ہے جو بائع کے پاس ہو اور مشتری نے عقد بیع کے وقت یا قبضہ کے وقت اس کو نہ دیکھا ہو۔ اس لیے کہ عیب کو دیکھ لینا اس پر رضا مندی ہے۔
تشریح
صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مشتری مبیع کے اندر کسی عیب پر مطلع ہوا تو مشتری کو اختیار ہے جی چاہے تو اسکو پورے شمن کے عوض لے لے اور جی چاہے تو مبیع کو واپس کر دے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مطلق عقد معقود علیہ اور بیع کے عیوب سے صحیح سالم ہونے کا تقاضہ کرتا ہے یعنی مطلق عقد کا تقاضہ یہ ہے کہ بیع عیوب سے سالم ہو جیسا کہ حدیث رسول اس کی شاہد ہے چنانچہ ارشاد ہے
ان رسول الله صلى الله عليه و سلم اشترى من عداء بن خالد بن هوذة عبدا وكتب في عهدته هذا ما اشترى محمد رسول الله من العداء بن خالد بن هوذة عبدا لا داء ولا غائلة ولا خبثة بيع المسلم من المسلم
Chapter of Option of Defect
Explanation: Since khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart are strong (aqwa) because they prevent completion of the transaction, and khiyar al-'ayb is weak (ad'af) because it prevents only bindingness after completion, and the strong takes precedence over the weak, therefore the mention of khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya was made first and the mention of khiyar al-'ayb later. In khiyar al-'ayb, the attribution of khiyar to 'ayb is of the type of attributing a thing to its cause, because in khiyar al-'ayb, the defect itself is the cause of the khiyar coming into existence. A defect is defined as that from which the original sound nature of a thing is missing, and due to which that thing is considered deficient. The buyer, if he becomes aware of a defect, takes the entire item or rejects it entirely.
And when the buyer discovers a defect in the sold item, he has the option: if he wishes, he takes it for the full price; and if he wishes, he returns it. Because the absolute contract requires the attribute of soundness. So when it is lost, he has a choice so that he is not harmed by being bound to what he is not pleased with. And it is not for him to keep it and take the compensation for deficiency, because in the mere contract, attributes do not correspond to any part of the price, and because the seller did not consent to its leaving his ownership for less than the named price, so he would be harmed, and removing harm from the buyer is possible by returning without harming him. And what is meant by defect is one that existed with the seller and the buyer did not see at the time of sale nor at the time of possession, because seeing it is consent to it.
Translation: If the buyer discovers a defect in the sold item, he has the option: if he wishes, he takes the item for the full price, and if he wishes, he returns it. Because the absolute contract of sale requires soundness. So when the attribute of soundness is lost, the buyer gets an option so that he does not suffer harm from being bound to something he is not pleased with. And it is not permissible for the buyer to keep the item and take from the seller the amount of the deficiency, because in the mere contract, attributes do not correspond to any part of the price, and because the seller did not consent to his property leaving his ownership for less than the specified price, thus he would be harmed. And removing harm from the buyer without harming the seller is possible by returning the item.
And the defect intended is that which existed with the seller and the buyer did not see it at the time of the contract or at the time of possession, because seeing it is consent to it.
Explanation: The scenario is that if the buyer discovers a defect in the sold item, he has the choice: if he wishes, he takes it for the full price; if he wishes, he returns it. The proof is that the absolute contract requires that the subject matter and the sale be free from defects, i.e., the absolute contract requires that the sale be free from defects, as the hadith of the Messenger witnesses. Thus the instruction is:
Indeed, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) bought a slave from 'Adda' ibn Khalid ibn Hawdhah and wrote in his guarantee: This is what Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah, bought from 'Adda' ibn Khalid ibn Hawdhah: a slave with no disease, no defect, and no treachery. The sale of a Muslim to a Muslim.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the chapter on Khiyar al-'Ayb (Option of Defect) in Al-Hidayah. It first explains why this chapter comes after the chapters on Khiyar al-Ru'ya and Khiyar al-Shart: those options are stronger (aqwa) because they prevent the completion of the transaction, whereas Khiyar al-'Ayb is weaker (ad'af) because it only prevents bindingness after completion. The term 'khiyar al-'ayb' is an idafa attributing the option to its cause (the defect). A defect ('ayb) is defined as something that departs from the sound original nature, making the item considered deficient. The core ruling is stated clearly: if a buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item that existed at the time of sale and was unknown to him at the time of contract and delivery, he has the choice to either keep the item for the full price or return it. He may not keep it and claim a partial refund for the defect. The proof is that the absolute contract implies soundness; when soundness is lacking, the buyer is given an option to prevent harm. Additionally, a hadith is cited where the Prophet (s) purchased a slave and had a written guarantee that the slave was free from defects, indicating the principle that goods should be sound. The commentary then reiterates the ruling in Urdu. This establishes the foundational rule for defect-based options in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of the buyer to return a defective item due to a pre-existing defect unknown at the time of sale.
عیب ('ayb): A defect that departs from the sound original nature, making the item considered deficient.
صفقہ (safqah): The transaction or contract of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the buyer's remedy when he discovers a pre-existing defect in the purchased item?
A) He must return the item and can claim damages.
B) He may keep the item and claim a partial refund.
C) He may either keep the item for the full price or return it.
D) He may only return the item within three days.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, khiyar al-'ayb gives the buyer the option to return the defective item or keep it for the full price; he cannot claim a partial refund.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Khiyar al-Ayb: Buyer's Option on Defect
Explains the ruling and proof for khiyar al-ayb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's options upon discovering a pre-existing defect and explain the Hanafi proof that prevents claiming a reduction.

Core Idea
A hidden defect gives the buyer only two choices: full acceptance or full return – no partial refund.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on Khiyar al-Ayb (Option of Defect). The passage explains why the contract implicitly requires the item to be sound, and how the buyer can avoid harm without harming the seller. We will see the textual proof from a hadith and the rational argument against allowing a reduction in price.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the buyer's options when a defect is discovered: he may keep the item with full price or return it for full refund. He cannot keep it and claim a reduction.
The ruling applies when the defect existed with the seller and the buyer was unaware at contract and delivery.
2
Understand the implied attribute of soundness
The absolute contract of sale inherently requires the sold item to be free from defects. This is called 'wasf al-salamah'.
When soundness is absent, the buyer's consent is incomplete, giving rise to the option.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The Prophet (peace be upon him) bought a slave and wrote a guarantee that the slave had no disease, hidden defect, or fraud. This establishes that a sale contract demands soundness.
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
4
Analyze why reduction is not allowed
Attributes (like soundness) do not have a separate portion of the price in the contract. Allowing a reduction would harm the seller, who did not consent to a lower price.
The buyer's harm can be fully removed by return, without harming the seller.
5
Identify the condition
The option is only valid if the defect existed with the seller and the buyer did not see it at the time of contract or delivery. Seeing and accepting implies consent and voids the option.
This condition ensures the buyer did not tacitly agree to the defect.

Source Text

ان رسول الله صلى الله عليه و سلم اشترى من عداء بن خالد بن هوذة عبدا وكتب في عهدته هذا ما اشترى محمد رسول الله من العداء بن خالد بن هوذة عبدا الاداء ولا غائلة و لا خبثة بيع المسلم من المسلم

نہیں ہے حالانکہ رضامندی حقیقت بیج میں داخل ہے پس رضا مندی کے فوت ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کو اختیار حاصل ہو گا۔ اور مشتری کو یہ اختیار حاصل نہ ہوگا کہ وہ مبیع معیب کو اپنے پاس روکے رکھے اور بائع سے شمن کی وہ مقدار واپس لے لے جو عیب کی وجہ سے کم ہو گئی ہے مثلا صبیح غیر معیب کی قیمت ایک ہزار روپے ہے اور معیب کی قیمت آٹھ سو روپیہ ہے تو دوسورد پیہ مقدار نقصان کہلائیں گے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ عیب کی وجہ سے مبیع کا وصف فوت ہوتا ہے اور عقد محض میں اوصاف کے مقابلہ میں شمن کا کوئی حصہ نہیں ہوتا ہے اور جب اوصاف کے مقابلہ میں ثمن نہیں ہوتا تو اوصاف کے کم ہونے سے کم نہ ہو گا۔ اس لیے کہا گیا ہے کہ مشتری کو بیع معیب اور بائع سے مقدار نقصان واپس لیا تو شمن کی مقررہ مقدار سے کم کے عوض دینا لا زم آئے گا اور اس میں کھلے طور پر بائع کا ضرر ہے پس بائع کو اس ضرر سے بچانے کے لیے مشتری کو مبیع معیب رو کنے اور مقدار نقصان لینے کا اختیار نہیں دیا گیا۔ رہا یہ سوال کہ مبیع معیب کو پورے شمن کے عوض لینے میں مشتری کا نقصان ہے تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ بائع کو ضرر پہنچائے بغیر مشتری کے ضرر کو دور کرناممکن ہے اس طور پر کہ مشتری مبیع معیب بائع کو واپس کر دے اور اپنا پوراشن واپس لے لے۔ اس صورت میں مشتری کو بھی نقصان نہ پہنچے گا اور بائع بھی ضرر سے بیچ جائے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ خیار عیب ثابت ہونے کے لیے شرط یہ ہے کہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں پیدا ہوا ہو۔ اور مشتری نے اس کو نہ عقد بیع کے وقت دیکھا ہو اور نہ قبضہ کے وقت دیکھا ہو۔ کیونکہ اگر دیکھ کر قبضہ کیا ہے تو یہ اس عیب پر رضا مندی ہوگی اور رضامندی ظاہر کرنے کے بعد خیار عیب باقی نہیں رہتا۔
Chapter of Option of Defect. Translation: This chapter is about the option of defect. Explanation: Since the options of inspection and condition are stronger because they prevent the completion of the contract, and the option of defect is weaker because it prevents binding after completion, and the stronger takes precedence over the weaker, therefore the option of condition and inspection were mentioned first, and the option of defect later. In khiyar al-ayb, the attribution of option to defect is of the type of attribution of something to its cause, because in khiyar al-ayb, the defect itself is the cause of the option. A defect is that which is absent from the sound original nature of a thing and due to which that thing is considered deficient. If the buyer is informed of a defect in the sold item, he has the option: if he wishes, he may take it for the full price, and if he wishes, he may return it, because the absolute contract demands the attribute of soundness. So when the attribute of soundness is lost, he has an option so that he is not harmed by being bound to something he does not consent to. He is not allowed to keep it and take a reduction, because attributes are not matched by any part of the price in the mere contract, and because the seller did not consent to its removal from his ownership for less than the specified price, so he would be harmed. And removing harm from the buyer is possible by return without harming him. What is meant by defect is that which existed with the seller and the buyer did not see it at the time of sale or at the time of delivery, because seeing it is consent to it. Explanation: The scenario is that if the buyer is informed of a defect in the sold item, then the buyer has the choice: if he wishes, he may take it for the full price, and if he wishes, he may return the item. The proof is that the absolute contract demands that the thing contracted upon be sound and free from defects, as the hadith of the Messenger testifies. Thus the Prophet (peace be upon him) bought a slave from 'Ada' ibn Khalid ibn Hawdhah and wrote in his guarantee: 'This is what Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah, bought from al-'Ada' ibn Khalid ibn Hawdhah: a slave without disease, without hidden defect, and without fraud. The sale of a Muslim to a Muslim.' In the hadith, 'da'' means disease in the stomach or liver, etc.; 'gha'ilah' means defects of action like the slave running away or stealing; and 'khabithah' means the appearance of a claim of ownership by another. Some said 'khabith' means madness. The translation of the hadith is: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) bought a slave from 'Ada' ibn Khalid ibn Hawdhah and wrote a document: 'Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah, bought a slave from al-'Ada' ibn Khalid ibn Hawdhah; there is no physical disease, no hidden defect, and no other claimant. This is the sale of a Muslim to a Muslim.' From this hadith it is also known that the contract of sale demands the soundness of the sold item, and if a defect exists, the attribute of soundness is lost. So due to the loss of the attribute of soundness, the buyer has the option so that he is not afflicted with harm he did not consent to, while consent is part of the reality of sale; so due to the loss of consent, he has the option. And the buyer does not have the option to keep the defective item and take back from the seller the amount by which the price was reduced due to the defect. For example, if the price of a sound item is 1000 rupees and the price of the defective item is 800 rupees, then 200 rupees is the amount of reduction. The proof is that the defect causes the loss of the attribute in the sold item, and in the bare contract there is no part of the price corresponding to attributes. And when there is no price for attributes, a decrease in attributes does not reduce the price. So it is said that the buyer does not have the option to keep the defective item and take back the amount of reduction from the price. A second proof is that the seller did not consent to remove the item from his ownership for less than the specified price. So if the buyer keeps the item and takes back the amount of reduction, then giving the item for less than the specified price would become necessary, and in that is clear harm to the seller. So to protect the seller from this harm, the buyer is not given the option to keep the defective item and take the amount of reduction. As for the question that in taking the defective item for the full price there is harm to the buyer, the answer is that it is possible to remove harm from the buyer without causing harm to the seller by the buyer returning the defective item to the seller and taking back his full price. In that case, neither the buyer suffers harm nor the seller. The author of al-Hidayah says: The condition for establishing the option of defect is that the defect arose while in the seller's possession, and that the buyer did not see it at the time of the contract nor at the time of delivery, because if he saw it and took delivery, that is consent to the defect, and after manifesting consent, the option of defect no longer remains.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on al-Hidayah, explaining the chapter on Khiyar al-Ayb (Option of Defect). The author begins by explaining why this chapter comes after the chapters on Khiyar al-Shart (Option of Condition) and Khiyar al-Ru'yat (Option of Inspection). The reason is that those two options prevent the completion of the contract (they are stronger), while Khiyar al-Ayb prevents the contract from becoming binding after completion (it is weaker). The stronger takes precedence. The term 'Khiyar al-Ayb' is an attribution of the option to its cause, since the defect itself is the cause of the option. A defect is defined as something that deviates from the sound original nature of the item, making it considered deficient. The core ruling: if the buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item that existed with the seller and was not seen by the buyer at the time of contract or delivery, the buyer has the option to either keep the item with the full price or return it. The buyer cannot keep the item and claim a partial refund of the price. The proof is twofold: first, the absolute contract of sale implies soundness; second, the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) when he bought a slave and stipulated freedom from defects. The hadith establishes that the seller guarantees the item is free from hidden defects. The author then provides a detailed rational argument: attributes (such as soundness) do not have a separate portion of the price allocated to them in the contract. Therefore, if the buyer keeps the item and demands a reduction, it would mean the seller is parting with his property without full consent, causing harm. The only way to avoid harming either party is to allow the buyer to either take the item with full price or return it entirely. The condition for the option is that the defect existed with the seller and the buyer was unaware at the time of contract and delivery; if the buyer saw the defect and still took delivery, he is deemed to have consented.

Key Terms
خیار عیب (khiyar al-ayb): The option granted to the buyer upon discovering a pre-existing defect in the purchased item that was not known at the time of contract or delivery.
عیب (ayb): A defect that deviates from the sound original nature of the item, causing a decrease in value.
وصف السلامة (wasf al-salamah): The attribute of soundness or freedom from defects that the contract of sale implies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item that existed with the seller, what are the buyer's options?
A) He may keep the item and claim a reduction in price equal to the defect.
B) He may either keep the item with the full price or return it for a full refund.
C) He must return the item; he cannot keep it under any circumstance.
D) He may keep the item and sue the seller for damages.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's option on a hidden defect is limited to full acceptance or full return; no partial refund is allowed to avoid harming the seller.

The Hanafi Definition of a Defect in Sales
Defines what constitutes a legal defect in sales according to Quduri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three elements of a defect in Hanafi sales law and apply the definition using merchant custom.

Core Idea
A defect is whatever reduces the price according to merchant custom — not a fixed list.

This lesson presents the foundational definition of a defect (ayb) from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. Understanding this definition is essential for applying the buyer's option of defect (khiyar al-ayb). The definition hinges on one key principle: it is not a matter of juristic enumeration, but of custom ('urf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three elements of the definition
The passage states: (1) something that causes a reduction in price, (2) according to the custom of merchants, (3) the reference is the custom of its people. These three elements together define a defect.
The custom of merchants (urf al-tujjar) is the benchmark.
2
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The harm (dharar) to the buyer is due to reduction in monetary value, which manifests as a lower market price. This connects the definition to the objective of preventing harm.
The rationale underlies the importance of custom.
3
Apply the definition to a scenario
Given a specific item and a claimed defect, determine if it qualifies by asking: Does this defect reduce the item's price according to the practice of the merchants in that market? If yes, it is a defect; if no, it is not.
Custom may vary by time and place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وكل ما اوجب نقصان الثمن فى عادة التجار فهو عيب لان التضرر بنقصان المالية وذالك بانتقاص القيمة و المرجع في معرفته عرف اهله
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا اور ہر وہ چیز جو تاجروں کی عادت میں نقصان شمن کا باعث ہو وہ عیب ہے اس لیے کہ ضرر ہونا مالیت کم ہونے سے ہے اور مالیت کم ہونا قیمت کے گھٹنے سے ہے اور اس کی معرفت تاجروں کے عرف پر موقوف ہے۔
He (al-Quduri) said: And everything that causes a reduction in the price according to the custom of merchants is a defect, because harm is due to a reduction in value, and that is by a decrease in price. The reference in knowing it is the custom of its people. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And every thing that causes a reduction in the price in the custom of merchants is a defect, because harm is from the decrease in monetary value, and that is by the decrease in price. Knowledge of it depends on the custom of the merchants.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is the definition of a defect ('ayb) as transmitted from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. It is a ruling-statement that establishes the criterion for what constitutes a defect that gives rise to the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb). The definition has three elements: (1) the defect must cause a decrease in the price; (2) the measure of that decrease is according to the custom of merchants (urf al-tujjar); (3) the underlying rationale is that harm (dharar) to the buyer is due to the reduction in monetary value, which manifests as a lower market price. The text emphasizes that the reference for determining defects is the custom of the people involved in the trade ('urf ahl al-tijarah). This means that defects are not defined abstractly by jurists but are identified by what merchants in that particular market consider a defect that reduces value. This is a practical, custom-based approach, allowing flexibility across time and place.

Key Terms
عیب (ayb): Anything that causes a reduction in price according to the custom of merchants.
عرف اهله (urf ahlihi): The custom of the relevant merchants/traders, which is the reference for identifying defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Quduri's definition, what determines whether something is a defect in a sale?
A) A list of specific defects mentioned in the Quran and Sunnah.
B) The opinion of a single jurist from the Hanafi school.
C) The custom of merchants regarding what reduces the price.
D) The buyer's personal preference.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a defect is defined by the custom of merchants: anything that reduces the price according to their practice is a defect.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Khiyar al-Ayb: Buyer Cannot Claim Price Reduction
Explains the hadith and ruling that the buyer must return the defective item, not keep it and claim compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-'ayb, explain why the buyer cannot claim a price reduction, and identify the condition for khiyar al-'ayb to be established.

Core Idea
The buyer of a defective item must return it for a full refund; he cannot keep it and claim compensation.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on when a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased item. While many think the buyer can demand a price reduction, the Hanafi school holds that the only remedy is rescission of the contract. We will explore the hadith evidence and rationale behind this ruling, as well as the condition that the defect must have existed in the seller's possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its key terms
Understand the three categories of defects mentioned: da' (illness), na'ilah (behavioral defects like absconding or stealing), and khabithah (third-party claims).
The hadith of the Prophet buying a slave with a guarantee against these defects.
2
Extract the ruling from the hadith
The hadith shows that the contract of sale inherently requires soundness. If a defect exists, the attribute of soundness is lost, and the buyer has an option to protect himself from harm he did not consent to.
Consent is integral to the contract; loss of consent gives the option.
3
Understand the two proofs against price reduction
First proof: The price is not apportioned to attributes, so loss of attributes does not reduce the price. Second proof: The seller did not consent to sell for less than the fixed price; demanding a reduction harms the seller.
Both proofs are grounded in protecting the seller's consent and contractual certainty.
4
Recognize the correct remedy
The buyer must return the defective item and get the full price back. This removes harm from both parties: the buyer is not stuck with a defective item, and the seller is not forced to accept a reduced price.
This is the only permitted remedy in the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the condition from Hidayah
For khiyar al-'ayb to be established, the defect must have occurred while in the seller's possession, and the buyer must not have seen it at the time of contract or delivery. If the buyer saw the defect and still took possession, he is deemed to have accepted it.
This condition protects against subsequent defects and waivers.

Source Text

کا ظاہر ہوتا ہے۔ اور بعض نے کہا کہ خبیث سے جنون مراد ہے اب حدیث کا ترجمہ یہ ہوگا کہ رسول اکرم صلى الله عليه وسلم نے عداء بن خالد بن ہوذہ سے ایک غلام خریدا اور ایک تحریر لکھی کہ محمد رسول اللہ نے عداء
