مقدار واپس لے لے جو عیب کی وجہ سے کم ہو گئی ہے مثلا صبیح غیر معیب کی قیمت ایک ہزار روپے ہے اور معیب کی قیمت آٹھ سو روپیہ ہے تو دوسورد پیہ مقدار نقصان کہلائیں گے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ عیب کی وجہ سے مبیع کا وصف فوت ہوتا ہے اور عقد محض میں اوصاف کے مقابلہ میں شمن کا کوئی حصہ نہیں ہوتا ہے اور جب اوصاف کے مقابلہ میں ثمن نہیں ہوتا تو اوصاف کے کم ہونے سے کم نہ ہو گا۔ اس لیے کہا گیا ہے کہ مشتری کو بیع معیب اپنے پاس روک کرشمن میں سے مقدار نقصان واپس لینے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا دوسری دلیل فیہ ہے کہ بائع مقرر کردہ شمن سے کم کے عوض مبیع کو اپنی ملک سے نکالنے پر راضی نہیں ہے پس اگر مشتری نے مبیع کو اپنے پاس روکا اور بائع سے مقدار نقصان واپس لیا تو شمن کی مقررہ مقدار سے کم کے عوض دینا لا زم آئے گا اور اس میں کھلے طور پر بائع کا ضرر ہے پس بائع کو اس ضرر سے بچانے کے لیے مشتری کو مبیع معیب رو کنے اور مقدار نقصان لینے کا اختیار نہیں دیا گیا۔ رہا یہ سوال کہ مبیع معیب کو پورے شمن کے عوض لینے میں مشتری کا نقصان ہے تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ بائع کو ضرر پہنچائے بغیر مشتری کے ضرر کو دور کرناممکن ہے اس طور پر کہ مشتری مبیع معیب بائع کو واپس کر دے اور اپنا پوراشن واپس لے لے۔ اس صورت میں مشتری کو بھی نقصان نہ پہنچے گا اور بائع بھی ضرر سے بیچ جائے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ خیار عیب ثابت ہونے کے لیے شرط یہ ہے کہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں پیدا ہوا ہو۔ اور مشتری نے اس کو نہ عقد بیع کے وقت دیکھا ہو اور نہ قبضہ کے وقت دیکھا ہو۔ کیونکہ اگر دیکھ کر قبضہ کیا ہے تو یہ اس عیب پر رضا مندی ہوگی اور رضامندی ظاہر کرنے کے بعد خیار عیب باقی نہیں رہتا۔
In the hadith, 'da'' refers to an illness in the stomach or liver etc., and 'na'ilah' refers to defects that are of the category of actions, like a slave absconding or stealing, and 'khabithah' refers to the appearance of some other person's claim to the slave. Some said 'khabith' means insanity. Now the translation of the hadith is: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) bought a slave from 'Ada' ibn Khalid ibn Hudhah and wrote a document: 'Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah, bought from 'Ada' ibn Khalid ibn Hudhah a slave in which there is no bodily illness, no internal defect, and no other claimant. (This is) the sale of a Muslim to a Muslim.' From this hadith it is also known that the contract of sale requires the soundness of the sold item, and if a defect exists, the attribute of soundness is lost. So due to the loss of the attribute of soundness, the buyer will have an option, so that the buyer does not suffer harm he did not agree to, whereas consent is included in the essence of the sale. So due to the loss of consent, he will have the option. And the buyer will not have the option to keep the defective sold item and take back from the seller that portion of the price that has been reduced due to the defect. For example, the price of a sound slave is one thousand rupees, and the defective one is eight hundred rupees, so two hundred rupees is called the amount of loss. The proof is that due to the defect, the attribute of the sold item is lost, and in the mere contract, no part of the price corresponds to the attributes. And when there is no price in exchange for attributes, then with the decrease of attributes, the price will not decrease. Therefore it is said that the buyer does not have the option to keep the defective sale item and take back the amount of loss from the price. The second proof in it is that the seller is not content to remove the sold item from his ownership for less than the fixed price. So if the buyer keeps the sold item and takes back the amount of loss from the seller, then it would be necessary to give the item for less than the fixed price, and in that there is clear harm to the seller. So to protect the seller from this harm, the buyer has not been given the option to keep the defective item and take the amount of loss. As for the question that taking the defective item for the full price harms the buyer, its answer is that it is possible to remove the buyer's harm without harming the seller, in such a way that the buyer returns the defective item to the seller and takes back his full price. In this case, the buyer will not suffer loss nor will the seller be harmed. The author of Hidayah says that for khiyar al-'ayb to be established, the condition is that the defect must have occurred while in the seller's possession, and the buyer must not have seen it at the time of the contract nor at the time of taking possession. Because if he took possession after seeing it, this would indicate consent to the defect, and after showing consent, khiyar al-'ayb does not remain.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of Hidayah discusses the ruling of khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). It begins by interpreting key terms from a hadith: 'da'' (illness), 'na'ilah' (behavioral defects like absconding or stealing), and 'khabithah' (another party's claim). Then it presents the hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) bought a slave with a guarantee of freedom from defects. From this, the commentator derives that the contract of sale inherently requires the sold item to be free from defects. When a defect exists, the attribute of soundness is lost, and the buyer has an option to protect himself from harm he did not consent to. The passage then states that the buyer does not have the option to keep the defective item and demand a reduction in price (arash). This is based on two arguments: 1) In a sale, the price is not apportioned to attributes, so loss of attributes does not reduce the price. 2) The seller did not agree to sell for less than the full price, so demanding a reduction harms the seller. The correct remedy is for the buyer to return the item and get the full price back. The passage ends with a condition from the author of Hidayah: khiyar al-'ayb is established only if the defect arose while in the seller's possession and the buyer did not see it at contract or delivery. If the buyer saw the defect and still took possession, he is deemed to have accepted it.

Key Terms
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option given to the buyer to annul the sale due to a defect in the sold item that existed at the time of contract.
داء (da'): In this hadith, refers to a physical illness, especially internal.
نائلہ (na'ilah): Defects that are actions, such as stealing or absconding.
خبیثہ (khabithah): That another person claims ownership of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, if a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased item, what is his remedy?
A) He can return the item and get a full refund.
B) He can keep the item and demand a reduction in price.
C) He can only sue the seller.
D) He must accept the item as is.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for khiyar al-'ayb: return the item or keep it; no partial refund.

Definition of Defect (Ayb) in Hanafi Sales Law
Definition of 'ayb as anything decreasing price by merchant custom, with commentary on rationale and types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'ayb according to Hanafi law and apply the merchant custom criterion to identify a defect.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a defect is whatever reduces the price according to merchants' custom.

This lesson unpacks Imam al-Quduri's concise definition of a defect ('ayb) in sales. The definition hinges on two key elements: harm through price reduction and the decisive role of trade custom. Understanding this definition is essential for applying the buyer's option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal concept being defined
The passage defines 'ayb (defect) in the context of sales. Focus on al-Quduri's exact wording: 'everything that necessitates a decrease in the price in the custom of merchants.'
2
Break down the definition into components
Identify the cause: decrease in price. Identify the criterion: custom of merchants. Understand that the definition ties together harm (dharar) with monetary loss.
3
Understand the rationale
The commentary explains that harm comes from loss of monetary value, which manifests as a price drop. Thus, any attribute causing a price drop is a defect.
4
Distinguish types of defects
Khwaharzadah distinguishes defects in essence (dhat) like a crack, and defects in benefit (manfa'ah) like a horse stumbling. Both are defects.
5
Recognize the role of custom in borderline cases
For attributes not affecting essence or benefit, the local merchant custom decides if it's a defect. This shows the Hanafi reliance on urf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عیب کی تعریف
قال وكل ما اوجب نقصان الثمن فى عادة التجار فهو عيب لان التضرر بنقصان المالية وذالك بانتقاص القيمة و المرجع في معرفته عرف اهله
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا اور ہر وہ چیز جو تاجروں کی عادت میں نقصان شمن کا باعث ہو وہ عیب ہے اس لیے کہ ضرر ہونا مالیت کم ہونے سے ہے اور مالیت کم ہونا قیمت کے گھٹنے سے ہے اور اس کی معرفت تاجروں کے عرف پر موقوف ہے۔

تشریح جن عیوب کی وجہ سے مشتری کو خیار عیب حاصل ہوتا ہے صاحب قدوری نے ان کے بارے میں ایک ضابطہ بیان فرمایا ہے ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ تاجروں کی عادت میں جو چیز شمن کے اندر نقصان پیدا کر دے وہ عیب شمار ہوگی کیونکہ آدمی کو ضرر پہنچتا ہے شی کی مالیت کم ہونے سے پس ثابت ہوا کہ ضر ر شئی کی قیمت کم ہونے سے لاحق ہوتا ہے اور جس چیز سے ضر ر لاحق ہو وہ عیب ہے اس لیے جو چیز شئی کے ثمن اور قیمت کے نقصان کا باعث ہو وہی عیب ہے لیکن اس کی معلومات تجار کی عادت اور ان کے عرف سے ہو سکتی ہے۔ فوائد شیخ الاسلام خواہر زادہ نے لکھا ہے کہ جو چیز بیع کی ذات میں مشاہدہ نقصان پیدا کر دے۔ جیسے حیوان کے ہاتھ پاؤں میں کبھی یا مثل ہونا اور برتنوں میں ٹوٹن ہونا یا وہ اس طبیع کے منافع میں نقصان پیدا کر دے۔ مثلاً گھوڑے کا ٹھو کر لینا تو یہ عیب ہے اور جو امر ذات یا منفعت میں نقصان پیدا نہیں کرتا اس میں لوگوں کا رواج معتبر ہے اگر وہ اس کو عیب شمار کریں تو یہ عیب ہے ورنہ نہیں ۔
Definition of defect: He (al-Quduri) said: And everything that necessitates a decrease in the price in the custom of merchants is a defect, because harm is caused by the decrease of monetary value, and that is by the decrease of the price. The reference in knowing it is the custom of its people. Translation: Al-Quduri said: Every thing that, in the custom of merchants, causes a decrease in the price is a defect, because harm comes from the decrease in monetary worth, and the decrease in monetary worth is by the decrease in price. And its knowledge is dependent on the custom of merchants. Commentary: Regarding the defects due to which the buyer gets khiyar al-'ayb, al-Quduri has stated a general rule: Whatever thing in the habit of merchants causes a decrease in the price will be considered a defect, because harm affects a person from the decrease of the thing's monetary value. So it is established that harm comes from the decrease of the thing's value, and whatever thing from which harm comes is a defect. Therefore, whatever causes a decrease in the thing's price and value is a defect. But its knowledge can only be obtained from the custom and habit of merchants. Benefits: Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzadah wrote: Whatever thing causes an observable defect in the essence of the sold item, like a break in an animal's hand or foot, or a crack in vessels, or causes a defect in its benefits, like a horse stumbling, then it is a defect. And as for that which does not cause a defect in the essence or benefit, the custom of the people is considered: if they consider it a defect, then it is a defect; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the legal concept of 'ayb (defect) in Hanafi law, quoting Imam al-Quduri's matn. The definition has two parts: (1) anything that reduces the price according to merchants' custom is a defect, and (2) the standard for determining defects is the custom of the trade. The commentary explains that the underlying rationale is harm caused by decrease in monetary value, which occurs through a drop in price. Since price is determined by the market, the reference for identifying defects is the common practice of merchants (urf al-tujjar). The passage also includes a useful elaboration from Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzadah, who distinguishes between defects that directly affect the essence (dhat) of the item (e.g., a break or crack) and those affecting its benefits (manafi; e.g., a horse that stumbles). Both are defects. If an issue does not affect essence or benefit, the local custom decides whether it is a defect. This demonstrates the Hanafi emphasis on urf (custom) as a source of law in mu'amalat (transactions).

Key Terms
عیب ('ayb): In Hanafi sales law, a defect is any attribute that reduces the price of an item according to merchants' custom.
عرف (urf): Custom, especially the common practice of merchants, which is the criterion for identifying defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Quduri's definition, what is the primary criterion for determining a defect in a sold item?
A) The opinion of the buyer
B) The custom of merchants
C) A list from the Quran
D) The opinion of the judge

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, a defect is defined by its effect on price in merchant custom, not by an exhaustive list.

Defects in Minor Slaves: Conditions for Absconding, Bed-Wetting, and Theft
Ruling on when these behaviors are considered defects in a minor slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a minor slave's absconding, bed-wetting, and theft are considered defects, and apply the Hanafi distinction between childhood and adulthood.

Core Idea
A slave's childhood defects are age-specific; after puberty, they are new defects only if repeated.

In sales of slaves, the buyer has the right to return a defective slave (khiyar al-'ayb). But what constitutes a defect can depend on the slave's age and mental capacity. This passage from the Hanafi school clarifies that certain behaviors—absconding, bed-wetting, and theft—are considered defects only when they occur during minority (sighar) and only if the child understands (ya'qil). The reasoning is rooted in the different causes: in children these behaviors stem from weakness, love of play, or carelessness, while in adults they stem from inner evil. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the option of defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the behaviors
List the three behaviors: abaq (absconding), al-bawl fi al-firash (bed-wetting), sariqah (theft). Recognize that these are potential defects in a slave.
These are not inherently defects; their status depends on context.
2
Apply the age condition
Note that the ruling states these are defects 'as long as he has not reached puberty' (malam yablugh). After puberty, they are not defects unless repeated after puberty.
The same behavior after majority is treated as a different condition due to different causes.
3
Understand the talil (cause)
Learn the reasoning: the sabab (cause) differs by age. In childhood, bed-wetting is due to weak bladder, absconding due to love of play, theft due to carelessness. In adulthood, these are due to internal disease or evil nature.
This difference in cause is why the ruling changes.
4
Define 'saghir'
The passage clarifies that 'saghir' means a child who understands (ya'qil). A non-understanding child is not considered an absconder, so his running away is not a defect.
This is a key limitation: not all minors are covered.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a case: Buyer purchases a minor slave. The slave wets the bed. Buyer wants to return. Determine: is it a defect? Check if the child understands; if yes, and he is still a minor, it is a defect. If after puberty, only if repeated.
This step integrates all conditions.

A slave's absconding, bed-wetting, and stealing in childhood are defects: "And absconding, bed-wetting, and stealing in a minor are defects as long as he has not reached puberty. When he reaches puberty, that is not a defect until he repeats it after puberty." Its meaning: When these appear while with the seller during his childhood, then they occur while with the buyer during his childhood, he can return him because it is the same defect. If they occur after his puberty, he cannot return him because it is something else. This is because the cause of these things differs by childhood and adulthood: bed-wetting in childhood is due to weak bladder, and after adulthood due to an internal disease; absconding in childhood is due to love of play, and stealing due to lack of carefulness; these two after adulthood are due to inner evil. And the meaning of 'saghir' is one who understands. As for one who does not understand, he is merely lost, not an absconder, so it is not a defect. Commentary: Abaq (absconding of a slave). Abaq is a defect whether the journey is long or short, because due to abaq the master's benefits are lost, and in this, the long journey and short journey are equal, so in both cases abaq is counted as a defect. And if a slave runs away from a usurper and comes to his master, this is not called abaq. And if a slave runs away from the usurper but does not go to his master and does not return to the usurper, there are two scenarios: (1) if he knows his master's location and ...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage discusses three specific behaviors in a slave - absconding (abaq), bed-wetting (al-bawl fi al-firash), and theft (sariqah) - and their status as defects triggering khiyar al-'ayb. The core ruling from the matn states that these are defects when they occur during the slave's minority (sighar), but after puberty (bulugh) they are not defects unless repeated. The reason is that the cause (sabab) differs: in children, these behaviors stem from weakness (weak bladder, love of play, carelessness), whereas in adults they stem from inner evil (khubth batini). Since the cause is different, the same behavior after majority is considered a different condition, not the same defect. Therefore, if a buyer sees these behaviors in a minor slave and later they occur after majority, they cannot be attributed to the same defect and the buyer cannot return the slave. The passage also clarifies that 'saghir' means a child who understands (ya'qil); a non-comprehending child is not considered an absconder. In the commentary, it adds that abaq is a defect even if the journey is short, because it still deprives the master of benefits. There is also a discussion about abaq in the context of a usurper, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
صغیر (saghir): A minor who has understanding (ya'qil); used in contrast to an infant who does not understand.
اباق ('abaq): A slave's act of running away from his master, considered a defect because it deprives the master of the slave's benefits.
بول فی الفراش (al-bawl fi al-firash): Bed-wetting, a defect in a slave, though in a child it is due to weak bladder.
سرقہ (sariqah): Theft by a slave, considered a defect; in a child it is due to carelessness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a slave's absconding if it occurs for the first time after the slave has reached puberty?
A) It is a defect, allowing the buyer to return the slave.
B) It is not a defect unless it repeats after puberty.
C) It is always a defect because absconding harms the master.
D) It is only a defect if the slave is still a minor.

Key Takeaway
Absconding, bed-wetting, and theft in a minor slave are defects only if the child understands and the behavior occurs before puberty; after puberty, only repetition makes them defects.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
The General Definition of a Defect (Ayb) in Hanafi Sales Law
Explains the general principle of defect definition based on custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a defect ('ayb) and apply it to determine whether a given condition is a defect based on merchant custom (urf).

Core Idea
A defect is anything that, by merchant custom, decreases the price; harm in value is the measure.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah lays the foundational principle for khiyar al-ayb. Al-Quduri defines a defect not by a fixed list but by the custom of merchants: anything that causes a loss in price is a defect. This rational, custom-based approach is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh and ensures flexibility in changing markets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Locate al-Quduri's general statement about what constitutes a defect in a sale. Note that the criterion is loss in price (thaman) according to merchant custom (urf al-tujjar).
The text says: 'تاجروں کی عادت میں جو چیز شمن کے اندر نقصان پیدا کر دے وہ عیب شمار ہوگی'.
2
Understand the Rationale
The passage explains that harm (darar) is caused by a decrease in value. Since the Shari'ah prohibits harming others, any condition that reduces the item's market value is considered a defect giving the buyer an option to return.
Darar is the underlying principle linking defect to the right of return.
3
Recognize the Role of Custom
The definition relies on custom (urf) rather than a fixed list. The commentator Khwahar Zadah adds examples: physical defects (e.g., crippled hand) and functional defects (e.g., horse stumbling) are defects only if custom so regards them.
Custom is dynamic; the same condition may be a defect in one market but not in another.
4
Apply to Examples
Consider hypothetical conditions (e.g., a slight discoloration in a garment) and decide whether they are defects by asking: Would merchants in that market consider this defect to reduce the price?
If yes, it is a defect even if not listed in classical texts.

چیز کا ارتکاب کیا پھر مولیٰ نے اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا تو غلام نے اگر مشتری کے پاس جا کر بھی اسی چیز کا اعادہ کیا تو مشتری کو اس عیب کی وجہ سے غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہو گا اور اگر مشتری کے پاس جا کر غلام نے اس عیب کا اعادہ نہیں کیا تو مشتری کو غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ قدوری کی عبارت حتى يعادوه بعد البلوغ کا یہی مطلب ہے جو خادم نے بیان کیا ہے۔ اس مسئلہ کی مزید وضاحت کرتے ہوئے صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ مذکورہ عیوب میں سے اگر کوئی عیب بچپن میں بائع کے پاس ظاہر ہوا پھر بچپن ہی میں وہ عیب مشتری کے پاس ظاہر ہوا تو مشتری کو اپنے خیار عیب کے تحت اس غلام کو واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ عیب جو مشتری کے پاس ظاہر ہوا ہے بعینہ وہی عیب ہے جو بائع کے پاس ظاہر ہوا تھا اور اگر کوئی عیب بائع کے پاس غلام کے اندر اس کے بچپن میں پیدا ہوا۔ بائع نے بچپن ہی میں اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا لیکن مشتری کے پاس اس عیب کا اعادہ غلام کے بالغ ہونے کے بعد ہوا تو مشتری کو خیار عیب حاصل نہ ہوگا اور اس غلام کو واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ غلام کے بالغ ہونے کے بعد جو عیب مشتری کے پاس رونما ہوا ہے وہ اس عیب کا غیر ہے جو بچپن میں بائع کے پاس ظاہر ہوا تھا اور مشتری کے پاس اگر مبیع کے اندر کوئی نیا عیب پیدا ہو جائے تو اس کی وجہ سے مشتری کو خیار عیب حاصل نہیں ہوتا ۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں مشتری کو غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ بالغ ہونے کے بعد ظاہر ہونے والا عیب بچپن میں ظاہر ہونے والے عیب کے مغایر اسلئے ہے کہ مذکورہ عیوب کے اسباب بچپن اور بلوغ کی وجہ سے مختلف جاتے ہیں۔ چنانچہ بچپن میں بستر پر پیشاب کرنا مثانہ کمزور ہونے کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے اور بالغ ہونے کے بعد اس کا سبب کوئی اندرونی بیماری ہے اور اسی طرح غلام کا بچپن میں بھگوڑا ہونا کھیل کو د پسند کرنے کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے اور چوری کا سبب ہے تو جہی اور لا ابالی پن ہے ۔ مگر بالغ ہونے کے بعد ان کا سبب خبث باطن ہے۔
صاحب بدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ متن میں صغیر سے مراد بجھدار بچہ ہے ورنہ بالکل ناسمجھ بچہ اگر کہیں چلا گیا تو اس کو بھٹکا ہوا تو کہا جاتا ہے مگر بھاگا ہوا نہیں کہا جاتا اور بھٹکنا عیب نہیں ہوتا۔ اسلئے اس پر اباق کا حکم بھی جاری نہ ہوگا۔
Explanation: The defects due to which the buyer obtains the option of defect. Author al-Quduri stated a principle regarding them: The principle is that whatever in the custom of merchants causes a loss in the price is considered a defect, because harm is caused to a person by the decrease in the value of the thing. So it is established that harm occurs from the decrease in the price of a thing, and whatever causes harm is a defect. Therefore, whatever causes a loss in the price and value of a thing is a defect, but knowledge of it can be obtained from the custom and usage of merchants. Benefits: Sheikh al-Islam Khwahar Zadah wrote: Whatever causes an observable defect in the essence of the sale, like a hand or foot being crippled or having a similar condition in an animal, or breakage in utensils, or causes a defect in its natural benefits, like a horse stumbling, then this is a defect. And regarding a matter that does not cause a defect in the essence or benefit, the practice of people is considered: if they consider it a defect, then it is a defect; otherwise, not.

The slave's running away, urinating in bed, and stealing are defects in a minor: "Al-abiq, al-bawl fi al-firash, and al-sariqah in a minor are a defect as long as he has not reached puberty. When he reaches puberty, that is not a defect until he repeats it after puberty." Its meaning: If these [actions] appeared at the seller's while he was minor, then they occurred at the buyer's while he was minor, he may return him because it is the very same [defect]. If they occurred after his puberty, he may not return him because it is different. This is because the cause of these things differs with childhood and adulthood: urinating in bed in childhood is due to weakness of the bladder, and after adulthood due to an internal disease; running away in childhood is due to love of play; and stealing is due to lack of care. Both of these after adulthood are due to internal wickedness. What is meant by "minor" is one who understands. As for one who does not understand, he is a wanderer, not a runaway, so it is not verified as a defect.

Translation: And the slave's running away, urinating in bed, and stealing are defects in childhood until he reaches puberty. When he reaches puberty, this is not a defect until he repeats it after puberty. Its meaning: When these things appear at the seller's during his childhood, then these same things occur at the buyer's during childhood, the buyer has the option to return him because it is the very same defect. If these things appear (at the buyer's) after his puberty, the buyer cannot return him because this defect is other than that. This is because the cause of these things differs due to puberty and non-puberty. So urinating in bed in childhood is due to weakness of the bladder, and after puberty due to internal disease; the slave's running away in childhood is due to love of play; stealing is due to lack of care; and these two things after puberty are due to internal wickedness. And by "minor" is meant one who understands; the child who does not understand is a wanderer, not a runaway, so this defect is not established.

Explanation of Ibaq: The slave's running away. Ibaq is a defect whether for the duration of a journey or less, because due to Ibaq the profits of the master are lost. And since in this [matter] the duration of a journey and less are equal, therefore in both cases Ibaq is considered a defect. If the slave runs away from a usurper and comes to his own master, it is not called Ibaq. If the slave runs away from the usurper but neither goes to his own master nor returns to the usurper, then there are two scenarios: First, he knows the location of his master and is able to go there; in this case this running away is considered a defect. Second, he does not know the location of his master, or he knows but is not able to go there; in this case this running away is not considered a defect.

Sariqah (Theft): Whether ten dirhams or less, in both cases it is a defect. Whether the theft is from the master or from someone other than the master, in both cases it is a defect. Yes, if he took some food item to eat from the master's property, this theft is not a defect. If he took it from the property of someone other than the master, it is considered a defect. If he took some food item from the master's property for selling, it is considered a defect.

A child's urinating in bed is considered a defect when the child is sensible enough to eat and drink alone. If he is not sensible, his urinating in bed is not considered a defect. According to author al-Kifayah, the urinating in bed of a child aged five years or more is a defect; and of a child less than that age is not a defect.

The scenario is: If a slave runs away in childhood, or urinates in bed, or steals, it is a defect before puberty; after puberty these things are not considered defects. Yes, if he repeats these things after puberty, then they are considered defects even after puberty. Its detail: The slave committed one of these mentioned things at his master's in childhood; then after puberty he committed it again at the master's; then the master sold this slave. If the slave repeats the same thing after going to the buyer, then the buyer has the option to return the slave due to this defect. If the slave does not repeat this defect after going to the buyer, the buyer does not have the option to return. This is the meaning of al-Quduri's phrase "حتى يعاوده بعد البلوغ" as explained by the servant.

Clarifying this issue further, author al-Hidayah said: If one of the mentioned defects appeared at the seller's in childhood, then that defect appeared at the buyer's in childhood, the buyer has the option to return this slave under his option of defect, because this defect that appeared at the buyer's is exactly the same defect that appeared at the seller's. If a defect occurred at the seller's in the slave's childhood, the seller sold this slave during childhood, but the repetition of this defect at the buyer's occurred after the slave's puberty, then the buyer does not have the option of defect and does not have the right to return this slave, because the defect that occurred at the buyer's after the slave's puberty is different from the defect that appeared at the seller's in childhood. And if a new defect occurs in the sold item at the buyer's, the buyer does not obtain the option of defect due to it. Therefore, in this case the buyer does not have the option to return the slave. Author al-Hidayah says: The defect appearing after puberty is different from the defect appearing in childhood because the causes of the mentioned defects differ due to childhood and puberty. So urinating in bed in childhood is due to weakness of the bladder, and after puberty its cause is some internal disease; similarly, the slave's running away in childhood is due to love of play; and the cause of stealing is lack of care and recklessness. After puberty, their cause is internal wickedness.

Author al-Bidayah says: In the text, "minor" means a sensible child; otherwise, a completely senseless child, if he goes somewhere, is called a wanderer, not a runaway, and wandering is not a defect. Therefore, the ruling of Ibaq will not apply to him.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah, explaining the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) in the sale of slaves. It begins by stating the general principle from al-Quduri: a defect is anything that, according to merchant custom, causes a decrease in the price. This is because harm is caused by loss of value. The principle is based on custom (urf) and is thus a rational derivation (dalil aqli).

The text then addresses three specific defects in a minor (saghir) slave: running away (abaq), urinating in bed (al-bawl fi al-firash), and stealing (sariqah). The ruling is that these are only considered defects if they occur before puberty. If they occur only after puberty, they are not defects unless the slave repeats them after puberty. The reason is that the underlying causes differ: in childhood, these actions result from weakness, playfulness, or lack of maturity; in adulthood, they stem from internal illness or moral corruption (khubth al-batin). Therefore, a post-puberty occurrence is a different phenomenon altogether and does not give the buyer the right to return the slave based on a pre-puberty defect.

A detailed analysis follows for each defect. For abaq, any absence whether short or long is a defect because it deprives the master of benefits. However, if a slave runs away from a usurper (ghasib) and returns to his master, it is not considered abaq. If he runs away but does not return, it depends on whether he knows his master's location and can reach it. For sariqah, the value (whether ten dirhams or less) and the victim (master or others) generally make it a defect, except if the slave takes food from the master for immediate consumption (since that is less blameworthy). For urinating in bed, the child must be old enough to eat and drink alone (i.e., around five years old according to al-Kifayah); otherwise, it is not a defect.

The core ruling is then summarized: if a defect appears at the seller's during childhood and reappears at the buyer's also during childhood, the buyer may return the slave because it is the same defect. If it reappears after puberty, it is a new and different defect, so the buyer cannot return. The commentary emphasizes that this distinction stems from the different causes (asbab) in each stage of life.

Finally, the passage clarifies that the term 'saghir' in the text refers to a child who understands (ya'qil). A completely senseless child's wandering is not considered running away, so it is not a defect.

Key Terms
عیب (ayb): A defect that causes a decrease in the price according to merchant custom.
خیار عیب (khiyar al-ayb): The option of the buyer to return a defective item and rescind the sale.
عرف (urf): Custom or common practice of the people, which in Hanafi fiqh is a valid source for determining factual matters like defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the primary criterion for determining whether a condition is a defect in a sale?
A) It must be specifically listed in Hanafi fiqh texts.
B) It must be a physical flaw that affects the item's function.
C) It must cause a decrease in the price according to merchant custom.
D) It must be something that the buyer would not normally accept.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines a defect by merchant custom, not by a fixed list, making the law flexible and responsive to market practices.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Specific Defects in Slaves: Abaq, Bawl, and Sariqah with Timing and Cause Distinction
Details three specific defects in minor slaves and the timing condition based on puberty and cause difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why running away (abaq), urinating in bed (bawl), and stealing (sariqah) are considered defects only in a minor slave and distinguish the ruling when these acts occur after puberty.

Core Idea
The same act is a different defect after puberty because its cause changes from childish weakness to moral corruption.

After laying out the general definition of defect, the passage turns to three specific defects that are especially common in slaves: running away, urinating in bed, and stealing. The ruling is nuanced: these are considered defects only when they occur in a minor (saghir) who understands (ya'qil). If they occur for the first time after puberty, they are not defects unless repeated. The reason is that the underlying causes differ — childhood actions stem from immaturity, while adult actions from internal wickedness. This talil (rationale) is crucial for understanding the Hanafi approach to causality in legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Defects and the General Rule
Locate the statement: 'الاباق والبول فى الفراش والسرقة فى الصغير عيب مالم يبلغ'. Note that these are defects only when the slave is a minor (saghir) before puberty (baligh).
The text explicitly limits the defects to the period before puberty.
2
Understand the Age Distinction
When the slave reaches puberty, the same acts are not considered defects unless they are repeated after puberty (حتى يعاوده بعد البلوغ). This means that if the acts occurred only in childhood and not after puberty, they are not grounds for return.
The key phrase is 'حتى يعاوده بعد البلوغ' — until he repeats it after puberty.
3
Grasp the Talil (Cause-Based Reasoning)
The passage provides a detailed explanation: the causes differ. Urinating in bed in childhood is due to weak bladder; in adulthood, due to internal disease. Running away in childhood is due to love of play; in adulthood, due to internal wickedness. Stealing in childhood is due to lack of care; in adulthood, due to internal evil. Therefore, the outward act is not the same defect because the cause (sabab) is different.
This cause-based reasoning is a hallmark of Hanafi usul al-fiqh, showing that the ruling is attached to the cause, not merely the act.
4
Apply the Rule to Scenarios
Consider scenarios: (1) A slave runs away as a child, is sold, and then runs away again as a child at the buyer's. The buyer may return because it is the same defect. (2) The same slave runs away as a child, then after puberty runs away at the buyer's. The buyer cannot return because it is a different defect due to different cause.
The scenario is explicitly worked out in the commentary.
5
Note the Definition of 'Saghir' (Minor)
The passage clarifies that 'saghir' means a child who understands (ya'qil). A completely senseless child (la ya'qil) is not considered a runaway (abaq) but a wanderer (dal), and the defect ruling does not apply.
This restricts the defect to minors capable of intentional action.

عیب شمار ہوگا۔ اور اگر غلام غاصب کے پاس سے بھاگ کر اپنے مولی کے پاس آیا تو اس کو اباق نہیں کہا جائے گا اور اگر غلام غاصب کے پاس سے بھا گا مگر نہ تو اپنے مولی کے پاس گیا اور نہ لوٹ کر غاصب کے پاس آیا تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں ایک تو یہ کہ وہ اپنے مولی کا ٹھکانہ پہنچانتا ہے اور وہاں جانے پر قادر بھی ہے تو اس صورت میں غلام کا یہ بھا گنا عیب شمار ہوگا۔ دوسرے یہ کہ وہ اپنے مولیٰ کا ٹھکانہ نہ جانتا ہو یا ٹھکا نہ تو جانتا ہوںگر وہاں جانے پر قادر نہ ہو تو اس صورت میں یہ بھا گنا عیب شمار نہ ہوگا۔ سرقہ ، چوری کرنا ، دس درہم ہو یا دس درہم سے کم ، دونوں صورتوں میں عیب ہے۔ چوری مولی کی ہو یا غیر مولی کی دونوں صورتوں میں عیب ہے۔ ہاں اگر کھانے کی کوئی چیز کھانے کیلئے مولی کی ملک سے لی ہو تو یہ سرقہ عیب نہ ہوگا۔ اور گر مولی کے علاوہ کی ملک سے لی ہو تو یہ عیب شمار ہوگا اور گر مولی کی ملک سے کھانے کی کوئی چیز فروخت کرنے کیلئے اٹھائی تو یہ عیب شمار ہو گا۔ بچہ کا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا عیب شمار ہوگا جبکہ بچہ اتنا سمجھدار ہو کہ وہ تنہا کھا پی سکتا ہے اور گر اتنا سمجھدار نہ ہو تو اس کا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا عیب شمار نہ ہو گا۔ صاحب کفایہ کے مطابق پانچ سال یا اس سے زائد عمر کے بچہ کا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا عیب ہے اور اس کم عمر کے بچہ کا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا عیب نہیں ہے۔
صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ بچپن میں کسی غلام کا بھاگ جانا ، یا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا یا چوری کرنا بالغ ہونے سے پہلے پہلے عیب ہے بالغ ہونے کے بعد یہ چیزیں عیب شمار نہ ہوں گی ۔ ہاں اگر بالغ ہونے کے بعد بھی ان چیزوں کا اعادہ ہوا تو بالغ ہونے کے بعد بھی عیب شمار ہوں گی ۔ تفصیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ غلام نے بچپن میں اپنے مولی کے پاس مذکورہ چیزوں میں سے کسی چیز کا ارتکاب کیا پھر بالغ ہونے کے بعد بھی مولی کے پاس اس چیز کا ارتکاب کیا پھر مولیٰ نے اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا تو غلام نے اگر مشتری کے پاس جا کر بھی اسی چیز کا اعادہ کیا تو مشتری کو اس عیب کی وجہ سے غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہو گا اور اگر مشتری کے پاس جا کر غلام نے اس عیب کا اعادہ نہیں کیا تو مشتری کو غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ قدوری کی عبارت حتى يعادوه بعد البلوغ کا یہی مطلب ہے جو خادم نے بیان کیا ہے۔ اس مسئلہ کی مزید وضاحت کرتے ہوئے صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ مذکورہ عیوب میں سے اگر کوئی عیب بچپن میں بائع کے پاس ظاہر ہوا پھر بچپن ہی میں وہ عیب مشتری کے پاس ظاہر ہوا تو مشتری کو اپنے خیار عیب کے تحت اس غلام کو واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ عیب جو مشتری کے پاس ظاہر ہوا ہے بعینہ وہی عیب ہے جو بائع کے پاس ظاہر ہوا تھا اور اگر کوئی عیب بائع کے پاس غلام کے اندر اس کے بچپن میں پیدا ہوا۔ بائع نے بچپن ہی میں اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا لیکن مشتری کے پاس اس عیب کا اعادہ غلام کے بالغ ہونے کے بعد ہوا تو مشتری کو خیار عیب حاصل نہ ہوگا اور اس غلام کو واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ غلام کے بالغ ہونے کے بعد جو عیب مشتری کے پاس رونما ہوا ہے وہ اس عیب کا غیر ہے جو بچپن میں بائع کے پاس ظاہر ہوا تھا اور مشتری کے پاس اگر مبیع کے اندر کوئی نیا عیب پیدا ہو جائے تو اس کی وجہ سے مشتری کو خیار عیب حاصل نہیں ہوتا ۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں مشتری کو غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ بالغ ہونے کے بعد ظاہر ہونے والا عیب بچپن میں ظاہر ہونے والے عیب کے مغایر اسلئے ہے کہ مذکورہ عیوب کے اسباب بچپن اور بلوغ کی وجہ سے مختلف جاتے ہیں۔ چنانچہ بچپن میں بستر پر پیشاب کرنا مثانہ کمزور ہونے کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے اور بالغ ہونے کے بعد اس کا سبب کوئی اندرونی بیماری ہے اور اسی طرح غلام کا بچپن میں بھگوڑا ہونا کھیل کو د پسند کرنے کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے اور چوری کا سبب ہے تو جہی اور لا ابالی پن ہے ۔ مگر بالغ ہونے کے بعد ان کا سبب خبث باطن ہے۔
صاحب بدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ متن میں صغیر سے مراد بجھدار بچہ ہے ورنہ بالکل ناسمجھ بچہ اگر کہیں چلا گیا تو اس کو بھٹکا ہوا تو کہا جاتا ہے مگر بھاگا ہوا نہیں کہا جاتا اور بھٹکنا عیب نہیں ہوتا۔ اسلئے اس پر اباق کا حکم بھی جاری نہ ہوگا۔
Explanation: The defects due to which the buyer obtains the option of defect. Author al-Quduri stated a principle regarding them: The principle is that whatever in the custom of merchants causes a loss in the price is considered a defect, because harm is caused to a person by the decrease in the value of the thing. So it is established that harm occurs from the decrease in the price of a thing, and whatever causes harm is a defect. Therefore, whatever causes a loss in the price and value of a thing is a defect, but knowledge of it can be obtained from the custom and usage of merchants. Benefits: Sheikh al-Islam Khwahar Zadah wrote: Whatever causes an observable defect in the essence of the sale, like a hand or foot being crippled or having a similar condition in an animal, or breakage in utensils, or causes a defect in its natural benefits, like a horse stumbling, then this is a defect. And regarding a matter that does not cause a defect in the essence or benefit, the practice of people is considered: if they consider it a defect, then it is a defect; otherwise, not.

The slave's running away, urinating in bed, and stealing are defects in a minor: "Al-abiq, al-bawl fi al-firash, and al-sariqah in a minor are a defect as long as he has not reached puberty. When he reaches puberty, that is not a defect until he repeats it after puberty." Its meaning: If these [actions] appeared at the seller's while he was minor, then they occurred at the buyer's while he was minor, he may return him because it is the very same [defect]. If they occurred after his puberty, he may not return him because it is different. This is because the cause of these things differs with childhood and adulthood: urinating in bed in childhood is due to weakness of the bladder, and after adulthood due to an internal disease; running away in childhood is due to love of play; and stealing is due to lack of care. Both of these after adulthood are due to internal wickedness. What is meant by "minor" is one who understands. As for one who does not understand, he is a wanderer, not a runaway, so it is not verified as a defect.

Translation: And the slave's running away, urinating in bed, and stealing are defects in childhood until he reaches puberty. When he reaches puberty, this is not a defect until he repeats it after puberty. Its meaning: When these things appear at the seller's during his childhood, then these same things occur at the buyer's during childhood, the buyer has the option to return him because it is the very same defect. If these things appear (at the buyer's) after his puberty, the buyer cannot return him because this defect is other than that. This is because the cause of these things differs due to puberty and non-puberty. So urinating in bed in childhood is due to weakness of the bladder, and after puberty due to internal disease; the slave's running away in childhood is due to love of play; stealing is due to lack of care; and these two things after puberty are due to internal wickedness. And by "minor" is meant one who understands; the child who does not understand is a wanderer, not a runaway, so this defect is not established.

Explanation of Ibaq: The slave's running away. Ibaq is a defect whether for the duration of a journey or less, because due to Ibaq the profits of the master are lost. And since in this [matter] the duration of a journey and less are equal, therefore in both cases Ibaq is considered a defect. If the slave runs away from a usurper and comes to his own master, it is not called Ibaq. If the slave runs away from the usurper but neither goes to his own master nor returns to the usurper, then there are two scenarios: First, he knows the location of his master and is able to go there; in this case this running away is considered a defect. Second, he does not know the location of his master, or he knows but is not able to go there; in this case this running away is not considered a defect.

Sariqah (Theft): Whether ten dirhams or less, in both cases it is a defect. Whether the theft is from the master or from someone other than the master, in both cases it is a defect. Yes, if he took some food item to eat from the master's property, this theft is not a defect. If he took it from the property of someone other than the master, it is considered a defect. If he took some food item from the master's property for selling, it is considered a defect.

A child's urinating in bed is considered a defect when the child is sensible enough to eat and drink alone. If he is not sensible, his urinating in bed is not considered a defect. According to author al-Kifayah, the urinating in bed of a child aged five years or more is a defect; and of a child less than that age is not a defect.

The scenario is: If a slave runs away in childhood, or urinates in bed, or steals, it is a defect before puberty; after puberty these things are not considered defects. Yes, if he repeats these things after puberty, then they are considered defects even after puberty. Its detail: The slave committed one of these mentioned things at his master's in childhood; then after puberty he committed it again at the master's; then the master sold this slave. If the slave repeats the same thing after going to the buyer, then the buyer has the option to return the slave due to this defect. If the slave does not repeat this defect after going to the buyer, the buyer does not have the option to return. This is the meaning of al-Quduri's phrase "حتى يعاوده بعد البلوغ" as explained by the servant.

Clarifying this issue further, author al-Hidayah said: If one of the mentioned defects appeared at the seller's in childhood, then that defect appeared at the buyer's in childhood, the buyer has the option to return this slave under his option of defect, because this defect that appeared at the buyer's is exactly the same defect that appeared at the seller's. If a defect occurred at the seller's in the slave's childhood, the seller sold this slave during childhood, but the repetition of this defect at the buyer's occurred after the slave's puberty, then the buyer does not have the option of defect and does not have the right to return this slave, because the defect that occurred at the buyer's after the slave's puberty is different from the defect that appeared at the seller's in childhood. And if a new defect occurs in the sold item at the buyer's, the buyer does not obtain the option of defect due to it. Therefore, in this case the buyer does not have the option to return the slave. Author al-Hidayah says: The defect appearing after puberty is different from the defect appearing in childhood because the causes of the mentioned defects differ due to childhood and puberty. So urinating in bed in childhood is due to weakness of the bladder, and after puberty its cause is some internal disease; similarly, the slave's running away in childhood is due to love of play; and the cause of stealing is lack of care and recklessness. After puberty, their cause is internal wickedness.

Author al-Bidayah says: In the text, "minor" means a sensible child; otherwise, a completely senseless child, if he goes somewhere, is called a wanderer, not a runaway, and wandering is not a defect. Therefore, the ruling of Ibaq will not apply to him.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah, explaining the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) in the sale of slaves. It begins by stating the general principle from al-Quduri: a defect is anything that, according to merchant custom, causes a decrease in the price. This is because harm is caused by loss of value. The principle is based on custom (urf) and is thus a rational derivation (dalil aqli).

The text then addresses three specific defects in a minor (saghir) slave: running away (abaq), urinating in bed (al-bawl fi al-firash), and stealing (sariqah). The ruling is that these are only considered defects if they occur before puberty. If they occur only after puberty, they are not defects unless the slave repeats them after puberty. The reason is that the underlying causes differ: in childhood, these actions result from weakness, playfulness, or lack of maturity; in adulthood, they stem from internal illness or moral corruption (khubth al-batin). Therefore, a post-puberty occurrence is a different phenomenon altogether and does not give the buyer the right to return the slave based on a pre-puberty defect.

A detailed analysis follows for each defect. For abaq, any absence whether short or long is a defect because it deprives the master of benefits. However, if a slave runs away from a usurper (ghasib) and returns to his master, it is not considered abaq. If he runs away but does not return, it depends on whether he knows his master's location and can reach it. For sariqah, the value (whether ten dirhams or less) and the victim (master or others) generally make it a defect, except if the slave takes food from the master for immediate consumption (since that is less blameworthy). For urinating in bed, the child must be old enough to eat and drink alone (i.e., around five years old according to al-Kifayah); otherwise, it is not a defect.

The core ruling is then summarized: if a defect appears at the seller's during childhood and reappears at the buyer's also during childhood, the buyer may return the slave because it is the same defect. If it reappears after puberty, it is a new and different defect, so the buyer cannot return. The commentary emphasizes that this distinction stems from the different causes (asbab) in each stage of life.

Finally, the passage clarifies that the term 'saghir' in the text refers to a child who understands (ya'qil). A completely senseless child's wandering is not considered running away, so it is not a defect.

Key Terms
اباق (abaq): A slave's running away from the master, considered a defect if it deprives the master of benefits.
سرقہ (sariqah): Theft by the slave, considered a defect generally, with exceptions for taking food for immediate consumption from the master.
صغیر (saghir): A child who has not reached puberty and understands (ya'qil).
بالغ (baligh): A person who has reached puberty; after which the causes of these defects are considered internal moral or physical issues.
خیار عیب (khiyar al-ayb): The option of the buyer to return a defective item and rescind the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when can a buyer return a slave who urinated in bed as a child, but the defect reappears after the slave reaches puberty?
A) The buyer can return the slave because the defect is the same.
B) The buyer cannot return the slave because the post-puberty occurrence is a different defect.
C) The buyer can return the slave only if the urination occurs again after puberty.
D) The buyer can always return the slave for any defect regardless of timing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, similar outward actions may have different legal consequences when the underlying causes differ due to age or maturity, emphasizing the importance of cause-based reasoning.

intermediate

Defects in Slaves: Ibaq, Sariqa, Bedwetting and Recurrence After Bulugh
Explaining when ibaq, sariqa, bedwetting are defects and effect of recurrence after bulugh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for when ibaq, sariqa, and bedwetting are defects in a slave, and apply the rule that recurrence after bulugh with the buyer is necessary for khiyar al-'ayb.

Core Idea
A childhood defect must recur after bulugh with the buyer to be actionable; the causes differ.

This passage addresses a subtle issue in khiyar al-'ayb: how to handle defects that appear in childhood but may vanish or change cause by adulthood. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the same behavior in childhood and adulthood due to different underlying causes. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly applying the buyer's right to return a defective slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify each defect and its conditions
List the three defects discussed: ibaq (running away), sariqa (theft), and bedwetting. For each, note the conditions given for it to be considered a defect (e.g., ibaq requires the slave to know the master's location and be able to return; sariqa is always a defect except for eating from the master's property; bedwetting requires the child to be sensible enough to eat and drink alone).
The text also cites Sahib Kifayah's opinion that bedwetting is a defect only for children aged five or above.
2
Understand the general rule for recurrence after bulugh
The central rule: If a defect existed in childhood (with the seller) but did not recur after the slave reached adulthood while with the buyer, it is not a valid defect. Recurrence with the buyer is essential. The reasoning is that the causes in childhood (e.g., weak bladder, love of play) differ from those in adulthood (disease, inner wickedness), making them legally distinct defects.
Quduri's phrase 'hata yu'adu ba'da al-bulugh' encapsulates this rule.
3
Apply the rule to two scenarios
Scenario A: Defect appears in childhood with seller and recurs while still a child with buyer → buyer has khiyar al-'ayb (same defect). Scenario B: Defect appears in childhood with seller but only recurs after adulthood with buyer → no khiyar al-'ayb (different defect due to changed cause).
This distinction is clarified by Sahib al-Hidayah and Sahib al-Bidayah.
4
Trace the talil (reasoning) for the distinction
The text provides the cause (sabab) for each behavior in childhood and adulthood: bedwetting in childhood is due to weak bladder, in adulthood due to disease; ibaq in childhood due to love of play, in adulthood due to inner wickedness; sariqa in childhood due to thoughtlessness, in adulthood due to depravity. Because the causes are different, the defects are different.
This is a classic Hanafi talil connecting legal ruling to the underlying cause.
5
Note the definition of 'saghir' in the matn
Sahib al-Bidayah clarifies that 'saghir' in the matn means a sensible child, not an infant. A non-sensible child who wanders is 'lost' not 'runaway', and thus not a defect.

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہشتم جانے پر قادر بھی ہے تو اس صورت میں غلام کا یہ بھا گنا عیب شمار ہوگا۔ دوسرے یہ کہ وہ اپنے مولیٰ کا ٹھکانہ نہ جانتا ہو یا ٹھکا نہ تو جانتا ہوںگر وہاں جانے پر قادر نہ ہو تو اس صورت میں یہ بھا گنا عیب شمار نہ ہوگا۔ سرقہ ، چوری کرنا ، دس درہم ہو یا دس درہم سے کم ، دونوں صورتوں میں عیب ہے۔ چوری مولی کی ہو یا غیر مولی کی دونوں صورتوں میں عیب ہے۔ ہاں اگر کھانے کی کوئی چیز کھانے کیلئے مولی کی ملک سے لی ہو تو یہ سرقہ عیب نہ ہوگا۔ اور گر مولی کے علاوہ کی ملک سے لی ہو تو یہ عیب شمار ہوگا اور گر مولی کی ملک سے کھانے کی کوئی چیز فروخت کرنے کیلئے اٹھائی تو یہ عیب شمار ہو گا۔ بچہ کا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا عیب شمار ہوگا جبکہ بچہ اتنا سمجھدار ہو کہ وہ تنہا کھا پی سکتا ہے اور گر اتنا سمجھدار نہ ہو تو اس کا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا عیب شمار نہ ہو گا۔ صاحب کفایہ کے مطابق پانچ سال یا اس سے زائد عمر کے بچہ کا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا عیب ہے اور اس کم عمر کے بچہ کا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا عیب نہیں ہے۔
صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ بچپن میں کسی غلام کا بھاگ جانا ، یا بستر پر پیشاب کرنا یا چوری کرنا بالغ ہونے سے پہلے پہلے عیب ہے بالغ ہونے کے بعد یہ چیزیں عیب شمار نہ ہوں گی ۔ ہاں اگر بالغ ہونے کے بعد بھی ان چیزوں کا اعادہ ہوا تو بالغ ہونے کے بعد بھی عیب شمار ہوں گی ۔ تفصیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ غلام نے بچپن میں اپنے مولی کے پاس مذکورہ چیزوں میں سے کسی چیز کا ارتکاب کیا پھر بالغ ہونے کے بعد بھی مولی کے پاس اس چیز کا ارتکاب کیا پھر مولیٰ نے اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا تو غلام نے اگر مشتری کے پاس جا کر بھی اسی چیز کا اعادہ کیا تو مشتری کو اس عیب کی وجہ سے غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہو گا اور اگر مشتری کے پاس جا کر غلام نے اس عیب کا اعادہ نہیں کیا تو مشتری کو غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ قدوری کی عبارت حتى يعادوه بعد البلوغ کا یہی مطلب ہے جو خادم نے بیان کیا ہے۔ اس مسئلہ کی مزید وضاحت کرتے ہوئے صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ مذکورہ عیوب میں سے اگر کوئی عیب بچپن میں بائع کے پاس ظاہر ہوا پھر بچپن ہی میں وہ عیب مشتری کے پاس ظاہر ہوا تو مشتری کو اپنے خیار عیب کے تحت اس غلام کو واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ عیب جو مشتری کے پاس ظاہر ہوا ہے بعینہ وہی عیب ہے جو بائع کے پاس ظاہر ہوا تھا اور اگر کوئی عیب بائع کے پاس غلام کے اندر اس کے بچپن میں پیدا ہوا۔ بائع نے بچپن ہی میں اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا لیکن مشتری کے پاس اس عیب کا اعادہ غلام کے بالغ ہونے کے بعد ہوا تو مشتری کو خیار عیب حاصل نہ ہوگا اور اس غلام کو واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ غلام کے بالغ ہونے کے بعد جو عیب مشتری کے پاس رونما ہوا ہے وہ اس عیب کا غیر ہے جو بچپن میں بائع کے پاس ظاہر ہوا تھا اور مشتری کے پاس اگر مبیع کے اندر کوئی نیا عیب پیدا ہو جائے تو اس کی وجہ سے مشتری کو خیار عیب حاصل نہیں ہوتا ۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں مشتری کو غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ بالغ ہونے کے بعد ظاہر ہونے والا عیب بچپن میں ظاہر ہونے والے عیب کے مغایر اسلئے ہے کہ مذکورہ عیوب کے اسباب بچپن اور بلوغ کی وجہ سے مختلف جاتے ہیں۔ چنانچہ بچپن میں بستر پر پیشاب کرنا مثانہ کمزور ہونے کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے اور بالغ ہونے کے بعد اس کا سبب کوئی اندرونی بیماری ہے اور اسی طرح غلام کا بچپن میں بھگوڑا ہونا کھیل کو د پسند کرنے کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے اور چوری کا سبب ہے تو جہی اور لا ابالی پن ہے ۔ مگر بالغ ہونے کے بعد ان کا سبب خبث باطن ہے۔
صاحب بدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ متن میں صغیر سے مراد بجھدار بچہ ہے ورنہ بالکل ناسمجھ بچہ اگر کہیں چلا گیا تو اس کو بھٹکا ہوا تو کہا جاتا ہے مگر بھاگا ہوا نہیں کہا جاتا اور بھٹکنا عیب نہیں ہوتا۔ اسلئے اس پر اباق کا حکم بھی جاری نہ ہوگا۔
Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah volume eight ... able to go, then in this case the slave's running away is considered a defect. Second, that he does not know his master's location or knows it but is not able to go there, then in this case running away is not considered a defect. Theft, stealing, whether ten dirhams or less than ten dirhams, in both cases it is a defect. Whether the theft is from the master or from someone other than the master, in both cases it is a defect. Yes, if he took something edible from the master's property to eat, then this theft is not a defect. And if he took it from someone other than the master, then it is considered a defect; and if from the master's property he took something edible to sell, then it is considered a defect. A child wetting the bed is considered a defect when the child is sensible enough to eat and drink alone. If not that sensible, then wetting the bed is not a defect. According to Sahib Kifayah, a child of five years or older wetting the bed is a defect, and a younger child wetting the bed is not a defect. The mas'alah is that a slave's running away in childhood, or wetting the bed, or stealing before reaching adulthood is a defect. After adulthood these things are not considered defects. Yes, if after adulthood these things recur, then after adulthood they are also considered defects. The detail is that the slave committed one of these things in childhood with the seller, then after adulthood he also committed it with the seller, then the seller sold the slave. If the slave then repeats the same thing with the buyer, then the buyer has the option to return the slave due to this defect. And if the slave does not repeat this defect with the buyer, then the buyer does not have the option to return the slave. This is the meaning of Quduri's phrase 'hata yu'adu ba'da al-bulugh' which Khadim has explained. Further clarifying this issue, Sahib al-Hidayah said that if any of the mentioned defects appeared in childhood with the seller, and then the same defect appeared with the buyer while still in childhood, then the buyer has the option of defect to return the slave, because this defect that appeared with the buyer is exactly the same defect that appeared with the seller. But if a defect occurred with the seller while the slave was a child, and the seller sold him while still a child, but the recurrence of this defect with the buyer occurred after the slave reached adulthood, then the buyer does not have the option of defect and cannot return the slave, because the defect that occurred with the buyer after adulthood is different from the defect that appeared with the seller in childhood, and if a new defect occurs in the sold item with the buyer, the buyer does not have the option of defect for that. Therefore, in this case the buyer does not have the right to return the slave. Sahib al-Hidayah says that the defect appearing after adulthood is different from the defect appearing in childhood because the causes of the mentioned defects differ due to childhood and adulthood. Thus, bedwetting in childhood is due to a weak bladder, while after adulthood its cause is some internal disease. Similarly, a slave running away in childhood is due to love of play, and theft is due to thoughtlessness and carelessness, but after adulthood their cause is inner wickedness. Sahib al-Bidayah says that in the matn, 'saghir' refers to a sensible child; otherwise, a completely senseless child who goes somewhere is called 'lost' not 'runaway', and being lost is not a defect, so the ruling of ibaq does not apply to him.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary of al-Hidayah, explaining when certain defects in slaves (running away, theft, bedwetting) are considered legal defects giving the buyer the right to return (khiyar al-'ayb). The key principle is that the same behavior in childhood and adulthood may have different underlying causes, and therefore a defect that appeared in childhood is only actionable if it recurs after the slave reaches adulthood while with the buyer. The text distinguishes between sensible and non-sensible children for bedwetting, and between different types of theft. It also cites the conditions for running away: the slave must be able to return and know the master's location; otherwise it is not considered ibaq. The ruling from Sahib al-Hidayah emphasizes that if the defect does not recur with the buyer, the buyer has no right to return, even if the defect existed before purchase. The text also clarifies from Sahib al-Bidayah that 'saghir' in the matn means a sensible child, as a non-sensible child wandering off is termed 'lost' not 'runaway', and is not a defect.

Key Terms
عیب ('ayb): A defect in the sold item that existed at the time of sale, giving the buyer the right of return (khiyar al-'ayb).
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's option to return a defective item due to a pre-existing defect, upon discovery after purchase.
اباق (ibaq): Running away of a slave from the master; a defect if the slave had the ability to return and knew the master's location.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave ran away frequently in childhood but after reaching adulthood never ran away. The seller sold him without informing. The buyer discovers the childhood history. Does the buyer have the right to return the slave?
A) Yes, because running away in childhood is a permanent defect.
B) No, because without recurrence after bulugh at the buyer's, it is not a valid defect.
C) Yes, only if the slave ran away more than three times.
D) No, because running away is never a defect in a slave.

Key Takeaway
Childhood defects in slaves only give the buyer a right to return if they recur after the slave reaches adulthood while with the buyer, due to the different underlying causes.

Childhood Insanity as a Perpetual Defect in Sales
Explaining the meaning of 'perpetual defect' and the necessity of recurrence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that childhood insanity is a perpetual defect and identify the condition of recurrence for khiyar al-'ayb.

Core Idea
Childhood insanity is a perpetual defect because its cause is uniform, but the buyer must witness recurrence to return the slave.

In Hanafi fiqh, a defect in a slave that existed before sale gives the buyer the right to return (khiyar al-'ayb). A special case is insanity that first appears in childhood: even if the slave appears sane at the time of sale, the defect is considered perpetual—but only if the insanity recurs with the buyer. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Jami' al-Saghir and its clarification in al-Hidayah, addressing why recurrence is still required despite the cause being the same.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its source
Locate the statement from Jami' al-Saghir: 'al-junun fi al-saghir 'ayb abadan'. This is the basis for the perpetual defect rule.
The text is a qawl sahabi from Imam Muhammad.
2
Understand the 'illah (cause)
The cause for considering it the same defect is the uniformity of the cause: fasad al-'aql (corruption of the mind) is the same in childhood and adulthood.
This talil shows why the defect persists in legal effect even if the symptom disappears temporarily.
3
Clarify the meaning of 'abadan' (forever)
Sahib al-Hidayah explains that 'abadan' does not waive recurrence; it means the defect retains its legal identity as the same defect. Recurrence is required because Allah can cure it.
Prevents misapplication that would allow return without observable defect.
4
State the condition for khiyar al-'ayb
The buyer must observe insanity recur after taking possession. If it never recurs, no return is allowed; Allah may have cured it.
Even if the seller had the child slave insane, the buyer must experience it to exercise the option.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on duration
Mashayikh differ on how long insanity must last to be considered a defect: any duration, more than a day and night, or continuity. This affects whether a brief recurrence qualifies.
The mufta bihi position is that recurrence is required, but the minimum duration may vary by context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جنون صغر میں ہمیشہ عیب ہے

في.
ترجمہ جامع صغیر میں کہا اور جنون بچپن میں ہمیشہ کے واسطے عیب ہے اور اس کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ اگر حالت صفر میں بائع کے قبضہ میں مجنون ہوا۔ پھر مشتری کے قبضہ میں بچپن میں یا بالغ ہونے کے بعد جنون نے عود کیا کیونکہ یہ بعینہ جنون اول ہے اسلئے کہ دونوں حالتوں میں سب متحد ہے اور وہ عقل کا فاسد ہونا ہے۔ اور اس کے یہ معنی نہیں نہیں کہ مشتری کے پاس اس کا عود کر نا شرط نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ اس کو زائل کرنے پر قادر ہیں۔ اگر چہ کم زائل ہوتا ہے۔ پس غلام واپس کرنے کیلئے جنون کا عود کر نا ضروری ہے۔ تشریح جنون اور اغماء میں فرق یہ ہے کہ جنون کی وجہ سے انسان مسلوب العقل ہو جاتا ہے۔ اور اغماء کی وجہ سے مستور العقل ہوتا ہے۔ بعض حضرات مشائخ نے فرمایا کہ جنون مطلقاً عیب ہے اگر چہ ایک ساعت کا ہو۔ اور بعض نے کہا کہ اگر ایک دن رات سے زائد جنون رہا ہو تو وہ عیب ہے۔ اور ایک دن رات یا اس سے کم کا جنون عیب نہیں ہے۔ اور بعض نے کہا کہ جنون اگر لگا تار ہے تو عیب ہوگا اور اگر لگا تار نہیں ہے تو عیب شمار نہ ہوگا ۔
صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر غلام یا باندی کو بچپن میں جنون لاحق ہوا تو وہ ہمیشہ عیب شمار ہوگا یعنی اگر بچپن میں بائع کے قبضہ میں مجنون ہوا پھر مشتری کے قبضہ میں جا کر عود کر آیا خواہ بچپن میں خواہ بالغ ہونے کے بعد تو یہ جنون عیب شمار ہوگا اور مشتری کو اسکی وجہ سے مبیع واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہوگا کیونکہ جو جنون مشتری کے قبضہ میں لاحق ہوا ہے یہ وہی جنون ہے جو بائع کے قبضہ میں غلام یا باندی کو لاحق ہوا تھا اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ جنون کا سبب بلوغ اور عدم بلوغ کی دونوں حالتوں میں ایک ہے یعنی عقل کا فاسد ہونا۔ پس جب دونوں حالتوں میں جنون کا سبب ایک ہے تو غلام کے بالغ ہونے کے بعد مشتری کے قبضہ میں جو جنون اس کو لاحق ہوا ہے یہ وہی جنون ہوگا جو بائع کے قبضہ میں بالغ ہونے سے پہلے غلام کو لاحق ہوا تھا۔ اور جب وہی جنون ہے جو بائع کے قبضہ میں پیدا ہوا تھا تو یہ یقیناً مشتری کیلئے خیار عیب کا سبب ہوگا ۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مصنف کے قول عیب ابدا کے معنی یہ نہیں ہیں کہ مشتری کے قبضہ میں جنون کا عود کرنا شرط نہیں ہے۔ جیسا کہ ظاہر عبارت سے وہم ہوتا ہے۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ اس پر قادر ہیں کہ وہ جنون کو زائل کر دیں اگر چہ زائل کیا جانا کم واقع ہوا ہے۔ پس خیار عیب کے تحت غلام واپس کرنے کیلئے مشتری کے قبضہ میں جنون کا عود کرنا ضروری ہے۔ اگر مشتری کے قبضہ میں جنون کا اعادہ نہیں ہوا تو مشتری کو خیار عیب کے تحت غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا اور یہ سمجھا جائے گا کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے اپنی قدرت سے جنون کو زائل فرما دیا ہے۔ وما ذالک علی الله بعزیز
Insanity in childhood is always a defect. He said: 'And insanity in childhood is a defect forever' and its meaning is that if [the slave] became insane in childhood in the hand of the seller, then it recurred in the hand of the buyer, whether in childhood or after adulthood, he returns it because it is exactly the first, since the cause in both cases is one: the corruption of the mind. It does not mean that recurrence with the buyer is not a condition, because Allah is able to remove it, although it is rarely removed. So recurrence is necessary for return. Translation of Jami' al-Saghir: He said: And insanity in childhood is a defect forever, and its meaning is that if in childhood while in the seller's possession he became insane, then in the buyer's possession, whether in childhood or after adulthood, the insanity recurred, because it is exactly the first insanity, since the cause in both conditions is unified: the corruption of the mind. Its meaning is not that recurrence with the buyer is not a condition, because Allah is able to remove it, although it is rarely removed. So for returning the slave, recurrence of insanity is necessary. Explanation: The difference between insanity and fainting is that due to insanity a person becomes deprived of reason, and due to fainting the reason is concealed. Some mashayikh said that insanity is absolutely a defect even if for one hour. Some said if it lasts more than one day and night, it is a defect; one day and night or less is not a defect. Some said if it is continuous, it is a defect; if not continuous, it is not considered a defect. The mas'alah is that if a male or female slave was afflicted with insanity in childhood, it will always be considered a defect. That is, if in childhood while in the seller's possession [the slave] became insane, then in the buyer's possession it recurred, whether in childhood or after adulthood, then this insanity is a defect and the buyer has the right to return the sold item because of it, because the insanity that occurred in the buyer's possession is exactly the same insanity that had occurred in the seller's possession. And its reason is that the cause of insanity in both states of majority and minority is one: the corruption of the mind. So when the cause is one in both states, then the insanity that occurs in the buyer's possession after the slave reaches adulthood is the same insanity that occurred in the seller's possession before adulthood. And since it is the same insanity that originated in the seller's possession, it certainly becomes a cause of khiyar al-'ayb for the buyer. Sahib al-Hidayah says that the author's saying 'defect forever' does not mean that recurrence with the buyer is not a condition, as might be misunderstood from the apparent wording. The reason is that Allah is able to remove it, although removal is rare. So for returning the slave under khiyar al-'ayb, it is necessary that insanity recurs with the buyer. If there is no recurrence with the buyer, the buyer does not have the option to return the slave, and it will be understood that Allah removed the insanity by His power. And that is not difficult for Allah.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Jami' al-Saghir, explaining that insanity (junun) occurring in a slave during childhood is considered a perpetual defect ('ayb abadan) because the underlying cause—corruption of the mind (fasad al-'aql)—is the same whether in childhood or adulthood. However, the author clarifies that 'perpetual' does not mean the buyer can return the slave without witnessing a recurrence. Instead, the buyer must still observe the insanity return after purchase. The text also records a difference among mashayikh regarding the minimum duration of insanity to be a defect: some consider any duration (even an hour), some require more than a day and night, and some require continuity. The passage contrasts junun with ighma' (fainting), where reason is concealed rather than lost. The key ruling is that if insanity occurred before adulthood with the seller and then recurs (even after bulugh) with the buyer, it is the same defect and the buyer has khiyar al-'ayb. If it does not recur, Allah may have cured it, and no return is allowed.

Key Terms
جنون (junun): Insanity, a state of loss of reason (maslub al-'aql) that is a defect in a slave.
اغماء (ighma'): Fainting, where reason is concealed (mastur al-'aql) rather than lost; not automatically a defect.
فساد العقل (fasad al-'aql): Corruption of the mind, the common cause of insanity in both childhood and adulthood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is childhood insanity considered a perpetual defect ('ayb abadan)?
A) Because it always lasts throughout the slave's life.
B) Because the cause (fasad al-'aql) is the same whether in childhood or adulthood.
C) Because the seller guarantees it will recur.
D) Because the buyer must accept the slave regardless.

Key Takeaway
Childhood insanity is a perpetual defect due to uniform cause, but the buyer must witness recurrence to exercise khiyar al-'ayb.

Bad Breath and Body Odor as Defects: Gender and Purpose
Ruling statement with rationale on defects in slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether bad breath and body odor are defects in male and female slaves, and explain the rationale based on intended purpose.

Core Idea
A defect is defined by how it affects the intended use: intimacy for females, service for males.

In sales, a defect ('ayb) is any flaw that diminishes the value or impairs the intended use of the item. The Hanafi school applies this principle to slaves, considering their primary purpose. For a female slave, intimacy is a key purpose, so bad breath and body odor are defects. For a male slave, service is the primary purpose, so these odors are not defects unless they stem from an underlying disease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the text and identify the two conditions discussed: bad breath (bakhr) and body odor (dhafar). Note that the ruling differs based on the gender of the slave.
2
State the ruling for each gender
For a female slave (jariya), both conditions are defects. For a male slave (ghulam), they are not defects unless caused by a disease.
3
Analyze the rationale (talil)
The text provides the reason: for females, the intended purpose may be intimacy (istifrash), and odors hinder that. For males, the purpose is service (istikhdam), and odors do not interfere unless from a disease.
4
Apply the exception
If the odor in a male slave is due to a disease, then it becomes a defect because the disease itself is a defect, and the odor indicates an underlying illness.
The disease is a defect regardless of odor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
گندہ دھن اور منہ کی بد بو والا ہونا لونڈی میں عیب ہے غلام میں نہیں
قال و البخر والذفر عيب فى الجارية لان المقصود قد يكون الاستفراش وهما يخلان به وليس بعيب في الغلام لان المقصود هو الاستخدام ولا يخلان به الا ان يكون من داء لان الدأ عيب
Having foul breath and body odor is a defect in a female slave, not in a male slave. He said: 'And bad breath (bakhran) and foul body odor (dhafar) are a defect in a female slave because the intended purpose may be for lying with her, and these two hinder that. They are not a defect in a male slave because the intended purpose is service, and they do not hinder that, unless it is from a disease, for a disease is a defect.'
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the text, explaining the distinction between defects applicable to male and female slaves. The key principle is that the classification of a condition as a defect depends on the primary purpose of the slave: for a female slave (jariya), the intended use often includes intimacy (istifrash), so bad breath (bakhr) and body odor (dhafar) are defects because they interfere with that purpose. For a male slave (ghulam), the primary purpose is service (istikhdam), and these odors do not inherently impede service, so they are not considered defects unless they stem from a disease (da'), in which case the disease itself is a defect. This reflects the Hanafi focus on the intended use of the sold item when defining defects.

Key Terms
بخر (bakhr): Bad breath from the mouth, considered a defect in female slaves.
ذفر (dhafar): Foul body odor, especially from armpits, considered a defect in female slaves.
استفراش (istifrash): Lying with for intimacy, the intended purpose of a female slave that makes odor a defect.
استخدام (istikhdam): Service, the primary purpose of a male slave; odor does not impede service normally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because body odor is always a defect.
B) No, because body odor in a male slave is not a defect unless from a disease.
C) Yes, because the buyer might be sensitive to odors.
D) No, because the slave is otherwise healthy.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, whether a condition is a defect depends on the intended use of the item: for female slaves, intimacy is a purpose, so odors are defects; for male slaves, service is the purpose, so odors are defects only if caused by disease.
