تشریح ماتن فرماتے ہیں کہ بائع کو یہ اختیار نہیں ہے کہ سلا ہوا کپڑایا رنگا ہوا کپڑایا گھی ملا ہو اسٹو مشتری سے لے لے اور اس کوشن واپس کر دے۔ کیونکہ مبیع کا زیادتی کے ساتھ بائع کی طرف واپس کرنے کا ممتنع ہونا بائع کے حق کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے، بلکہ شریعت کے حق کی وجہ سے ہے۔ اس لئے کہ مبیع کے ساتھ ملی ہوئی زیادتی ربوا کے معنی میں ہے، اور رباء کی حرمت شریعت کا حق ہے۔ پس جب صبیع کے ساتھ ملی ہوئی زیادتی کا واپس کرنا حق شرع کی وجہ سے ممنوع ہے، تو بائع کو زیادتی کے ساتھ مبیع واپس لینے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ اگر مشتری نے
If he cuts the cloth and sews it, or dyes it red, or mixes سويق with ghee, then discovers a defect, he returns by its reduction because return is prevented due to the addition. For there is no way to rescind the original without it, since it is inseparable, and no way to rescind with it, since the addition is not part of the sold item, so it is absolutely prevented. And the seller has no right to take it, because the prevention is due to the right of Shari'ah, not his right. If the buyer sells it after seeing the defect, he returns by the reduction, because return was absolutely prevented before, so he does not become a detainer by the sale. And from this we say: whoever buys a cloth and cuts it as a garment for his young child and sews it, then discovers a defect, he does not return by the reduction; but if the child is adult, he returns, because ownership was established in the first case before sewing, and in the second after delivery to him.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage continues from the previous lesson, now addressing cases where the buyer not only cuts but also further alters the item by sewing, dyeing, or mixing with other substances (e.g., سويق with ghee). The ruling: the buyer still claims compensation, but for a different reason — not because of the cutting alone, but because the added element is inseparable, making rescission of the sale impossible both with and without the addition. The commentary explains that the addition prevents both returning the item without it (inseparable) and returning it with it (since the addition is not part of the original sale contract). Therefore, to protect the buyer from harm, compensation is allowed. Additionally, the seller cannot force the buyer to return the altered item, as the prohibition stems from Shari'ah (due to riba implications), not the seller's right. Interestingly, if the buyer sells the altered item after discovering the defect, he may still claim compensation, because return was already impossible from the start due to legal prohibition. This contrasts with the earlier case of mere cutting. Finally, a sub-case: if the buyer sews the cloth for his minor child, he cannot claim compensation because ownership transferred before sewing; but for an adult child, he can, because ownership transfers after sewing (upon delivery).

Key Terms
زیادتی (ziyadah): An addition to the sold item that is inseparable and not part of the original sale, such as sewing, dyeing, or mixing.
ربوا (riba): In this context, the prohibition of riba (usury) is the reason why the seller cannot take back the altered item, as the addition resembles a form of prohibited exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer sews a cloth after purchase and then discovers a pre-existing defect, can the seller demand the buyer to return the cloth?
A) Yes, because the buyer created a new defect.
B) No, because return is prevented by Shari'ah (riba-like prohibition).
C) Yes, if the seller agrees to pay for the sewing.
D) No, only if the buyer sells it to a third party.

Key Takeaway
An inseparable addition by the buyer after purchase blocks rescission of the sale; the buyer's remedy is limited to compensation for the defect.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

6 min
Compensation for Defect after Buyer Adds Inseparable Addition
States ruling when buyer alters item, then discovers defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the remedy when a buyer adds an inseparable addition to a defective item and explain why return is impossible.

Core Idea
After inseparable addition, the buyer cannot return the item but can claim compensation for the defect.

When a buyer modifies a purchased item—such as cutting and sewing cloth or mixing sattu with ghee—and then discovers a pre-existing defect, the usual option of returning the item (radd bil-‘ayb) becomes impossible. The Hanafi school rules that the buyer must keep the item and instead claims compensation (naqsān al-‘ayb) for the difference in value caused by the defect. This lesson explores the legal reasoning behind this ruling and its practical implications in sale transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Recognize the buyer's action: cutting and sewing cloth, dyeing, or mixing ingredients. The ruling is that compensation is taken, not return.
The act is an addition that cannot be separated.
2
Understand the two possible rescission methods
One without the addition (impossible because addition cannot be separated), and one with the addition (impermissible because the addition is not part of the sale).
3
Analyse why each method is impossible
Without addition: due to inseparability. With addition: because rescission applies only to the sold item, not to something extra.
The reasoning hinges on the definition of mabi‘ (sold item).
4
Conclude the remedy: compensation (naqsan)
Since return is completely prevented, the buyer is allowed to claim the loss from the defect (difference in value) to remove harm.
This is a concession from the Shari‘ah.
5
Note the seller's position
The seller cannot force the buyer to return the item with the addition because the prevention is a Shari‘ah right, not the seller's right.
The seller cannot take back the item even if willing to pay for the addition.

Source Text

پھر اگر کپڑا کاٹ کر اس کو سلایا یا اس کو سرخ رنگ میں رنگا یا ستو کو گھی کے ساتھ ملایا پھر کسی عیب پر واقف ہوا۔ تو اس کا نقصان واپس لے لے کیونکہ زیادتی کی وجہ سے مبیع واپس کرنا ممتنع ہے اس لئے اصل مبیع میں بغیر زیادتی کے فسخ کرنے کی کوئی وجہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ زیادتی اس سے جدا نہیں ہو سکتی ہے اور زیادتی کے ساتھ بھی فتح کرنے کی کوئی وجہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ زیادتی مبیع نہیں ہے اس لئے واپس کرنا بالکل ممتنع ہو گیا۔
تشریح مسئلہ، اگر مشتری نے کپڑا کاٹ کر سلا لیا یا اس کپڑے کو سرخ رنگ میں رنگ دیا یا ستوخرید کر اس میں گھی یا مکھن ملا دیا پھر اس عیب پر واقف ہوا جو بائع کے پاس پیدا ہوا تھا۔ تو مشتری نقصان عیب واپس لے سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ مبیع کے ساتھ ایک زائد چیز مخلوط ہونے کی وجہ سے مبیع کا واپس کرنا ممتنع ہو گیا۔ اس طور پر کہ اصل مبیع میں بیج یا تو بغیر زیادتی کے فسخ کرے گا یا زیادتی کے ساتھ ۔ اور یہ دونوں صورتیں ممکن نہیں ہیں۔ اول تو اس لئے کہ شی زائد اصل مبیع سے جدا نہیں ہو سکتی ہے پس بغیر شی زائد کے اصل مبیع میں بیچ فسخ کرنا کس طرح ممکن ہو سکتا ہے اور دوسری صورت اس لئے ممکن نہیں ہے کہ شی زائد مبیع نہیں ہے اور فن بیع مبیع کے اندر ہوتا ہے غیر مبیع کے اندر نہیں ہوتا۔ اس لئے زیادتی کے ساتھ فتح کرنا بھی ممکن نہ ہوگا۔ پس جب بیع فسخ کر کے مبیع واپس کرنا بالکل ممتنع ہو گیا تو مشتری سے ضر ر دور کرنے کے لئے مشتری کو نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا مجاز قرار دیا گیا ہے۔
بائع مبیع کو واپس لینا چاہے تو نہیں لے سکتا
وليس للبائع ان ياخذه لان الامتناع لحق الشرع لا لحقه
Then if he cut the cloth and sewed it, or dyed it red, or mixed sattu with ghee, then became aware of a defect, he takes back its loss because due to the addition, returning the sold item is prevented, so there is no reason to rescind the original sold item without the addition because the addition cannot be separated from it, and there is no reason to rescind with the addition because the addition is not part of the sold item, so returning is completely prevented. Explanation of the issue: If the buyer cut cloth and sewed it, or dyed that cloth red, or bought sattu and mixed ghee or butter in it, then became aware of a defect that existed with the seller, then the buyer can take back the compensation for the defect. Because due to an extra thing being mixed with the sold item, returning the sold item became prevented. In this way: either he rescinds the original sold item without the addition, or with the addition. And both these forms are not possible. First, because the extra thing cannot be separated from the original sold item, so how can rescission of the sale in the original sold item without the extra thing be possible? And the second form is not possible because the extra thing is not part of the sold item, and rescission of sale occurs in the sold item, not in something other than the sold item. So rescission with the addition is also not possible. So when rescinding the sale and returning the sold item became completely prevented, then to remove harm from the buyer, the buyer was allowed to take back the compensation for the defect. The seller wants to take back the sold item, but he cannot. And the seller does not have the right to take it because the prevention is due to a right of the Shari'ah, not due to his right.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement followed by its explanatory commentary. The author states that if a buyer makes an inseparable addition to the purchased item—such as cutting and sewing cloth, dyeing it, or mixing sattu (a type of grain) with ghee—and then discovers a pre-existing defect, the buyer cannot return the item but can claim compensation for the defect (arsh). The reasoning is that returning the item would require either separating the addition (impossible) or returning the addition with it (impermissible because the addition is not part of the sale). The seller also cannot force the buyer to return the item with the addition, because the prohibition on taking back an item with an addition is a right of the Shari'ah (due to riba considerations), not a right of the seller. The relief of compensation is granted to the buyer to prevent his loss.

Key Terms
نقصان عیب (naqsān al-‘ayb): Compensation for the defect; the difference in value between the sound and defective item.
ممتنع (mumtani‘): Prevented, not possible due to a legal impediment.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission, annulment of a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer sews cloth after purchase and then discovers a defect, what is the remedy according to the Hanafi school?
A) He can return the cloth and get a full refund.
B) He can only claim compensation for the defect and keep the cloth.
C) He must keep the cloth and has no remedy.
D) He can return the cloth but must pay for the sewing.

Key Takeaway
When the buyer's addition makes return impossible, the only remedy is compensation for the defect.

Selling Defective Altered Item: Right to Compensation
Explains that selling the altered item after defect discovery does not bar compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer's right to compensation after selling a defective altered item, and explain the rationale that prevents forfeiture.

Core Idea
Selling an altered item after discovering the defect does not forfeit compensation because the barrier to return existed before the sale.

This passage from the matn clarifies that a buyer's subsequent sale of an altered item does not imply acceptance that would nullify compensation. The inability to return the item due to the inseparable addition is independent of the sale, so the buyer retains the right to claim damages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The buyer sells the item after seeing the defect. The ruling: he can still claim compensation (nuqsan).
The act of selling does not forfeit the right.
2
Note the pre-existing obstacle
The item could not be returned before the sale because of an inseparable alteration (e.g. sewing cloth).
This obstacle is independent of any subsequent act.
3
Understand the consequence of selling
By selling, the buyer does not become a 'hābis' (detainer) who voluntarily retains the item.
The sale is irrelevant to the right to compensation.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
The right to compensation is tied to the inability to return, not to possession or subsequent transactions.
A rational proof (dalil aqli): since return was already impossible, selling does not create a new bar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان باعه المشترى بعد ماراى العيب رجع بالنقصان لان الرد متنع اصلا قبله فلايكون بالبيع حابسا للمبيع
So if the buyer sells it after seeing the defect, he returns to (claim) the compensation because return was originally prevented before it, so by selling he does not become a detainer of the sold item.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This is a brief proof text (matn) stating that if the buyer sells the altered item to someone else after discovering the defect, he can still claim compensation from the original seller. The reason is that the inability to return the item existed before the sale (due to the inseparable addition). Therefore, by selling it, the buyer does not become a 'hābis' (detainer) who would forfeit the right to compensation. The commentary elaborates: since the buyer could not return the item, and the seller's right to take it back was also blocked by Shari'ah, the buyer's subsequent sale does not change his right to claim compensation. The key principle is that the right to compensation is tied to the inability to return, not to the buyer's act of selling.

Key Terms
حابس للمبيع (hābis li al-mabī‘): One who retains the sold item voluntarily, thus forfeiting the right to compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer sews cloth, discovers a defect, then sells the cloth to a third party, can he still claim compensation from the original seller?
A) No, because selling implies acceptance.
B) Yes, because the return was already impossible before the sale.
C) Yes, but only if he informs the third party of the defect.
D) No, because he no longer has the item.

Key Takeaway
A buyer who sells an altered defective item after discovering the defect retains the right to compensation because the inability to return existed prior to the sale.

Buyer Cutting Cloth for Child: Minor vs Adult
Ruling on buyer cutting cloth for child before defect discovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the ruling on compensation when a buyer cuts cloth for a minor child versus an adult child, and explain the underlying principle of constructive ownership (tamlīk) and detainer (hābis).

Core Idea
Cutting cloth for a minor child transfers ownership before sewing, forfeiting compensation; for an adult, delivery is required, so compensation remains.

This lesson explores a nuanced Hanafi ruling on the buyer's right to compensation after discovering a defect. The key is whether an act of cutting and sewing constitutes constructive transfer of ownership (tamlīk) to the child. The distinction between a minor and an adult child hinges on who can legally take possession. Understanding this distinction clarifies when a buyer becomes a detainer (hābis) and loses the right to claim compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
State the two cases: buyer cuts and sews cloth for (1) minor child, (2) adult child. For minor: no compensation; for adult: compensation allowed.
The ruling is not about the validity of the sale but about the right to claim defect compensation.
2
Understand the operative factor (ta'lil)
The text explains the reasoning: ownership (tamlīk) was obtained before sewing in the minor case, and after sewing by delivery (taslim) in the adult case. Identify why this timing matters.
For a minor, the father as guardian can take possession on behalf of the child constructively. Cutting with intent to gift constitutes constructive possession.
3
Apply the concept of hābis (detainer)
A buyer who detains the item by an act implying acceptance (like cutting for a minor) becomes a hābis and forfeits the right to compensation. Confirm that cutting for a minor makes the buyer a hābis; for an adult, it does not because ownership hasn't transferred.
The act of cutting itself is not the cause; it is the intention to transfer ownership that triggers the constructive possession.
4
Distinguish the two cases
For minor: cutting = constructive delivery → ownership transfers → buyer becomes hābis → no compensation. For adult: cutting only, delivery needed later → ownership not transferred until actual delivery → buyer not yet hābis → compensation allowed.
The same physical act (cutting) yields different rulings based on the legal capacity of the recipient.
5
Summarize the principle
The ruling illustrates that the timing of ownership transfer (before or after sewing) determines whether the buyer is a hābis. The principle is based on the guardian's ability to perfect delivery for a minor.
This principle is consistent with the broader Hanafi rule that constructive possession (qabd hukmi) suffices for certain legal effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن هذا قلنا ان من اشترى ثوبا فقطعه لباسا لولده الصغير وخاطه ثم اطلع على عيب لا يرجع بالنقصان ولو كان الولد كيبر يرجع لان التمليك حصل فى الاول قبل الخياطة وفى الثاني بعده بالتسليم اليه
And from this we said that whoever buys a cloth and cuts it as a dress for his minor child and sews it, then comes to know of a defect, he does not return to (claim) the compensation; but if the child is adult, he returns (claims) because ownership was obtained in the first before sewing, and in the second after sewing by delivery to him.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents a specific application of the principle of 'hābis'. The author (quoting Imam al-Quduri) states that if a buyer cuts cloth with the intention of making a garment for his minor child and sews it, then discovers a defect, he cannot claim compensation. The reason is that cutting the cloth with the intent of giving it to the minor child constitutes a constructive transfer of ownership (tamlik) before sewing, since the father as guardian can take possession on behalf of the child. Thus, the buyer becomes a 'hābis' (detainer) who has voluntarily disposed of the item, forfeiting the right to compensation. In contrast, if the child is an adult, cutting alone does not transfer ownership; actual delivery (taslim) is required. Since the item is sewn before delivery, the buyer does not become a detainer at the time of defect discovery, so he retains the right to compensation.

Key Terms
تملیک (tamlīk): Transfer of ownership; here, constructive transfer to a minor via intention and cutting.
حابس (hābis): One who detains the sold item by an act implying acceptance, thereby forfeiting the right to return or compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer buys cloth, cuts it into a dress for his adult son, sews it, then discovers a defect. Can he claim compensation?
A) Yes, because ownership transferred only after sewing and delivery.
B) No, because cutting alone transfers ownership.
C) Yes, but only if the son did not pay for it.
D) No, because sewing is an addition that bars return.

Key Takeaway
The timing of ownership transfer (before or after sewing) determines whether the buyer becomes a hābis and loses the right to defect compensation.

Compensation for Defect After Freeing a Slave for Compensation: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on kitabah case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on defect compensation when a slave is freed for compensation (kitabah) and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
When freeing for compensation, detaining the substitute bars further claim; Sahibayn hold no compensation, Abu Hanifa allows it.

This passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a buyer who frees a slave under a kitabah contract can later claim compensation for a hidden defect. The text first establishes the general rule: if the slave dies or is freed unconditionally, the buyer can claim compensation. It then addresses the special case where freedom is granted for a payment, triggering a difference between the Sahibayn and Imam Abu Hanifa. Understanding this dispute requires tracing the role of istihsan and the concept of 'detaining the substitute'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The text states that if the slave dies or is freed without compensation, the buyer can claim compensation (naqsan) for a hidden defect. This is because ownership ends, making return impossible but still allowing a claim.
Death and unconditional freeing are treated similarly by istihsan.
2
Understand the qiyas and its override
By qiyas, freeing would be like killing: the buyer's own act prevents return, so no compensation. But istihsan overrides this, treating freeing like death because the slave is not a permanent object of ownership.
This shows the power of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Focus on the special case: freeing for compensation (kitabah)
The text says: 'wa in a'taqahu 'ala mal lam yarja' bi shay'.' The majority (Sahibayn) hold that when the buyer frees the slave for a payment (kitabah), he cannot claim compensation because he has 'detained his substitute' (habasa badalahu). Accepting the substitute is like keeping the original, so defect claim is lost.
This is the crux of the dispute.
4
Compare Abu Hanifa's contrary view
Abu Hanifa holds that even when freeing for compensation, the buyer can claim defect compensation because the act of freeing terminates ownership entirely, regardless of whether a substitute was received. Ownership termination is the decisive factor.
Internal Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa vs. the two companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن اشترى عبدا فاعتقه اومات عنده ثم اطلع على عيب رجع بنقصانه اما الموت فلان الملك ينتهى به والامتناع حكمى لا يفعله واما الاعتاق فالقياس فيه ان لا يرجع لان الامتناع بفعله فصار كالقتل في الاستحسان يرجع لان العتق انهاء الملك لان الادمى ما خلق في الاصل محلا للملك وانما يثبت الملک فيه موقتا الى الاعتاق فكان انهاء فصار كالموت وهذا لان الشئى يتقرر بانتهاء فيجعل كان الملك باق والرد متعذر والتدبير والاستيلاد بمنزلته لان تعذر النقل مع بقاء المحل امر حكمى وان اعتقه على مال لم يرجع بشئى لانه حبس بدله وجس البدل كحبس المبدل وعن ابي حنيفة انه يرجع لانه انها للملك وان كان بعوض
He said: And whoever buys a slave and frees him or he dies in his possession, then comes to know of a defect, he returns (to claim) its compensation. As for death, because ownership ends with it, and the prevention (of return) is a legal (constructive) prevention, not by his act. As for freeing, by qiyas he should not return because the prevention is by his act, so it becomes like killing. By istihsan, he returns because freeing terminates ownership, because a human was not originally created as a subject of ownership; ownership is established in him temporarily until freeing, so it (freeing) is a termination, so it becomes like death. And this is because a thing is confirmed upon its termination, so it is as if ownership remains and return is difficult. Tadbir and istilad are like it because the difficulty of transfer with the subsistence of the locus is a legal matter. And if he freed him for compensation (kitaba), he does not return anything because he detained his substitute, and detaining the substitute is like detaining the substituted. And from Abu Hanifa that he returns because he terminated ownership, even if for compensation.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a hidden defect when the buyer has either freed a slave or the slave dies before the defect is known. The author (al-Quduri) states that in both cases, the buyer can claim compensation (naqsan) for the defect. The reasoning differs: Death ends ownership automatically, so the inability to return is not caused by the buyer—he can still claim compensation. Freeing, however, is an act of the buyer, but by istihsan (juristic preference), he can still claim compensation because freeing terminates ownership much like death, and the slave as a human is not permanently a subject of ownership. The qiyas (analogy) would have been to treat freeing like killing (where compensation is not given), but istihsan overrides. The text also mentions that if the buyer frees the slave for compensation (i.e., in a kitabah contract), he cannot claim compensation because he has accepted a substitute. However, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that even in that case compensation can be claimed because ownership is terminated. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the istihsan ruling that compensation is returned when freeing or death occurs, but not when freeing for compensation (except according to Abu Hanifa).

Key Terms
اعتاق (i‘tāq): Manumission, freeing a slave.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference, a method of legal reasoning that overrides qiyas in favor of a more equitable outcome.
تدبير واستيلاد (tadbīr wa istīlād): Tadbir: freeing a slave after the owner's death; istilad: a slave woman becoming umm walad by bearing her master's child.
البيع مع الخيار (al-bay‘ ma‘ al-khiyār): Sale with an option to return; in this context, the buyer has no option to return after freeing or death, but can still claim compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), if a buyer frees a slave for compensation (kitabah) and then discovers a hidden defect, what is the ruling?
A) The buyer can claim compensation because freeing terminates ownership.
B) The buyer cannot claim compensation because he detained the substitute (badal).
C) The buyer can claim compensation only if the defect was latent.
D) The buyer cannot claim compensation because the slave is no longer in his possession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a buyer who frees a slave for compensation cannot claim defect compensation because accepting a substitute (badal) bars further claim, per the majority view.

The Principle of Ḥābis li al-Mabīʿ and Its Application to Tailored Cloth
Explains the ḥābis principle with tailoring examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a buyer is a ḥābis li al-mabīʿ and determine whether he can claim defect compensation (naqṣ al-ʿayb) using the Hanafi criterion of prevention of return.

Core Idea
A buyer who prevents return forfeits defect compensation; intention and ownership transfer matter.

The Hanafi school holds that a buyer who has made the return of a defective item impossible (ḥābis li al-mabīʿ) cannot claim compensation for the defect. This lesson examines the principle through the example of tailoring cloth for a minor versus a major child. Understanding when the buyer becomes a 'preventer' is crucial for correct application in sales transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the General Rule
Identify the ruling: a buyer who is a ḥābis li al-mabīʿ (prevents return) cannot claim defect compensation. The buyer is considered ḥābis if his own action makes return impossible.
The key is the buyer's action, not the seller's.
2
Identify the Two Scenarios
From the text: (1) if the buyer is ḥābis, no compensation; (2) if not ḥābis, compensation is allowed. The text first gives examples where the buyer is not ḥābis (selling after addition), then applies to tailoring.
The examples of sewing, dyeing, mixing oil – if sold afterwards, the buyer is not ḥābis because the sale itself does not prevent return.
3
Apply to the Tailoring Case for a Minor Child
When a buyer cuts and sews cloth to make a garment for his minor child, he is considered to have gifted it and taken possession as guardian. Thus he becomes ḥābis and cannot claim compensation.
Cutting cloth for a minor is equivalent to delivery of a gift.
4
Distinguish the Major Child Case
For a major child, cutting alone does not transfer ownership; ownership passes only after sewing and delivery. Since sewing itself prevents return before the gift, the buyer is not ḥābis and retains the right to compensation.
The father is not authorized to take possession for a major child, so no constructive gift occurs.

عیب دیکھنے کے بعد اس سلے ہوئے کپڑے یا رنگے ہوئے کپڑے یا گھی ملے ستو کو کسی کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دیا تو بھی مشتری کو نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا اختیار ہوگا کیونکہ کپڑے یاستو میں مشتری کی ملک سے زیادتی پیدا ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کو مشتری کے فروخت کرنے سے پہلے ہی واپس کرنا منع ہو گیا ہے۔ پس مشتری فروخت کرنے کی وجہ سے مبیع کو روکنے والا نہیں ہوگا اور جس صورت میں مشتری مبیع روکنے والا نہ ہوا اور مبیع کو بائع کی طرف واپس کرنا بھی ممتنع ہو تو اس صورت میں مشتری کو نقصان عیب لینے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے ۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں مشتری کو نقصان عیب لینے کا اختیار دیا گیا ہے ۔ حاصل یہ ہوا کہ مشتری جس صورت میں حابس للمبيع ( یعنی مبیع روکنے والا) ہوگا ۔ اس صورت میں اس کو نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا۔ اور جس صورت میں حابس للمبیع نہیں ہوتا اس صورت میں اس کو نقصان عیب لینے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے۔ اسی اصول کی وجہ سے ہم نے کہا کہ اگر کسی نے کپڑا خریدا اور اپنے نابالغ بچہ کا لباس بنانے کے لئے اس کو کاٹ کر سلا لیا پھر کسی عیب پر واقف ہوا تو اس کو بائع سے نقصان عیب لینے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر بچہ بالغ تھا تو مشتری نقصان عیب لینے کا مجاز ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مشتری نے جونہی اپنے نابالغ بچہ کا لباس بنانے کے ارادے سے کپڑا کاٹا تو گویا باپ بچہ کو وہ کپڑا مہبہ کر کے سپرد کرنے والا ہو گیا اور ولی ہونے کی وجہ سے خود اس پر قبضہ کر لیا تو مشتری بہ مع التسلیم کی وجہ سے حابس للسمیع ہو گیا اور حابس السمیع کونقصان عیب رجوع کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا۔ اس لئے اس صورت میں نقصان عیب لینے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا۔ اور بچہ اگر بالغ ہے تو محض کپڑا کاٹنے سے وہ کپڑے کا مالک نہ ہوگا بلکہ کپڑا کاٹنے کے بعد جب اس کو سلا کر ولد کبیر کو سپر د کر دے گا تب ولد کبیر اس کا مالک ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ باپ اپنے والد کبیر کی طرف سے قبضہ کا مجاز نہیں ہوتا۔ پس اس کپڑے کو ولد کبیر کی طرف ہبہ کر کے سپرد کرنا ، سلنے کے بعد پایا گیا حالانکہ سلنے ہی سے اس کپڑے کو بائع کی طرف واپس کرنا منع ہو گیا پس جب ہبہ کرنے سے پہلے ہی کپڑا واپس کرنا منع ہوگیا تو والد کیر کی طرف ہبہ مع التسلیم کرنے سے مشتری حابس للمریع نہیں ہوا۔ اور جب مشتری حابس السمیع نہیں ہوا تو اس کو نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا بھی اختیار ہوگا۔
After seeing the defect, if he sold the sewn cloth or dyed cloth or oil-mixed barley to someone, even then the buyer will have the option to take compensation for the defect because due to the addition in the buyer's property from the cloth or barley, returning it before the buyer sold it became prohibited. So the buyer will not be the preventer of the sold item because of selling. And in the case where the buyer is not the preventer of the sold item and returning the sold item to the seller is also prevented, then in that case the buyer has the option to take compensation for the defect. Therefore, in this case the buyer is given the option to take compensation for the defect. The conclusion is that the buyer, in whichever case he is the preventer of the sold item, in that case he does not have the option to take compensation for the defect. And in which case he is not the preventer of the sold item, in that case he has the option to take compensation for the defect. Due to this principle, we said that if someone bought cloth and cut and sewed it to make a garment for his minor child, then became aware of a defect, he will not have the option to take compensation for the defect from the seller. And if the child was major, then the buyer will be permitted to take compensation for the defect. The proof is that as soon as the buyer cut the cloth with the intention of making a garment for his minor child, it is as if the father gifted that cloth to the child and delivered it, and being the guardian, he himself took possession of it, so the buyer became the preventer of the sold item by reason of gift with delivery, and the preventer of the sold item does not have the option to return for compensation. Therefore, in this case he will not be permitted to take compensation. And if the child is major, then merely cutting the cloth does not make him the owner of the cloth; rather, after cutting and sewing, when he delivers it to the major child, then the major child will be its owner. Because the father is not authorized to take possession on behalf of his major child. So gifting this cloth to the major child and delivering it occurred after sewing, while sewing itself made returning this cloth to the seller prohibited. Since the cloth became prohibited to return before the gift, then by the gift with delivery to the major child, the buyer did not become the preventer of the sold item. And when the buyer did not become the preventer of the sold item, he will have the option to take compensation for the defect.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi principle that a buyer who prevents the return of a defective item (ḥābis li al-mabīʿ) cannot claim compensation for the defect. The text first gives examples: if the buyer sews, dyes, or mixes the item and then sells it to another, he is not considered a preventer because the addition was made before the sale, and the sale itself does not prevent return. The key is whether the buyer's own action makes return impossible. The general rule: if the buyer is the preventer, he cannot claim compensation; if he is not, he can. The author then applies this to a cloth purchased and cut for a child: for a minor child, cutting and sewing with intent to clothe him is equivalent to gifting and taking possession on his behalf (since the father is guardian), so the father becomes the preventer and loses the right to defect compensation. For a major child, cutting alone does not transfer ownership; ownership passes only after sewing and delivery. Since sewing itself prevents return before the gift, the buyer is not the preventer and retains the right to compensation. This illustrates how the Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between actions that do and do not constitute 'prevention'.

Key Terms
حابس للمبيع (ḥābis li al-mabīʿ): The buyer who, by his own action, makes the return of the defective item to the seller impossible, thereby forfeiting his right to compensation for the defect.
نقصان عیب (naqṣ al-ʿayb): The compensation paid by the seller to the buyer for a hidden defect, equal to the difference in value between the sound and defective item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases cloth and sews it into a garment for his own use, then discovers a defect, can he claim compensation for the defect?
A) Yes, because sewing is not a prevention of return.
B) No, because sewing adds value and prevents return by the buyer's action.
C) Yes, but only if the defect is major.
D) No, because any alteration forfeits the right.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's right to defect compensation depends on whether his own action prevents return of the item; if he is a ḥābis li al-mabīʿ, he cannot claim naqṣ al-ʿayb.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Buyer's Right to Defect Compensation After Death or Freeing of the Slave
Intra-Hanafi ruling with dalil aqli (qiyas and istihsan) on defect compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where a buyer can claim defect compensation despite being unable to return the slave, and state the Hanafi ruling for death, freeing, tadbir, istilad, and freeing for a price.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave ends ownership like death, so the buyer can still claim defect compensation despite his own act — istihsan overrides qiyas.

When a purchased slave dies or is freed before the buyer discovers a defect, the buyer cannot return the slave. The key question is whether the buyer can still claim compensation for the defect. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning, which distinguishes between involuntary prevention (death) and voluntary acts (freeing) and applies istihsan to allow compensation in both cases. We also examine the intra-Hanafi dispute over freeing for a price and see how the madhhab settles on the majority view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core scenarios
The passage treats two main cases: the slave dies in the buyer's possession, or the buyer frees the slave. In both, the sold item cannot be returned, but the buyer may still seek defect compensation.
Note that tadbir and istilad are treated like freeing.
2
Understand the qiyas argument against compensation for freeing
Qiyas compares freeing to killing the slave: both are voluntary acts that prevent return, so the buyer should forfeit compensation. This is the default analogy.
The prevention of return is by the buyer's own action (bi-fi'lihi).
3
Apply istihsan to override qiyas for freeing
Istihsan grants compensation because freeing ends ownership (inhāʾ al-milk), just like death. Man is not naturally a subject of ownership; ownership in a slave is temporary until freed. Thus, freeing is treated as a legal termination, not a voluntary destruction.
The key rationale: 'al-ʿitq inhāʾ al-milk' (freeing is ending ownership).
4
Extend to tadbir and istilad
Tadbir (making the slave free after the master's death) and istilad (becoming umm walad) also make transfer impossible legally (ḥukmī), so the same rule applies: the buyer can claim compensation.
Even though the slave still exists, legal transfer is impossible.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute for freeing with a price (kitāba)
If the buyer frees the slave for a price (e.g., the buyer accepts payment for freedom), the majority (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no compensation because the buyer has held back the substitute (the price). Abu Hanifa says compensation is still allowed because ownership ends regardless.
Mufta bihi is the majority view: no compensation when freeing for a price.
6
Determine the mufta bihi position
For death, freeing without consideration, tadbir, and istilad: compensation is allowed. For freeing with consideration: compensation is not allowed (following the Sahibayn). This is the relied-upon ruling.
The istihsan position is also held by Shafi'i and Hanbalis.

صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ غلام خرید کر اس کو مد بر بنانا یا باندی خرید کر اس کو ام ولد بنانا آزاد کرنے کے حکم میں ہے۔ یعنی ان دونوں صورتوں میں بھی مشتری کو عیب پر مطلع ہونے کے بعد نقصان عیب سے رجوع کرنے کا اختیار ہے۔ کیونکہ مد بر اورام ولد کو بحکم شرع بقائے محل کے باوجود ایک ملک سے دوسرے ملک کی طرف منتقل کرنا متعذر ہے اور جب محل یعنی بیع موجود ہو لیکن اس کو بائع کی طرف واپس کرنا مستعد رہو تو مشتری کو رجوع بالنقصان کا حق ہوتا ہے اس لیے ان دونوں صورتوں میں مشتری کو نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا اختیار ہوگا اور اگر مشتری نے مال کے عوض غلام آزاد کیا اور پھر عیب پر مطلع ہوا تو مشتری کو نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا کیونکہ مشتری
He who buys a slave and frees him or he dies in his possession, then discovers a defect, he may recover the deficiency. As for death, because ownership ends by it and the prevention is legal, not by his action. As for freeing, analogically he should not recover because the prevention is by his action, so it is like killing. By istihsan, he recovers because freeing ends ownership: man was not originally created as a subject of ownership, rather ownership is established in him temporarily until freeing, so it is an ending, thus similar to death. And because a thing is confirmed by its ending, so it is as if ownership remains and return is impossible. Tadbir and istilad are like it because impossibility of transfer with the subject remaining is a legal matter. If he freed him for a price, he recovers nothing because he has held back its substitute, and the substitute is like holding back the substituted. From Abu Hanifa: he recovers because ending ownership, even if for a consideration. Translation: al-Quduri said: And whoever buys a slave and frees him, or the slave dies in his presence, then he becomes aware of a defect, the buyer can recover the deficiency. As for death, because ownership is terminated by it and the prevention of return is non-volitional, not by his action. As for freeing, the analogy is that he should not recover because prevention is by his action. But istihsan says he recovers because freeing ends ownership. Explanation of the ruling: if someone buys a slave and frees him, or the slave dies in the buyer's possession, then the buyer discovers a defect that existed with the seller, in this case the buyer can take the deficiency from his seller. In the case of death, because death brings ownership to its end, and a thing upon reaching its end becomes confirmed and established. So it is as if ownership exists but due to death, returning the slave (the sold item) is impossible. And the buyer had no part in the prevention. When return is impossible due to a non-volitional cause, the buyer has the right to take the deficiency for the old defect. Therefore, in the case of death, the buyer can take the deficiency. In the case of freeing, qiyas requires that the buyer not be given the right to take deficiency because the prevention of return involves the buyer's own act. So the buyer is a preventer (hābis). And a preventer of the sale does not have the right to take deficiency. So in this case, the buyer should not have the right. This is like killing a slave and then discovering a defect: no right to deficiency. But istihsan dictates that the buyer does have the right to take deficiency, and this is also the view of Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad. The reason for istihsan is that freeing brings ownership to an end. Because in original creation, man was not made a subject of ownership; rather, ownership in a slave is established for a limited time until freedom. So freeing a slave is like bringing his ownership to an end, and when a thing reaches its end, it becomes complete. For this reason, the freed slave's wala belongs to the manumitter, and wala is among the effects of ownership. So it will be said that the buyer's ownership remains, but due to freeing, returning the slave is impossible. And when returning the sold item to the seller becomes impossible, the buyer has the right to take deficiency, as in the case of death. Therefore, even in the case of freeing, the buyer can take deficiency from the seller. The author of al-Hidayah said: Making a slave mudabbar or a female slave umm walad is like freeing. In both cases, the buyer has the right to seek deficiency after discovering the defect. Because legally, despite the existence of the subject, transfer from one ownership to another is impossible for mudabbar and umm walad. When the subject (the sale) exists but its return to the seller is impossible, the buyer has the right to recourse by deficiency. Therefore, in these two cases, the buyer can take deficiency. And if the buyer freed the slave for a price and then discovered a defect, the buyer does not have the right to take deficiency because the buyer
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from al-Hidayah presents the Hanafi ruling on defect compensation when the sold slave dies or is freed. The matn states that in both cases the buyer can recover the deficiency. The commentary explains the reasoning: For death, the prevention of return is not the buyer's fault; thus he retains the right. For freeing, qiyas would deny the right because the buyer's own act prevents return (like killing), but istihsan grants it because freeing ends ownership (man was not created for ownership, so ownership in a slave is temporary). Thus freeing is like death: ownership ends, and the buyer is not considered a 'preventer'. The same applies to tadbir (declaring a slave free after death) and istilad (begetting a child from a female slave), as they make transfer impossible legally despite the slave still existing. For freeing for a price (kitāba), the majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the buyer cannot claim compensation because he has retained the price (the substitute), which is like retaining the slave. However, a narration from Abu Hanifa states that he can, because even with consideration, ownership ends. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the majority opinion. This passage also notes that the istihsan position is shared by Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad.

Key Terms
اعتاق (iʿtāq): Freeing a slave; treated as ending ownership (inhāʾ al-milk), similar to death for defect compensation.
تدبير (tadbīr): A contract making a slave free upon master's death; treated like iʿtāq for defect compensation.
استيلاد (istīlād): Female slave bearing her master's child, becoming umm walad; transfer impossible legally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a slave, frees him immediately, and then discovers a hidden defect. According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), can the buyer claim compensation for the defect?
A) Yes, because freeing ends ownership and istihsan allows compensation.
B) No, because freeing is a voluntary act that prevents return like killing.
C) Yes, but only if the slave was freed without any payment.
D) No, because the defect should have been discovered before freeing.

Key Takeaway
A buyer can claim defect compensation even if the slave dies or is freed, because the prevention of return is not considered a voluntary bar; freeing for a price is an exception where compensation is denied.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Buyer Frees Slave Then Discovers Defect: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Explanation of qiyas vs istihsan on buyer freeing slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on claiming compensation for a defect after freeing a slave, explain the qiyas/istihsan conflict, and apply the same ruling to mudabbar and umm walad.

Core Idea
Freeing perfects ownership like death; compensation for defect is allowed despite the buyer's voluntary act.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah presents a classic case of istihsan overriding qiyas. When a buyer frees a slave and then discovers a pre-existing defect, strict analogy (qiyas) would deny compensation because the buyer's own voluntary act prevented return. However, the Hanafi school adopts istihsan, ruling that freeing completes ownership like death, so compensation is due. The same reasoning extends to making a slave a mudabbar or umm walad. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the key distinction between harmful acts like killing and praiseworthy acts like emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and legal question
The buyer frees a slave after purchase, then discovers a defect that existed with the seller. Can the buyer claim compensation (arsh al-ayb) from the seller? The passage also considers the slave's death, making a mudabbar, and making an umm walad.
The base case is death: involuntary impossibility of return leads to compensation.
2
Trace the qiyas argument
Qiyas analogizes freeing to killing: both are voluntary acts that prevent return. Since a buyer who kills the slave cannot claim compensation, the same should apply to freeing. The buyer becomes a habis (preventer) and loses the right.
The illa (effective cause) is 'voluntary act causing impossibility of return'.
3
Identify the istihsan counterargument
Istihsan holds that freeing is not destruction but completion of ownership. Just as death perfects ownership involuntarily, freeing perfects ownership voluntarily. The wala' (patronage) that passes to the freed slave is evidence that ownership effects remain. Thus, compensation is allowed.
Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad also hold this view.
4
Extend to mudabbar and umm walad
The passage explicitly extends the same ruling to making a slave a mudabbar (to be freed upon death) or a female slave an umm walad (mother of the owner's child). These acts also make return impossible while perfecting ownership.
By Islamic law, a mudabbar and umm walad cannot be sold or transferred.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon Hanafi fatwa is that the buyer can claim compensation. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the buyer bears the loss or recovers it from the seller.
This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, as recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Explanation of the case: If someone bought a slave and freed him, or the slave died after coming to the buyer, then the buyer becomes aware of a defect that existed with the seller, then in this case the buyer can claim compensation for the defect from his seller. In the case of the slave's death, it is because death causes ownership to reach its limit, and when something reaches its limit it becomes fixed and established. So it is as if ownership exists, but due to the decree of death, i.e., an involuntary matter, returning the slave, which is the sold object, has become impossible. And the buyer's action has no involvement in the impossibility of return. And when the return of the sold object becomes impossible due to an involuntary matter, the buyer has the right to take back the compensation for the defect due to the old defect. Therefore, in the case of the slave's death, the buyer will have the right to take back the compensation for the defect. And in the case of buying and freeing the slave, qiyas requires that the buyer not be given the right to take compensation for the defect, because in this case the impossibility of returning the slave involves the buyer's own action. Thus the buyer has prevented the return of the sold object. And one who prevents the return of a sound object does not have the right to take back compensation for the defect. Therefore, in this case the buyer will not have the right to take compensation for the defect. And this is like someone who bought a slave and killed him, then became aware of the defect: in that case the buyer has no right to take compensation for the defect. Similarly, in the case of freeing, the buyer will not have the right to take compensation for the defect. And istihsan requires that the buyer has the right to take back compensation for the defect; this is the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad. The reason for istihsan is that freeing brings ownership to its limit. Because according to the original creation, a human is not created as a subject of ownership, but rather for a slave, ownership is established for a limited time, i.e., at the time of freeing. So freeing a slave is like bringing his ownership to its limit, and when something reaches its limit, it becomes complete. This is why the freed slave's wala' (patronage) goes to the emancipator, and wala' is among the effects of ownership. Thus it will be said: the buyer's ownership remains, but due to freeing, returning the slave has become impossible. And when returning the sold object to the seller becomes impossible, the buyer has the right to take back compensation for the defect, as in the case of the slave's death. Therefore, in the case of freeing as well, the buyer will have the right to take compensation for the defect from the seller. The author of al-Hidayah said: Making a slave a mudabbar (one who is to be freed upon the owner's death) or a female slave an umm walad (mother of the owner's child) is in the same ruling as freeing. That is, in both these cases, after becoming aware of the defect, the buyer has the right to claim compensation for the defect. Because a mudabbar and an umm walad, by Islamic law, even with the existence of the subject, their transfer from one ownership to another is impossible. And when the subject, i.e., the sale, exists but its return to the seller is impossible, the buyer has the right to claim compensation for the defect. Therefore, in both these cases, the buyer will have the right to take back compensation for the defect.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah explains the ruling when a buyer frees a slave or makes her an umm walad, then discovers a pre-existing defect. The author first establishes the basic principle using the case of the slave's death as a benchmark: death is involuntary, so ownership is complete and return is impossible, yet the buyer still gets compensation. He then contrasts the case of freeing, where the buyer's own act prevents return. According to strict analogy (qiyas), since the buyer voluntarily prevented return, he should not be able to claim compensation, just as if he had killed the slave. However, the Hanafi school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) here, following Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad, allowing compensation. The reasoning is that freeing is not a harmful act like killing; rather, it perfects ownership, similar to death. Since the slave is no longer returnable, the buyer can claim compensation for the defect. The passage then extends the same ruling to tadbir (making a mudabbar) and istilad (making an umm walad), as these also prevent return while perfecting ownership. Thus, the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is that the buyer can recover the difference in value due to the defect.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference overriding qiyas based on a stronger consideration, here the completion of ownership and removal of the slave from being a commodity.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical deduction; here the analogy of freeing to killing is rejected in favor of istihsan.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who will be freed upon the owner's death; he cannot be sold or transferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer frees a slave and then discovers a pre-existing defect, what is the Hanafi ruling regarding claiming compensation?
A) The buyer cannot claim any compensation because his own act prevented return.
B) The buyer can claim compensation because freeing perfects ownership like death.
C) The buyer can claim compensation only if the slave was a mudabbar.
D) The buyer must return the slave to get compensation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to allow compensation when a buyer frees a slave before discovering a defect, because freeing perfects ownership like death.

Freeing a Slave for Consideration: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Defect Compensation
Ruling on buyer's compensation for defect after freeing slave for payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer frees a slave in exchange for payment and then discovers a defect, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute including its basis and the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Accepting payment makes the buyer a habis, forfeiting the right to compensation for defects.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a buyer purchases a slave, then frees him in exchange for a payment from the slave (similar to kitaba). Later, a pre-existing defect is discovered. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether the buyer can claim compensation. The predominant view forbids it, while a minority view allows it. We will analyze the reasoning behind each position and identify the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The buyer owns the slave after purchase, then frees him for a payment from the slave. After freeing, a defect is found that existed before the sale.
This is different from freeing without payment (e.g., as a gift).
2
State the predominant Hanafi ruling and its basis
The predominant view (zahir al-riwayah from Abu Hanifa) says the buyer cannot claim compensation. The reason is that by taking payment, the buyer has effectively prevented the return of the slave (habis lil-mabī'), and a habis loses the right to arsh al-ayb.
Qiyas: keeping the badal (substitute) is like keeping the mabī' itself.
3
State the minority view and its basis
Another narration from Abu Hanifa, supported by Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad, allows compensation. They argue that freeing, whether for payment or not, perfects ownership just like death, and compensation is allowed upon death.
They focus on the completion of ownership, not on the receipt of payment.
4
Compare the two lines of reasoning
The predominant view emphasizes the buyer's action of accepting payment, which makes him a habis. The minority view focuses on the effect of freeing (ownership ends), similar to death. The key difference is whether receiving payment creates a new bar (habis) or not.
The dispute is about the legal characterization of the buyer's act.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and the key factor
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the predominant view: no compensation. The decisive factor is the receipt of payment, which distinguishes this case from freeing without payment.
The commentator of al-Hidayah gives weight to this view as the fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he freed the slave for payment, then he cannot take anything back because he has kept back the substitute for the sold object, and keeping back the substitute is like keeping back the substitute for the substitute. And from Abu Hanifa there is a narration that he can go back, because freeing for payment sometimes completes ownership even if it is in exchange. And if the buyer frees the slave in exchange for payment, then he becomes aware of the defect, then the buyer will not have the right to take back compensation for the defect, because the buyer has kept back the substitute for the slave, and keeping back the substitute is like keeping back the substitute for the substitute. So it is as if the buyer has prevented the sold object. And in the case of being a preventer of the sold object, the buyer does not have the right to claim compensation for the defect. Thus in this case as well, the buyer will not have the right to take back compensation for the defect. There is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that in this case too, the buyer can take back compensation for the defect. This is also the saying of Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad also hold this view, because freeing a slave, whether for consideration or without, brings ownership to its limit. So this too becomes like death, and in the case of the slave's death, the buyer has the right to claim compensation. Therefore, in the case of freeing for payment, he also will have the right to claim compensation.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage deals with a sub-case: the buyer frees the slave in exchange for a payment (e.g., the slave pays for his freedom, similar to kitaba). Here, the majority Hanafi view (and the one attributed to Abu Hanifa in the well-known narration) is that the buyer cannot claim compensation for a pre-existing defect. The reason is that by taking the payment, the buyer has effectively detained the substitute price; this is like detaining the sold object itself. The buyer is therefore a habis (preventer), and a habis cannot claim compensation. However, there is another narration from Abu Hanifa, also adopted by Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad, that the buyer can claim compensation. They argue that freeing, whether for payment or not, perfects ownership just like death, so the impossibility of return is similar to death, and compensation should be allowed. The predominant fatwa in the Hanafi school follows the first view (no compensation), as it is given more weight in the Hidayah.

Key Terms
حابس للمبيع (habis lil-mabī'): One who prevents the return of the sold object by his own action, thereby forfeiting the right to claim for defects.
بدل (badal): Payment or substitute; here the payment received from the slave for his freedom is considered a substitute for the slave (the mabī'), so retaining it is like retaining the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a buyer frees a slave in exchange for a payment and then discovers a pre-existing defect, what is the predominant ruling?
A) The buyer can claim compensation because freeing perfects ownership.
B) The buyer cannot claim compensation because he has received payment and is thus a habis.
C) The buyer can claim compensation only if the payment was less than the slave's value.
D) The buyer must return the payment to the slave to claim compensation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes freeing for payment from freeing for free: payment makes the buyer a habis, barring defect compensation.

Buyer Kills the Slave: Compensation After Defect Discovery
Killing of slave by buyer – internal Hanafi dispute on compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf) on compensation when a buyer kills a purchased slave and then discovers a defect, and explain the reasoning for each position.

Core Idea
Killing a slave is a guaranteed act; even ownership doesn't remove the tort, so Abu Hanifa denies compensation.

If a buyer kills the slave he just purchased and later discovers a hidden defect, does he still have a right to claim compensation? The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer's destructive act forfeits any claim, while Abu Yusuf considers the killing akin to natural death due to ownership. This lesson walks through the arguments and the authority of the narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The buyer kills the slave, then discovers a defect. The dispute is whether he can claim compensation for the defect.
The act of killing is intentional but done after ownership transferred.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its authority
Abu Hanifa says: no compensation. This is the zahir al-riwayah (apparent narration).
Zahir al-riwayah is the primary source for fatwa in the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
Killing is a guaranteed act (madmin). Ownership drops the worldly guarantee for the tort, but the act itself is still destructive. Hence the buyer cannot claim; he is like one who received benefit in exchange for the object.
Compare with freeing: freeing does not necessitate guarantee, so it's different.
4
State Abu Yusuf's position and dalil
Abu Yusuf says the buyer gets compensation because killing one's own slave carries no worldly penalty, so it resembles death by natural causes.
This position is a weaker narration (nawadir).
5
Determine the strength of each view
The text establishes Abu Hanifa's view as zahir al-riwayah, making it the relied-upon position for fatwa. Abu Yusuf's view is recorded but not authoritative.
Tarjih is based on the chain of transmission and consistency with usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان قتل المشترى العبد او كان طعاما فاكله لم يرجع بشي عند ابي حنيفة اما القتل فالمذكور ظاهر الرواية وعن ابي يوسف انه يرجع لان قتل المولى عبده لا يتعلق به حکم دنیا وی فصار كالموت حتف انفه فيكون انهاء ووجه الظاهر ان القتل لايوجد الامضمونا وانما يسقط الضمان ههنا باعتبار الملك فيصير كالمستفيد به عوضا بخلاف الاعتاق لانه لا يوجب الضمان لا محالة كاعتاق المعسر عبدا مشتر کا واما الاكل فعلى الخلاف عندهما يرجع وعنده لا يرجع استحسانا وعلى هذا الخلاف اذالبس الثوب حتى تخرق لهما انه صنع فى المبيع ما يقصد بشرائه ويعتا دفعله فيه فاشبه الاعتاق وله انه تعذر الرد بفعل مضمون منه فى المبيع فاشبه البيع والقتل ولا معتبر بكونه مقصودا الاترى ان البيع مما يقصد بالشراء ثم هو يمنع الرجوع فان اكل بعض الطعام ثم علم بالعيب فكذا الجواب عند ابي حنيفة لأن الطعام كشيء واحد فصا ركبيع البعض وعنهما انه يرجع بنقصان العيب فى الكل وعنهما انه يرد مابقى لانه لا يضره التبعيض
If the buyer kills the slave, or if it was food and he ate it, he does not get anything back according to Abu Hanifa. As for killing, what is mentioned is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah). From Abu Yusuf it is narrated that he does get back, because the master's killing of his slave carries no worldly legal consequence, so it becomes like death by natural causes, thus it is a completion. The apparent proof is that killing does not occur except as a guarantee (daman), and here the guarantee is dropped because of ownership, so he becomes like one who benefits from a compensation, unlike freeing because it does not necessitate guarantee, like an insolvent partner freeing a jointly owned slave. As for eating, there is a difference: according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he gets back; according to him (Abu Hanifa), he does not get back by istihsan. And on this same difference is the case when he wore the garment until it tore: for them (the two), he made in the sold object what is intended by purchasing it and he customarily does such an act, so it resembles freeing; for him (Abu Hanifa), the return has become impossible due to an act from him that is guaranteed in the sold object, so it resembles selling and killing, and there is no consideration for it being intended. Do you not see that selling is intended by purchasing, yet it prevents return? If he ate part of the food then learned of the defect, similarly the answer is with Abu Hanifa because the food is like one thing, so it becomes like selling part. And from them (the two) that he gets back compensation for the defect in the whole; and from them (the two another narration) that he returns what remains because partiality does not harm him.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is the Arabic matn from al-Hidayah, presented with translation and commentary in Urdu. It covers multiple scenarios where the buyer's action destroys or consumes the object before discovering a defect. The base ruling: according to Abu Hanifa (zahir al-riwayah), if the buyer kills the slave, he gets no compensation. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that killing one's own slave has no worldly penalty, so it is like death by natural causes, which would allow compensation. But Abu Hanifa's view is that killing is a tortious act (it necessitates guarantee), even if the guarantee is dropped due to ownership, so the buyer becomes like one who has obtained benefit (the slave) in exchange for something, thus no compensation. For eating food, there is a difference: Abu Hanifa says no compensation by istihsan (since the buyer consumed it, he is like a habis), while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow compensation. Similarly, if the buyer wears a garment until it tears: the two say it resembles freeing, so compensation allowed; Abu Hanifa says it resembles sale or killing because the act is guaranteed, so no compensation. If the buyer eats only part of the food, then discovers defect: Abu Hanifa says no compensation for the whole because the food is considered one unit, and consuming part is like selling part (which prevents return). The two have two narrations: one that he gets compensation for the defect in the whole, another that he returns the remaining part. The passage thus illustrates the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on these issues, with Abu Hanifa generally taking the stricter view that the buyer's voluntary destructive act forfeits compensation.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and transmitted ruling from Abu Hanifa through his students in the six books of Imam Muhammad.
مضمون (madmin): An act that is tortious or necessitates guarantee (daman); here used for the buyer's destructive acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa (zahir al-riwayah), if a buyer kills the slave he purchased and then discovers a pre-existing defect, what is the ruling?
A) The buyer can claim compensation because the slave died anyway.
B) The buyer cannot claim compensation because killing is a guaranteed act.
C) The buyer can claim compensation if he had a good reason.
D) The buyer must return the slave's body to the seller.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the buyer's intentional destruction of the object (killing) forfeits his right to claim defects, even if the act is legally permissible due to ownership.

Buyer Consumes or Wears Out the Object: Compensation After Defect Discovery
Eating food and wearing garment – internal Hanafi dispute on compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the rulings of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when a buyer eats food or wears out a garment and then discovers a defect, and apply the key distinction between 'guaranteed act' and 'intended use'.

Core Idea
Consuming or wearing out the object is a guaranteed act for Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn see it as intended use; the difference determines compensation.

When a buyer eats the purchased food or wears a garment until it tears, then discovers a defect, the school is divided. Abu Hanifa holds that any act that makes return impossible (like eating or wearing to destruction) is a guaranteed act barring compensation. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider these acts as the intended use of the object, like freeing a slave, and thus allow compensation. This lesson analyzes the arguments for each scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
Two cases: (1) buyer eats the food (whole or part); (2) buyer wears the garment until it tears.
Both involve voluntary consumption or destruction of the object.
2
State Abu Hanifa's general ruling
Abu Hanifa says no compensation in both cases (whole food, torn garment) and even for partial eating of food. He uses istihsan to deny compensation.
The reasoning: the buyer's act is madmun (guaranteed), making return impossible.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The two say compensation is allowed. For eating, the buyer can claim back the price minus the value of what he ate. For tearing, they analogize to freeing (which does not bar claim).
They consider these acts as intended use, not destructive in the legal sense.
4
Analyze the key distinction
The disagreement hinges on whether the act is 'madmin' (tortious) or 'intended use'. Abu Hanifa emphasizes the effect (return impossible); the Sahibayn emphasize the intention (buyer used as normal).
Also note: eating part of the food: Abu Hanifa treats whole food as one unit; Sahibayn have two narrations.
5
Summarize the practical outcome
Under Abu Hanifa, buyer bears the loss; under the Sahibayn, buyer gets compensation for the defect (and may return remaining part in one narration).
The fatwa is generally based on Abu Hanifa's caution, but the ikhtilaf remains.

Commentary
This passage is the Arabic matn from al-Hidayah, presented with translation and commentary in Urdu. It covers multiple scenarios where the buyer's action destroys or consumes the object before discovering a defect. The base ruling: according to Abu Hanifa (zahir al-riwayah), if the buyer kills the slave, he gets no compensation. Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference; Abu Hanifa uses istihsan to deny compensation in eating cases, meaning he prefers the ruling that prevents loss to the seller.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the buyer's consumption or substantial use of the object before discovering a defect is considered a guaranteed act that bars compensation, even if the use was normal.
