Sale of Walnuts with Internal Defect: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on sale validity when nuts found rotten inside after breaking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the sale of walnuts found rotten after breaking and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument.

Core Idea
Breaking the sold item creates a new defect; the buyer cannot return it but can claim compensation.

This lesson explores a classical Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over the sale of walnuts found rotten after the buyer breaks them open. You will learn why Hanafis treat the buyer's act of breaking as a new defect that bars return, and how the Shafi'i school reaches a different conclusion. The discussion reveals deeper principles about ownership, permission, and the concept of 'new defect' in sales law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
Determine the exact question disputed: Can a buyer who breaks open walnuts and finds them rotten return the broken fruit for a full refund?
The dispute hinges on whether the buyer's act of breaking is considered a new defect or not.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi school holds that the buyer cannot return because breaking is a new defect. Their proof is the analogy of cutting cloth after purchase: the buyer's alteration bars return, and only compensation can be claimed.
The analogy is based on the principle that the buyer's own action creates the new defect, not the seller's permission.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the buyer can return because the breaking is attributed to the seller who gave permission. The maxim is: 'the one who gives power over an action is like the doer'.
Thus, in Shafi'i view, no new defect is created by the buyer.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis distinguish that the seller gave power to break in the buyer's ownership, not his own. After the sale, the seller has no authority over the buyer. Therefore, the act is solely the buyer's, analogous to cutting cloth after purchase.
The ownership distinction is key: the seller cannot be credited with the act because the item no longer belongs to him.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the exception
Practical consequence: Hanafi buyer gets compensation only, Shafi'i buyer gets full refund. Additionally, Hanafis carve out an exception for small quantities (customarily negligible) where the sale is valid by istihsan and no claim is allowed.
If a large quantity is rotten, the sale becomes fasid because it combines mal and ghayr mal in one contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

سے واپس لے گا کیونکہ خراب اخروٹ قیمت نہ ہونے کی وجہ غیر مال ہے اور جو اچھے ہیں خراب نہیں ہیں وہ مال ہیں تو گویا بائع نے مال اور غیر مال کو مقدار واحد میں جمع کر دیا ہے اور مال اور غیر مال کو عقد واحد میں جمع کرنا مفسد بیع ہے اس لیے اس صورت میں پوری بیچ کے اندر بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی۔ جیسا کہ آزاد اور غلام کوعقد واحد میں جمع کرنا مفسد بیج ہے۔
Book of Sales: 130. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary on Hidayah, volume eighth: The sale of walnuts should be valid for the shells, and the buyer should take back the price of the kernels from the seller. This is the madhhab of some mashayikh. The author of Hidayah responded: The quality of walnut shells is not considered, because before breaking, the value of walnuts is based on the kernel, not the shell. Since the kernel is spoiled and is non-beneficial, the subject of the sale is absent, so the sale is void. When the sale is void, the buyer takes back his full price and returns the shells to the seller. If the fruit, despite being spoiled, is still usable, then the buyer does not have the option to return the fruit and take back the full price, unless the seller agrees. The proof is that the buyer's breaking of walnuts etc. is a new defect. When a new defect occurs in the sold item in the buyer's possession, he does not have the option to return it due to an old defect. Therefore, the buyer cannot return the sold item; he can only take compensation for the defect from the seller. In this way, harm is removed as much as possible from both seller and buyer: neither does the seller suffer loss nor the buyer. Imam Shafi'i's madhhab: If the sold item despite being spoiled is still usable, the buyer can return it and take back the full price. Because the buyer broke the walnuts etc. by the power given by the seller, and the one who gives power over an action is like the doer. So it is as if the seller himself broke those fruits. When the action of breaking is attributed to the seller, no new defect occurred with the buyer. When no new defect occurred, the buyer has full option of defect to return the sale. Our response: The power to break the fruit (the sold item) undoubtedly came to the buyer from the seller, but he gave the power to break in the buyer's ownership, not in his own, because after the contract of sale, the seller's ownership no longer remains. When the seller's ownership is gone, giving the power to break also occurs in the buyer's ownership, not the seller's. Giving the buyer the power to break in his own ownership is completely meaningless, because the seller has no right or authority over the buyer. So this is like a person who buys cloth and cuts it, then finds it defective: the buyer can take compensation for the defect, even though the power to cut came from the seller. Similarly, in the mentioned case, the buyer can take compensation for the defect, but he cannot return the broken fruit and take the full price. If some walnuts turn out to be rotten, there are two cases: the amount of rot is small or large. Small amount is what walnuts are customarily not free from, like one or two walnuts out of a hundred. If more than that are rotten, the amount is large. In the case of a small amount, by istihsan the sale is valid and the buyer has no right to dispute with the seller. If the amount is large, the sale is not valid, neither for the rotten ones nor for the good ones, and the buyer will take back the full price from the seller. Because rotten walnuts, having no value, are non-property, while the good ones are property. So the seller has combined property and non-property in one measure, and combining property and non-property in one contract invalidates the sale. Thus, in this case, the entire sale becomes invalid. Just as combining a free person and a slave in one contract invalidates the sale.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the sale of walnuts (or similar fruits) that are found to be rotten inside after the buyer breaks them open. The Hanafi position, as expounded by the author of Al-Hidayah, is that the sale of walnut shells is not considered; only the kernel has value. If the kernel is rotten, the sale is void for that walnut because the subject matter (the kernel) is absent. However, if after breaking, the nut is still usable, the buyer cannot return the broken fruit because breaking constitutes a new defect created by the buyer. Instead, he can only claim compensation from the seller for the original defect. The Shafi'i position holds that the buyer can return the fruit and get a full refund, because the breaking is attributed to the seller who gave the buyer permission to break. The Hanafi response distinguishes that the seller gave permission to break in the buyer's ownership, not his own, and after the sale, the seller has no authority over the buyer. Therefore, the act of breaking is solely the buyer's action, creating a new defect. The passage also discusses scenarios where a small or large quantity of walnuts is rotten. In small quantities (customarily negligible), the sale is considered valid by istihsan. In large quantities, the sale is invalid because it combines property (good nuts) and non-property (rotten nuts) in one contract, analogous to combining a free person and a slave in one sale.

Key Terms
عين المبيع (ayn al-mabīʿ): The specific sold item (the walnut kernels) that must exist for the sale to be valid.
غير مشفع به (ghayr mutashaffaʿ bihi): An item that cannot be used for its intended purpose; here, rotten kernel is non-beneficial.
عارض جديد (ʿāriḍ jadīd): A new defect introduced by the buyer after possession.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; here, allows the sale to be valid when the quantity of rotten nuts is small.
مال وغير مال (māl wa ghayr māl): Property and non-property; rotten walnuts have no value and are non-property, good walnuts are property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi madhhab, if a buyer breaks open walnuts and finds them rotten inside but the pieces are still usable, what is his recourse?
A) He can return the broken walnuts to the seller for a full refund.
B) He must keep the walnuts and has no claim against the seller.
C) He can only claim compensation (arsh) from the seller for the defect, not return the nuts.
D) He can return the walnuts only if he did not break them himself.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's alteration of the sold item creates a new defect that bars return, allowing only compensation; this is a central principle in Hanafi sales law.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Return of Slave After Subsequent Sale Due to Defect
Ruling on whether first buyer can return slave to original seller after second buyer returns it due to defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a first buyer can return a slave to the original seller after the second buyer returns it due to a defect, and explain the distinction from the agent-for-sale case.

Core Idea
A judge's retroactive rescission of the second sale restores the first buyer's ownership, enabling return to his seller.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific sale scenario: a person sells a slave, the buyer resells it, and then the second buyer returns the slave to the first buyer due to a defect. The key question is whether the first buyer can then return the slave to the original seller. The ruling hinges on the nature of the return — if it was by a judge's judgment, the second sale is rescinded retroactively, allowing the first buyer to act as owner and return to his seller. The text also distinguishes this from a sale by an agent, where returns go to the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Read the passage to grasp the three-level chain: original seller → first buyer → second buyer. The second buyer returns the slave due to a defect. The ruling: the first buyer can return the slave to the original seller if the return from the second buyer was based on a judge's judgment (admission, evidence, or refusal of oath).
The return must be by qada (judgment), not voluntary.
2
Understand the legal basis: faskh min al-asl
The judge's order rescinds the second sale from its inception, deeming it as if it never happened. This retroactive rescission restores the first buyer's full ownership, enabling him to exercise his right of return against the original seller.
This is a key Hanafi principle: rescission (faskh) can be ex tunc (from the beginning).
3
Compare with the agent-for-sale case
The text contrasts this scenario with an agent (wakil bil-bay') who sells and the buyer returns the item due to a defect. In that case, the return goes to the principal, not the agent. The difference is the number of sales: one in the agent case, two here. Rescinding the second sale does not automatically rescind the first.
This distinction prevents confusion between agency and resale.
4
Analyze the three judicial grounds
The Urdu commentary explains three ways the judge's decision can be made: (1) the first buyer's admission of the defect is proven by witnesses, (2) the defect itself is proven by witnesses, (3) the first buyer refuses to take an oath, so the defect is considered proven. All three satisfy the condition for the first buyer to return.
Each ground ensures the defect is legally established.

Source Text

قال ومن باع عبداقباعه المشترى ثم رد عليه بعيب فان قبل بقضاء القاضي باقرار وببيئة اوباباء يمين له ان يرده على بائعه لانه فسخ من الاصل فجعل البيع كان لم يكن غاية الأمرانه انكر قيام العيب لكنه صار مكذبا شرعا بالقضاء ومعنى القضاء بالاقرارانه انكر الاقرار فاثبت بالبينة وهذا ابخلاف الوكيل بالبيع اذا رد عليه بعيب بالبينة حيث يكون رد اعلى الموكل لان البيع هناك واحد والموجود ههنا بيعان فبفسخ الثاني لا ينفسخ الأول ترجمہ کہا کہ ایک شخص نے غلام فروخت کیا پھر اس کو مشتری نے فروخت کیا پھر مشتری کی جانب عیب کیوجہ سے واپس کر دیا گیا اپس اگر مشتری اول نے اس کو قاضی کے حکم سے قبول کیا ہے (خواہ) اقرار سے یا گواہی سے یا قسم کے انکار سے تو مشتری اول اس کو اپنے بائع پر واپس کر سکتا ہے اس لیے کہ یہ اصل بیج کا ضح ہے پس پین ثانی کو ایسا قرار دیا گیا گویا واقع ہی نہیں ہوئی تھی۔ زیادہ سے زیادہ یہ کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ اس نے عیب موجود ہونے کا انکار کیا ہے لیکن شرع نے بحکم قضا اس کو جھٹلایا اور قضاء بالا قرار کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ مشتری اول نے عیب کا اقرار کرنے سے انکار کیا۔ پس گواہی کے ذریعہ اس کو ثابت کیا گیا۔ اور یہ اس کے برخلاف ہے کہ جب وکیل بالبیع پر میچ گواہی کے ذریعہ عیب ثابت کر کے واپس کی گئی تو یہ واپسی موکل پر ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ وکیل کی صورت میں ایک بیج ہے اور یہاں دو بیع میں تو دوسری بیچ صح ہونے سے پہلی بیع فسخ نہ ہوگی۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے اپنا غلام فروخت کیا۔ پھر مشتری نے غلام پر قبضہ کر کے دوسرے کسی کو فروخت کیا پھر مشتری ثانی نے غلام کے اندر موجود عیب کی وجہ سے اس کو مشتری اول کی طرف واپس کر دیا ۔ مگر مشتری اول نے اس کو بحکم قاضی قبول کیا ہے۔ قاضی کے فیصلہ کی تین بنیاد میں ہو سکتی ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ مشتری ثانی نے دعوی کیا کہ مشتری اول بیچ کے اندر عیب کا اقرار کر چکا ہے تو مشتری اول نے عیب کے اقرار کرنے کا انکار کر دیا۔ پس مشتری ثانی نے مدعی ہونے کی حیثیت سے مشتری اول کے اقرار عیب پر گواہ پیش کر دیئے تو گواہوں کی گواہی سے مشتری اول کا اقرار عیب ثابت ہو جائے گا اور جب عیب موجود ہونے کا مشتری اول کا اقرار کرنا ثابت ہو گیا تو اس اقرار کی بنیاد پر قاضی مشتری اول کی طرف غلام مبیع واپس کرنے کا حکم دے گا۔ مصنف کے قول اقرار سے یہی مراد ہے۔ دوسری بنیاد یہ ہے کہ مشتری ثانی دعوی کرتا ہے کہ غلام جو خریدا گیا ہے اس میں مشتری اول کے پاس عیب تھا اور مشتری اول سرے سے عیب کا انکار کرتا ہے پس مشتری ثانی نے قاضی کی عدالت میں ثبوت عیب پر گواہ پیش کر دئیے قاضی نے اس عیب کی وجہ سے مشتری اول کی طرف غلام واپس کرنے کا حکم دیا۔ مصنف کے قول وبینہ سے یہی مراد ہے تیسری بنیاد یہ ہے کہ مذکورہ صورت میں مشتری ثانی ثبوت عیب پر گواہ پیش کرنے سے عاجز آ گیا۔ قاضی نے شریعت کے مطابق مشتری اول سے قسم کا مطالبہ کیا مشتری اول نے قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا ۔ پس مشترکی اول کے قسم سے انکار کر دینے کی وجہ سے عیب کو ثابت مان کر غلام مشتری اول کی طرف واپس کرنے کا حکم دے دیا۔ مصنف کے قول او باباء یمین سے یہی مراد ہے۔ بہر حال ان تینوں صورتوں میں سے جس صورت میں بھی مشتری اول کی طرف غلام واپس کیا گیا ہو۔ مشتری اول کو یہ حق ہے کہ وہ اس غلام کو اپنے بائع کی طرف واپس کر دے یعنی جس سے مشتری اول نے خریدا ہے
He said: Whoever sells a slave, then the buyer sells him, then the slave is returned to him [first buyer] due to a defect, if he accepted him by the judgment of the judge – whether by admission, evidence, or refusal of oath – then he has the right to return him to his seller. Because this is a rescission from the origin, so the [first] sale is deemed as if it never occurred. The most that can be said is that he denied the existence of the defect, but he is legally contradicted by the judgment. The meaning of 'judgment by admission' is that he denied the admission, so it was proven by evidence. And this is unlike the agent for sale: when [the item] is returned to him due to a defect by evidence, then the return is upon the principal. Because there is one sale there, while here there are two sales. By rescinding the second, the first is not rescinded. Translation: He said: A person sold a slave, then the buyer sold him, then from the buyer's side he was returned due to a defect. So if the first buyer accepted him by the judge's order – whether by admission, evidence, or refusal of oath – then the first buyer can return him to his own seller. Because this is a rescission of the original sale, so the second sale is considered as if it never happened. At most, it can be said that he denied the existence of the defect, but the law contradicted him by the judgment. And the meaning of 'judgment by admission' is that the first buyer denied making an admission, so it was proven by evidence. And this is opposite to the case of the agent for sale: when the item is returned to him due to a defect by evidence, then the return is upon the principal. Because in the agent's case, there is one sale, while here there are two sales. By rescinding the second, the first is not rescinded. Elaboration of the case: A person sold his slave. Then the buyer took possession and sold him to someone else. Then the second buyer returned the slave to the first buyer due to a defect, and the first buyer accepted him by the judge's order. The judge's decision can be based on three grounds. First: The second buyer claimed that the first buyer had admitted the defect in the sale, but the first buyer denied making that admission. So the second buyer, as claimant, brought witnesses to prove the first buyer's admission. With their testimony, the first buyer's admission of the defect is proven, and based on that admission, the judge orders the return of the slave to the first buyer. The author's word 'by admission' refers to this. Second: The second buyer claims that the slave had a defect at the time of purchase from the first buyer, and the first buyer denies it outright. So the second buyer brings witnesses before the judge to prove the defect. The judge, due to that defect, orders the return of the slave to the first buyer. The author's word 'by evidence' refers to this. Third: In the mentioned case, the second buyer is unable to bring witnesses to prove the defect. The judge, according to Islamic law, demands an oath from the first buyer, and he refuses to swear. So, due to his refusal, the defect is considered proven, and the judge orders the return of the slave to the first buyer. The author's word 'or refusal of oath' refers to this. In any case, whichever of these three ways the slave is returned to the first buyer, the first buyer has the right to return that slave to his own seller – meaning the one from whom he purchased.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling from the matn of Al-Hidayah regarding a situation where a slave is sold, then resold to a second buyer, and then the second buyer returns the slave to the first buyer due to a defect. The key question is whether the first buyer can then return the slave to the original seller. The text explains that if the return from the second buyer is accepted by the first buyer by virtue of a judge's judgment (through admission, evidence, or refusal of oath), then the first buyer can indeed return the slave to his own seller. The reason is that the return by the second buyer effectively rescinds the second sale from its inception (faskh min al-asl), rendering it as if it never happened. This then leaves the first buyer in the same position as if he still owned the slave, and he can exercise his right of return against the original seller if the defect existed at that initial sale. The text contrasts this with a case involving an agent for sale (wakil bil-bay'): if an agent sells an item and the buyer returns it to the agent due to a defect, the return is made to the principal, not the agent, because there is only one sale (agent sells on behalf of principal). Here, there are two separate sales, so the rescission of the second does not automatically rescind the first; but once the second is rescinded by the judge, the first buyer's ownership is restored retroactively, enabling him to act as owner returning the item. The Urdu commentary elaborates on three possible ways the judge's decision can be made: (1) the first buyer's prior admission of the defect is proven by witnesses; (2) the defect itself is proven by witnesses; (3) the first buyer refuses to take an oath, leading to the defect being considered proven. In all three, the first buyer can return the slave to his seller.

Key Terms
فسخ من الأصل (faskh min al-aṣl): A rescission from the beginning (ex tunc), rendering the contract as if it never existed.
قضاء بالإقرار (qaḍā' bi-l-iqrār): A judicial decision based on an admission by the first buyer that the defect existed at the time of his sale to the second buyer.
باباء يمين (bā'ā' yamīn): Refusal to take an oath; in this context, the first buyer's refusal to swear that there was no defect leads to the defect being considered proven.
وکیل بالبيع (wakīl bil-bayʿ): An agent authorized to sell on behalf of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can the first buyer return the slave to the original seller after the second buyer returns it due to a defect?
A) If the second buyer returns the slave voluntarily.
B) If the first buyer admits the defect.
C) If the return was based on a judge's judgment (admission, evidence, or refusal of oath).
D) The first buyer can never return, as he sold the slave.

Key Takeaway
A judge's retroactive rescission of a subsequent sale restores the intermediate buyer's ownership, enabling him to return the item to his own seller.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Return of Defective Slave After Multiple Sales
Ruling on first buyer's right after court-ordered return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning a defective item after multiple sales, explain the retroactive annulment effect of a court-ordered return, and distinguish this from the case of an agent's sale.

Core Idea
Court-ordered return annuls the second sale retroactively, reviving the first buyer's right to return to the original seller.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in sales (bay'): a slave is sold from S to A, then from A to B. B returns the slave to A due to a defect, and the return is ordered by a judge. Can A now return the slave to S? The Hanafi school says yes, because the court-ordered return retroactively cancels the second sale (faskh min al-asl), as if it never existed. This preserves the first sale and A's option of defect. The lesson also contrasts this with voluntary returns and agency sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
There are two sales: S→A (first buyer) and A→B (second buyer). B returns the defective slave to A, but only by court order (qada) based on confession, evidence, or refusal of oath. Understand the parties and the three possible bases for the judge's decision.
The three bases are explained in the commentary: confession of defect by A, evidence of defect, or A's refusal to take an oath.
2
Understand the legal effect: faskh min al-asl
The court-ordered return is an annulment from the origin (faskh min al-asl). This means the second sale is treated as if it never happened. Therefore, the first sale remains intact, and A still owns the slave as if he had never sold it to B.
This retroactive effect is unique to court-ordered returns. Without a court order, the return is considered a new sale (bay' jadid) regarding third parties.
3
Resolve the denial contradiction
If A initially denied the defect, how can he later return the slave to S for the same defect? The answer: the judge's judgment overrides A's denial (mukadhdhab shar'an). A's denial becomes ineffective, and the defect is proven by the court.
The 'judgment by confession' means A denied confessing, but witnesses proved he confessed. This is evidence, not a new confession.
4
Compare with the agent case
If an agent (wakil) sells on behalf of the principal, and the buyer returns the item to the agent due to a defect, the return is automatically to the principal. In the multi-sale case, there are two distinct sales, so B's return to A does not automatically go to S. A must separately return to S.
The key difference: in agency, one sale exists; in the chain, two sales exist. Annulling the second does not annul the first.
5
Learn the opposite: no court order
If B returns the slave to A without a court order (e.g., by mutual agreement), A cannot return it to S. Without qada, the return is considered a new sale with respect to third parties like S. A's option of defect against S is not revived.
This rule is from al-Jami' al-Saghir, a Zahir al-Riwayah source.

Source Text

کرتا ہے کہ غلام جو خریدا گیا ہے اس میں مشتری اول کے پاس عیب تھا اور مشتری اول سرے سے عیب کا انکار کرتا ہے پس مشتری ثانی نے قاضی کی عدالت میں ثبوت عیب پر گواہ پیش کر دئیے قاضی نے اس عیب کی وجہ سے مشتری اول کی طرف غلام واپس کرنے کا حکم دیا۔ مصنف کے قول وبینہ سے یہی مراد ہے تیسری بنیاد یہ ہے کہ مذکورہ صورت میں مشتری ثانی ثبوت عیب پر گواہ پیش کرنے سے عاجز آ گیا۔ قاضی نے شریعت کے مطابق مشتری اول سے قسم کا مطالبہ کیا مشتری اول نے قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا ۔ پس مشتری اول کے قسم سے انکار کر دینے کی وجہ سے عیب کو ثابت مان کر غلام مشتری اول کی طرف واپس کرنے کا حکم دے دیا۔ مصنف کے قول او باباء یمین سے یہی مراد ہے۔ بہر حال ان تینوں صورتوں میں سے جس صورت میں بھی مشتری اول کی طرف غلام واپس کیا گیا ہو۔ مشتری اول کو یہ حق ہے کہ وہ اس غلام کو اپنے بائع کی طرف واپس کر دے یعنی جس سے مشتری اول نے خریدا ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مشتری ثانی کا بحکم قاضی مشتری اول کی طرف غلام واپس کرنا یہ بیع ثانی کا فسخ کرنا ہے پس بیع ثانی گویا واقع ہی نہیں ہوئی ہے اور بیع اول موجود ہے تو مشتری اول کو عیب کی وجہ سے غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ غاية الامر الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ جب مشتری اول نے عیب کا انکار کر دیا تو اس کو اپنے بائع کی طرف اس عیب کی وجہ سے مبیع واپس کرنے کا اختیار کس طرح ہوگا جس کا وہ انکار کر چکا ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں مشتری اول کے کلام میں تناقض واقع ہو گیا ہے۔ جواب ۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ مشتری اول نے عیب کا انکار تو یقیناً کیا تھا لیکن شریعت نے بحکم قاضی اس کو جھٹلایا ہے پس تکذیب شرع کی وجہ سے اس کا انکار کالعدم ہو گیا ہے اور جب انکار کا لعدم ہو گیا تو مشتری اول کے کلام میں کوئی تناقص باقی نہ رہا اور جب مشتری اول کے کلام میں تناقص باقی نہیں رہا تو ثابت شدہ عیب کی وجہ سے مشتری اول کو بھی طبیع اپنے بائع کی طرف واپس کرنے کا پورا پورا اختیار ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ قضا بالا قرار کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ مشتری اول نے اقرار عیب کا انکار کر دیا اور پھر وہ اقرار گواہی کے ذریعہ ثابت کیا گیا۔ یہ مطلب ہرگز نہیں ہے کہ مشتری اول نے عیب کا اقرار کر لیا ہے اس کی پوری تفصیل پہلی بنیاد کے تحت خادم ذکر کر چکا ہے۔ وهذا بخلاف الوكيل بالبیع سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اگر وکیل بالبیع نے کوئی چیز فروخت کی پھر مشتری نے گواہوں کے ذریعہ مبیع کے اندر عیب ثابت کر کے مبیع وکیل کی طرف واپس کر دی تو یہ وکیل کی طرف واپس کرنا مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنا ہے۔ مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنے کے لیے وکیل کو علیحدہ سے کوئی اقدام کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ اس کے بر خلاف متن کے مسئلہ میں مشتری ثانی مشتری اول کی طرف مبیع واپس کرنا مشتری اول کے بائع کی طرف واپس کرنا نہیں ہے بلکہ مشتری اول اگر چاہے گا تو مبیع معیب اپنے بائع کی طرف واپس کرے گا ورنہ نہیں۔ یعنی مشتری ثانی کے واپس کرنے سے مشتری اول کے بائع کی طرف واپس کرنا لازم نہیں آئے گا۔ حالانکہ وکیل اور مشتری اول دونوں کا حال برابر ہے۔ پس جس طرح وکیل کی طرف مبیع واپس کرنے سے مؤکل کی طرف واپس ہو جاتی ہے اسی طرح مشتری اول کی طرف واپس کرنے سے اس کے بائع کی طرف واپس ہو جانی چاہیے تھی۔ جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ مسئلہ تو کیل میں بیع فقط ایک ہے اس لئے وکیل کی طرف مبیع واپس کرنا در حقیقت مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنا ہے۔ اور متن کے مسئلہ میں بیع دو ہیں ایک بائع اول اور مشتری اول کے درمیان اور دوم مشتری اول اور مشتری ثانی کے درمیان اور چونکہ دوسری بیع کے فسخ ہونے سے پہلی بیع فسخ نہیں ہوتی۔ اسی لیے مشتری اول کی طرف مبیع واپس کرنے سے اس کے بائع کی طرف ہونے سے پہلی بیع بھی واپس نہ ہوگی۔ بغیر قضاء قاضی کے رد کیا تو بائع اول پر رد نہیں ہوسکتا وان قبل بغير قضاء القاضى ليس له ان يرده لانه بيع جديد في حق ثالث وان كان فسخا في حقهما والاول ثالثهما وفى الجامع الصغير وان رد عليه باقراره بغير قضاء بعيب لا يحدث مثله لم يكن له ان يخاصم الذى
(The author) said: And whoever sold a slave, then the buyer sold him, then the slave was returned to him due to a defect — if he accepted it by the judgment of the judge, either by confession, or by evidence, or by refusal to take an oath, then he may return him to his seller, because it is an annulment from the origin, so the sale is treated as if it never occurred. The utmost is that he denied the existence of the defect, but he became legally contradicted by the judgment. The meaning of judgment by confession is that he denied the confession, so it was established by evidence. This is contrary to the case of an agent for sale when something is returned to him due to a defect by evidence, for then the return is upon the principal, because there is one sale there, while here there are two sales; by the annulment of the second, the first is not annulled. Translation: He said: A person sold a slave, then the buyer sold him, then he was returned from the side of the buyer due to a defect. Then if the first buyer accepted him by the order of the judge (whether) by confession, or by evidence, or by refusal to take an oath, the first buyer can return him to his seller, because this is an annulment of the original sale, so the second sale is regarded as if it never occurred. At most it can be said that he denied the existence of the defect, but the sharia contradicted him by order of judgment. The meaning of 'judgment by confession' is that the first buyer denied making a confession of the defect, so it was established through evidence. And this is contrary to the case when an agent for sale has a thing returned to him through evidence of a defect, then that return will be upon the principal, because in the agent's case there is one sale, and here there are two sales; when the second sale is annulled, the first sale is not annulled. Explanation: The scenario is that a person sold his slave. Then the buyer took possession and sold him to someone else. Then the second buyer returned him to the first buyer due to a defect present in the slave, but the first buyer accepted him by order of the judge. The judge's decision can be based on three foundations. First: The second buyer claimed that the first buyer had confessed to the defect in the sale, and the first buyer denied having confessed. Then the second buyer, as claimant, brought witnesses to the first buyer's confession of the defect; by their testimony, the first buyer's confession of the defect is proven. When the first buyer's confession of the defect's existence is proven, the judge orders the return of the slave to the first buyer based on that confession. This is the meaning of the author's word 'by confession'. Second: The second buyer claims that the slave purchased had a defect while in the possession of the first buyer, and the first buyer denies the defect entirely. Then the second buyer brings witnesses to the existence of the defect in the judge's court; the judge orders return of the slave to the first buyer due to this defect. This is the meaning of the author's word 'by evidence'. Third: In the mentioned scenario, the second buyer is unable to produce witnesses for proof of the defect. The judge, according to sharia, demands an oath from the first buyer; the first buyer refuses to take the oath. Then, due to the first buyer's refusal to take the oath, the defect is considered proven and the judge orders the return of the slave to the first buyer. This is the meaning of the author's word 'or by refusal to take an oath'. In any of these three scenarios where the slave is returned to the first buyer, the first buyer has the right to return this slave to his own seller, i.e., the one from whom he purchased it. The proof is that the second buyer's return of the slave to the first buyer by order of the judge is annulment of the second sale; so the second sale is as if it never occurred, and the first sale remains. Therefore the first buyer will have the option to return the slave due to the defect. From 'The utmost is...' is an answer to a question. The question is: When the first buyer denied the defect, how can he have the option to return the sold item to his seller due to that same defect which he denied? Because in that case there is a contradiction in the first buyer's statement. Answer: His answer is that the first buyer indeed denied the defect, but the sharia contradicted him by order of the judge; so due to the sharia's contradiction, his denial becomes void. When the denial becomes void, no contradiction remains in the first buyer's statement. When no contradiction remains, the first buyer has full right to return the defective sold item to his seller. The author of al-Hidayah says: The meaning of 'judgment by confession' is that the first buyer denied making a confession of the defect, and then that confession was established through evidence. It does not mean that the first buyer actually confessed to the defect; the full detail under the first basis has already been mentioned by the servant (commentator). 'And this is contrary to the case of an agent for sale' is an answer to a question. The question is: If an agent for sale sold something, then the buyer proved a defect in the sold item through witnesses and returned it to the agent, then this return to the agent is a return to the principal. The agent does not need to take any separate action to return it to the principal. In contrast, in the text's issue, the second buyer's return of the sold item to the first buyer is not a return to the first buyer's seller; rather, the first buyer, if he wishes, will return the defective item to his seller, otherwise not. That is, the return by the second buyer does not necessitate return to the first buyer's seller, even though the situation of the agent and the first buyer are similar. So just as returning the sold item to the agent results in return to the principal, similarly returning to the first buyer should have resulted in return to his seller. Answer: The answer is that in the agent's case, there is only one sale, so returning the sold item to the agent is in reality a return to the principal. In the text's issue, there are two sales: one between the first seller and the first buyer, and the second between the first buyer and the second buyer. Since the annulment of the second sale does not annul the first sale, therefore returning the sold item to the first buyer does not effect a return to his seller. If the return was without the judge's judgment: (then) he cannot return it to the first seller. And if he accepts it without the judge's judgment, he does not have the right to return it, because it is a new sale with respect to a third party, even if it is an annulment between the two of them; and the first seller is a third party with respect to them. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If he returns it to him by his confession without a judgment, due to a defect that does not normally occur anew, then he does not have the right to litigate against the one who...
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the ruling on returning a defective slave after multiple sales, drawing from the matn of al-Hidayah. The passage type is a ruling with proof, expanded through commentary. The core ruling: if the first buyer (A) buys a slave from the original seller (S), then sells it to a second buyer (B), and B returns the slave to A due to a defect by a court order (qada), then A may return the slave to S. This is because B's return, when ordered by a judge, constitutes an annulment (faskh) of the second sale from its origin, as if it never existed. Thus the first sale revives, and A retains the right to return the defective item to S. The commentator then addresses a potential contradiction: A had initially denied the defect when B claimed it, yet later wishes to return it to S for the same defect. The answer is that the court's judgment legally overrides A's denial; A is considered refuted (mukadhdhab) by sharia, so his denial becomes ineffective. The passage also compares this scenario to the case of an agent (wakil) who sells on behalf of a principal. If the buyer returns the item to the agent due to a defect (proven by witnesses), the return automatically goes to the principal, not the agent. Why the difference? In the agent case, there is one sale between the principal and buyer; the agent is merely a representative. In the multi-sale case, there are two distinct sales: S→A and A→B. Annulling the second sale does not automatically annul the first; A remains the buyer vis-à-vis S. Therefore, a separate act of return by A is needed. Finally, the passage notes the opposite scenario: if B returns the slave to A without a court order (e.g., by mutual agreement), then A cannot return it to S. This is because without qada, the return is considered a new sale (bay' jadid) with respect to third parties like S. The original seller would not be bound by B's acceptance of return; A retains ownership until he voluntarily exercises his option. The text cites al-Jami' al-Saghir (a Zahir al-Riwayah source) for the rule that if B returns the slave by his own confession of a non-novel defect without a judge, A cannot sue S.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or rescission of a contract, retroactively nullifying it from inception.
قضاء القاضي (qada' al-qadi): The judgment or decree of a judge, which has binding legal effect and can override the parties' prior denials.
الوكيل بالبيع (al-wakil bil-bay'): An agent appointed to sell a specific item on behalf of the owner (principal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the first buyer return the defective slave to the original seller after a court-ordered return from the second buyer, but not when the return is without court order?
A) Because the court order cancels the second sale retroactively, reviving the first sale, while without court order it is considered a new sale.
B) Because the first buyer confesses to the defect only when court orders it.
C) Because the original seller is bound only by court orders.
D) Because the second buyer's return without court order is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The first buyer can return a defective item to the original seller only if the second buyer's return was by court order, which retroactively annuls the second sale and revives the first buyer's option of defect.
